1401 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Halpern


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Long Beach


79 Misc. 2d 790; 361 N.Y.S.2d 578; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1750



November 20, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant individuals, represented by the same attorney, filed an omnibus motion to dismiss the respective informations against them that alleged violations of the City of Long Beach, N.Y., Municipal Code ch. 10, art. 8, § 10-814.3(3), promulgated by Ordinance 1038/71 as amended by Ordinance 1132/72, and City of Long Beach, N.Y. Municipal Code ch. 10, art. 8, § 10-810.1, promulgated by Ordinance 1140/72.


OVERVIEW: The charges in each case were similar in nature alleging that the respective individuals, as owners of a rooming house, failed to provide an automatic sprinkler system throughout the subject premises consonant with the requirements of law. The individuals argued that the local city ordinances they are charged under violated N.Y. Exec. Law § 386, which precluded a municipality that had adopted the New York State Building Construction Code, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, §§ 600 to 1,300, from enacting an ordinance with provisions more restrictive than contained in the Code. The court found that the applicable statutory requirements for automatic sprinklers pertaining to the subject property at the time of the adoption of the Code in 1957, was contained in N.Y. Mult. Resid. Law § 52. That section was not rendered inoperative by adoption of the Code because the latter did not, at the time of its adoption, govern the subject property pursuant to N.Y. Mult. Resid. Law § 329(2). The court also held that the ordinances did not violate N.Y. Const. art. III, § 16 because § 16 was not applicable to local government.


OUTCOME: The court denied the individuals' motion to dismiss, found them guilty as charged in each of the two informations, and directed their respective appearance at court for sentencing.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- fire prevention requirements -- defendants, owners of rooming house, are guilty of failing to provide automatic sprinkler system in violation of city ordinances; city was not precluded under section 386 of Executive Law from having local ordinance more restrictive than provisions contained in New York State Building Construction Code ( 9 NYCRR Parts 600-1300) -- local ordinances did not violate constitutional prohibition against incorporation by reference.

1. Defendants, as owners of a rooming house, are charged with failing to provide an automatic sprinkler system throughout the subject premises in violation of city ordinances contained in the Municipal Code of the City of Long Beach. Although a municipality that has adopted the New York State Building Construction Code (Code) ( 9 NYCRR Parts 600-1300) is precluded from enacting an ordinance with provisions more restrictive than those contained in the Code (Executive Law, § 386), the ordinance is nonetheless valid since the subject property is excluded from coverage under the Code (9 NYCRR 701.2 [a]). The applicable statutory requirements for automatic sprinklers pertaining to the property are [***2] instead governed by section 52 of the Multiple Residence Law, which law specifically authorizes more restrictive local ordinances (Multiple Residence Law, § 329, subd. 1).

2. In addition, by attempting to incorporate by reference certain compliance standards prepared by a private association, the local ordinances did not violate section 16 of article III of the New York State Constitution since article III only applies to the State Legislature and not to local government. Local government is controlled by article IX of the State Constitution which does not contain restrictions against incorporation by reference in local ordinances.

3. Accordingly, defendants' motion to dismiss the informations is denied and defendants are found guilty as charged.

 

1402 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Bright v. Warden, New York City Correctional Institution for Men


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Bronx County


79 Misc. 2d 959; 361 N.Y.S.2d 809; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1800



November 18, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Alleging that they had been denied due process because of their loss of good behavior time, relator inmates brought four separate habeas corpus actions against respondent wardens.


OVERVIEW: The first inmate was charged with inciting to riot, attempted escape, and noncriminal infractions of various prison rules. The second was charged with various infractions, the third was charged with assault, and the fourth was charged with inciting to riot. The court rejected the inmates' argument that they did not know in advance what conduct was prohibited. It stated that all citizens, including inmates, were presumed to know the law; thus, inmates could not escape punishment for the commission of a crime by arguing that they had not been given notice that the commission of a crime violated a prison's rules and regulations. The court held, however, that inmates were entitled to notice of infractions that did not constitute crimes. Applying these principles, the court held that adequate notice was given to the first inmate regarding the noncriminal infractions and that he and the third and fourth inmates were not entitled to advance notice that their criminal infractions were violations of prison rules. As for the second inmate, the court found that his petition was premature in that it was brought too far in advance of his tentative release date.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the writs of the four inmates. It granted the second inmate leave to renew his writ within a reasonable time of his tentative release date.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Prisons and prisoners -- good time allowance -- good time allowance is not granted to inmate prior to commencement of his sentence by reason of procedural practice of entering on inmate's card upon his admission to institution "Tentative Expiration Date"; sections 803 and 804 of Correction Law and section 70.30 of Penal Law dictate that good time allowance must be earned; inmates do not have right to demand or require allowances authorized by sections 803 and 804; Department of Correctional Services has sole discretion of either granting, denying, or withholding good time allowance in accordance with law -- inmates, even in absence of all prison rules and regulations are bound to obey provisions of Penal Law and are presumed to have knowledge and notice that such acts also constitute infractions of prison discipline -- there is no constitutional right of confrontation and cross-examination nor is there right to counsel at disciplinary hearings -- when inmate is deprived of good time allowance after hearing conducted long in advance of tentative release date any writ brought too soon before that date is premature and should be summarily denied with leave to renew at appropriate  [***2] date.

1. Relators allege that they have been denied due process of law in their loss of "good behavior time", which was taken away from them by the warden following disciplinary hearings which found them guilty of violating the rules of the institution. A good time allowance is not granted to an inmate prior to the commencement of his sentence by reason of a procedural practice of the Department of Correctional Services which consists of entering on an inmate's card upon his admission to the institution a "Tentative Expiration Date" which is fixed by subtracting the maximum good time allowance from the maximum sentence. Sections 803 and 804 of the Correction Law and section 70.30 of the Penal Law dictate that good time allowance must be earned after an inmate has demonstrated satisfactory performance or good behavior. Inmates do not have the right to demand or require the allowances authorized by sections 803 and 804. The Department of Correctional Services has the sole discretion of either granting, denying or withholding good time allowance when said discretion is exercised in accordance with the law. In the event the department arbitrarily or capriciously denies or withholds [***3] earned good time allowance beyond a reasonable time prior to the tentative expiration date inmates may apply to the courts for a review and determination of their claims for earned good time allowance.

2. Although an institution must promulgate rules and regulations so as to give inmates advance notice as to what acts are considered by the institution to be infractions, where the infraction is a crime in violation of the Penal Law, no such advance notice is required since all inmates, even in the absence of all prison rules and regulations, are bound to obey the provisions of the Penal Law, and are presumed to have knowledge and notice that such acts also constitute infractions of prison discipline.

3. While inmates are afforded some basic constitutional rights at disciplinary hearings, there is no constitutional right of confrontation and cross-examination, nor is there a right to counsel (see Wolff v. McDonnell, 418 U.S. 539).

4. When an inmate is deprived of good time allowance after a hearing conducted long in advance of the tentative release date entered on his record, he has not sustained any loss at that time since no extension to his minimal sentence has occurred.  [***4] The execution of the board's decision cannot take effect immediately but must be held in abeyance until the tentative release date. Any writ or article 78 proceeding brought too soon before that date is premature and should be summarily denied with leave to renew at an appropriate date within a reasonable time prior to the tentative release date.

5. Based upon various grounds discussed above, the writs are denied.

 

1403 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wixson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Westchester County


79 Misc. 2d 557; 360 N.Y.S.2d 818; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1705



November 12, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the fifth and sixth branches of defendant's omnibus motion are denied in their entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant brought an omnibus motion seeking relief from an indictment for criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 220.4(3) criminal possession of a controlled substance in the second degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 220.18(3), and criminal possession of a controlled substance in the sixth degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 220.06(1).


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant had prior felony convictions in New York and Connecticut. He was indicted an indictment for criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 220.4(3), criminal possession of a controlled substance in the second degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 220.18(3), and criminal possession of a controlled substance in the sixth degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 220.06(1). Defendant brought an omnibus motion seeking relief from the indictment. Each of the offenses under indictment was committed the same day, and the substance involved in each instance was amphetamines. The court denied defendant's omnibus motion because the legislation represented a proper exercise of the legislature's right to reasonably restrict the right of any class of defendants to plead to reduced criminal charges. The court explained that the mere mention of foreign convictions and imprisonments in the penal law did not contravene N.Y. Const. Art. III § 16 by making the laws of other jurisdictions a part of state law, but merely declared New York's policy to provide enhanced punishment upon proof of such foreign predicates for offenders later convicted in the state.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's omnibus motion because the legislation represented a proper exercise of the legislature's right to reasonably restrict the right of any class of defendants to plead to reduced criminal charges.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- second felony drug offenders -- prior felony conviction in another State validly and constitutionally counts as prior felony conviction under New York laws -- mandatory sentences for drug offenders, constitutional -- lesser pleas validly and constitutionally limited.

In Connecticut in 1969 defendant was convicted of burglary and sentenced to three to five years; and in New York in 1971 he was again convicted of burglary and was sentenced to four years. Now in New York in 1974, for an alleged criminal possession and sale on May 14, 1974 of a controlled substance (amphetamine, a stimulant drug), he has been indicted under article 220 of the Penal Law for two class A-II felonies and one class D felony. On the class A-II felony counts, he cannot plead guilty to less than a class A felony the maximum term for which is life imprisonment; and on the class D felony count he cannot plead guilty to anything less than a felony ( CPL 220.10, subd. 6, pars. [a], [b]); and, on any of the three counts, if he is convicted of any felony less than class A, he must be sentenced [***2] as a prior felony offender (Penal Law, § 70.06, subd. 1, par. [b], cl. [i]). These statutes validly and constitutionally count the Connecticut felony conviction as a prior felony conviction (cf. N. Y. Const., art. III, §§ 1, 16; also, CPL 400.21, subd. 7, par. [b]). These statutes do not contravene State and Federal constitutional principles pertaining to equal protection, due process, cruel punishment, ex post facto, or double jeopardy. Defendant's motion, attacking these statutes as unconstitutional, is denied.

 

1404 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Warren v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Wyoming County


79 Misc. 2d 643; 361 N.Y.S.2d 125; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1726



November 12, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus in his suit against respondent Correctional Facility Superintendent challenging his sentence as a second-felony offender under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimed his sentence as a second-felony offender violated his due process rights because he did not have an opportunity to test the validity of his prior felony. The court granted habeas relief because petitioner was denied due process when the court failed to tell him that he could contest the constitutionality of a prior felony and to warn him that failure to do so waived the right. Petitioner ended up with a mandatory increased prison sentence and lost his constitutional right to test the matter without as much as a warning from the court.


OUTCOME: The court declared petitioner's sentence invalid and ordered him returned to the lower court for resentencing.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- sentence -- second felony offender -- Constitution requires that on any second felony sentencing under section 70.06 of Penal Law and CPL 400.21, court must inform defendant of his rights to contest constitutionality of any prior felony and that failure to so contest it waives his right to do so; since no warning was given, relator's sentence is invalid, and he is ordered to be returned for resentencing.

Relator's sentence as a second felony offender pursuant to section 70.06 of the Penal Law was invalid. During the course of an arraignment on a predicate felony information, relator admitted that he was convicted of a prior felony. He was then sentenced as a second felony offender without being warned by the court, that he has a right under CPL 400.21 to contest the constitutionality of his prior felony conviction or that the failure to so contest waives his right to do so. Although no statute requires this, the Constitution requires that on any second felony sentencing under section 70.06 of the Penal Law and CPL 400.21, the court must inform the defendant of his right to contest the constitutionality of any prior felony and that failure to so contest it waives [***2] his right to do so. Since no such warning was given here, relator is ordered to be returned for resentencing.

 

1405 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rogers v. Williamsburgh Sav. Bank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Suffolk County


79 Misc. 2d 852; 361 N.Y.S.2d 531; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1770



November 7, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons stated above, defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint is in all respects denied.

Motion costs to plaintiffs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant bank filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff homeowners' complaint on grounds that a defense was based upon documentary evidence, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a)(1), and that the complaint failed to state a cause of action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a)(7). The complaint was based on N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 254-a, for the bank's refusal to consent to either a transfer subject to the mortgage or waiver of the prepayment fee.


OVERVIEW: The homeowners sought a refund of the prepayment fee and attorney's fees. The bank contended that N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 254-a did not apply to it because the statute became effective after the mortgage. The bank contended that to allow the statute to preclude the charge of the prepayment fee would have given the statute retrospective effect, thereby impairing a vested contractual right, and rendering it unconstitutional. The court held that § 254-a did indeed apply to the facts of the case, and also was constitutional as applied, because it was a valid exercise of the state's power to promote the general welfare of its citizens. Two other defenses had no merit and the motion to dismiss was denied.


OUTCOME: The bank's motion to dismiss the homeowners' complaint, which sought a refund of the prepayment fee and attorney's fees, was in all respects denied.


HEADNOTES

Mortgages -- prepayment fee -- Real Property Law ( § 254-a) prohibits mortgage prepayment fee when mortgagor sells premises; statute validly and constitutionally applies to already existing mortgages.

 Section 254-a of the Real Property Law provides: "If a bond or note, or the mortgage on real property, improved by a one to six family residence occupied by the owner, securing the payment of same, contains (1) a provision whereby the mortgagee retains the right to accelerate the due date for payment of the balance of principal upon a transfer or sale of such real property or by alienation of title of such real property due to an act or operation of law, and (2) a provision for payment of any charge, however denominated, in the nature of a prepayment fee and if a mortgagor sells or transfers his property or if title to the mortgaged property is transfers by act or operation of law and the purchaser requests permission to assume the mortgage or take the mortgaged premises subject to the mortgage, but the mortgagee does not consent to such request [***2] and thereby necessitates prepayment of the mortgage, the mortgagee shall not levy a prepayment fee; provided, however, that the provisions of this section shall not apply to the extent such provisions are inconsistent with any federal law or regulation." The statute validly and constitutionally applies to already existing mortgages.

 

1406 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bullock


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


80 Misc. 2d 73; 362 N.Y.S.2d 682; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1844



November 7, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in a jury trial of first-degree criminal sale of a controlled substance and other charges. Arguing that the sentencing provisions for the sale conviction violated the United States Constitution, he filed a motion to set aside the sale conviction.


OVERVIEW: Under N.Y. Penal Law §§ 60.05 and 70.00, first-degree criminal sale of a controlled substance was a class A-I felony that required a mandatory indeterminate sentence with a maximum of life. The court rejected defendant's argument that the punishment violated the Cruel and Unusual Punishment Clause. It stated that because the imposition of the maximum sentence was mandatory, it could not be arbitrarily applied; that the severity of the life maximum was offset by a defendant's eligibility for parole following service of the minimum sentence; that defendant had not shown that a less severe punishment would provide a sufficient deterrent to drug trafficking; and that the present sentencing structure, rather than being rejected by contemporary society, had been prompted by society's demand that strong action be taken against the drug problem. The court then rejected defendant's argument that the statute violated his equal protection rights because it limited plea bargaining. It stated that there was no constitutional right to plea bargaining and that the court could not substitute its judgment for that of the legislature, which had chosen to limit plea negotiations.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- drug law -- defendant who is charged with class A-I felony cannot plea bargain below class A-III felony, and, when convicted of class A-I felony, mandatory sentence is at least 15 years to life, and any parole after serving minimum must be on lifetime conditions; these statutes are constitutional.

A defendant who is charged with criminal sale of a controlled substance in the first degree (a class A-I felony) cannot plea bargain below a class A-III felony, nor may he have the class A felony charge satisfied by a plea to a separate indictment of a lesser charge; and, when he is duly convicted of the class A-I felony, the mandatory sentence is an indeterminate one with a minimum of between 15 and 25 years and a maximum of life; and when thus imprisoned, he may be paroled after serving his minimum sentence but the conditions of the parole are for his lifetime (Penal Law, § 60.05, subd. 1; § 70.00, subd. 2, par, [a]; § 70.00, subd. 3, par. [a], cl [i]; § 70.40, subd. 1; CPL 220.10, subd. 6, par. [a]; CPL 220.30, subd. 3, par. [b], cl. [i]; Correction Law, § 212, subd. 8; Correction Law,  [***2] § 213; 7 NYCRR 1.10). These provisions do not deny the defendant the equal protection of the laws, nor do they subject him to cruel or unusually severe punishment. His motion to set aside his conviction after trial by jury is denied.

 

1407 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sutton v. Garcia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


80 Misc. 2d 690; 363 N.Y.S.2d 695; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1921



November 7, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's motion to dismiss and to vacate the attachment on the ground of forum non conveniens is granted subject to the condition, to be incorporated in the order to be settled hereon, that defendant stipulates to accept service of process in and submit to the jurisdiction of the appropriate court in Colombia, South America, for a resolution on the merits of the dispute between the parties and to waive any applicable Statute of Limitations.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant payor filed a motion before the court to dismiss the action and to vacate an order of attachment in plaintiff payee's favor on the ground of forum non conveniens.


OVERVIEW: One question before the court was whether it had jurisdiction over the claim. The payee contended that the court could not decline jurisdiction over its claim, even in the exercise of discretion, because the causes of action arose in New York in that the checks underlying the dispute were drawn on a New York bank where they were also stopped. However, the checks were executed, delivered, and ultimately indorsed in Colombia, where the stop payment orders were apparently issued to the New York bank. The court found that all of the pertinent transactions relative to liability on the checks took place in Colombia, among residents and citizens of Colombia, including the underlying transaction which led to issuance of the checks. Therefore, the court did not have jurisdiction over claims arising from that transaction.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss the claim on the grounds of forum non conveniens was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- forum non conveniens -- proper forum for action between Colombia residents on Colombia checks is Colombia, even though checks were drawn on New York bank; warrant of attachment on New York bank will be vacated on condition that defendant accepts service in Colombia.

Three checks, made by a resident of Colombia (South America), and payable to a Colombia resident, and indorsed by said Colombia payee to two Colombia residents, were drawn on a New York bank, and were stopped by the maker. All these acts occurred in Colombia. Apparently for purposes of harassment and collection, the Colombia indorsees brought this action on the checks in the New York Supreme Court and there obtained a warrant of attachment [***2] upon the maker's funds in the New York bank. The proper place for this Colombia cause of action by Colombia residents against Colombia residents is Colombia ( CPLR 327). The New York Supreme Court declines to take jurisdiction. The court will vacate the attachment, on conditions that the defendant stipulates to accept service of process in an appropriate court in Colombia and waives any applicable Statute of Limitations.

 

1408 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York Times Co. v. New York, Com. on Human Rights


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


79 Misc. 2d 1046; 362 N.Y.S.2d 321; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2025; 9 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P10,031



October 28, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner newspaper sought a review, pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B1-9.0, of an order of respondent New York Commission on Human Rights (commission), which held that all advertisements in the newspaper for employment in the Republic of South Africa had aided and abetted discriminatory acts. The order enjoined the newspaper from printing any advertisement seeking employees or employment located in the Republic of South Africa.


OVERVIEW: The newspaper was charged with printing advertisements that directly or indirectly expressed limitations, specifications, or discriminations as to race, color, or national origin in violation of § B1-7.0(1)(d). The advertisements cited were for teaching or managerial positions, and they contained no reference to race, color, or creed. The commission held that specification of South Africa as the location of the employment constituted an "expression" of discrimination because the words "South Africa" were understood to be synonymous with white supremacy. The court vacated the order of the commission, which held that the advertisements had aided and abetted discriminatory acts, and which enjoined the newspaper from printing any advertisement seeking employees or employment located in the Republic of South Africa. A perception by New York residents that South Africa discriminated in its hiring practices was not a proper basis for an injunction. By its determination, the commission, in effect, was questioning the employment methods and practices of a foreign government.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the commission, which held that all advertisements in the newspaper for employment in the Republic of South Africa had aided and abetted discriminatory acts. The order enjoined the newspaper from printing any advertisement seeking employees or employment located in the Republic of South Africa.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil rights -- discrimination in advertisements -- Commission on Human Rights of City of New York erroneously enjoined New York Times from printing advertisements for employment in South Africa, since advertisements contained no reference to race, showed no intent to discriminate and since injunction invades policies of foreign government in excess of Commission's jurisdiction -- advertisements are commercial and not protected by First and Fourteenth Amendments.

1. The Commission on Human Rights of the City of New York erroneously enjoined the New York Times from printing advertisements seeking employees or employment in the Republic of South Africa, pursuant to subdivision 6 of section B1-7.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which makes illegal the "aiding" of any of the practices proscribed by that section. The advertisements contained no reference to race, color or creed and evince no intent to discriminate or to aid discrimination. By its determination the Commission, in effect, was questioning the employment methods and practices of a foreign government, an invasion on a local level of the policies of a foreign nation, which constitutes an unjustifiable [***2] extension of the Commission's powers beyond its jurisdiction.

2. Since the advertisements were within the category of commercial advertising, they are not within the purview of the protections of the First and Fourteenth Amendments.

 

1409 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Richard's Service Station, Inc. v. Huntington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


79 Misc. 2d 834; 361 N.Y.S.2d 497; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1766



October 26, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tow truck operators filed a motion for summary judgment in their action to permanently enjoin defendants, town and police department, from enforcing a local law. The tow truck operators argued that the local law was vague and uncertain, arbitrary and capricious, created a monopoly to one class of tow truck operators over another, and that it unlawfully restrained their right to engage in business.


OVERVIEW: The town passed a law to standardize the operations of tow trucks. The law set up classes of licenses for types of tow truck businesses, an annual fee to obtain the licenses, and established rates and other requirements for tow truck operators to meet to qualify for inclusion on a list that police would call to assist disabled motor vehicles. The tow truck operators filed an action to permanently enjoin the town's enforcement of the law, and the operators then filed a motion for summary judgment. The court granted the motion in part and denied it in part and held that provisions in the law on non-transferability of licenses, non-solicitation of business, license revocation, and requirements that tow trucks be properly equipped were valid. However, provisions establishing rates and prohibiting trucks without licenses from operating in the town were unreasonable, as were requirements that applicants divulge information to the town clerk, that written agreements with auto body shops were necessary for licensees, and that licenses would not be issued to established businesses. The police department was permanently enjoined from maintaining a list of approved tow truck operators.


OUTCOME: In the operators' action for a permanent injunction against the implementation of the town's licensing law, the court granted partial summary judgment to the operators and denied it in part. The court permanently enjoined the police department from maintaining a list of approved tow truck operators. The law was invalid as to the establishment of rates. The provisions on -solicitation, non-transferability, and equipment were valid.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor vehicles -- regulation of tow-truck operators -- defendant Town of Huntington which enacted local Tow Truck Law requiring licensing of all tow trucks operating within town and also providing for maintenance of approved list of qualified tow truck operators which Suffolk County Police Department must call to remove disabled motor vehicles is permanently enjoined from enforcing those sections of local law declared invalid and void -- since requirements for approved list have been struck down, Suffolk County Police Department is permanently enjoined from maintaining approved list.

1. Plaintiffs who are engaged in the tow truck business seek to permanently enjoin defendant Town of Huntington from enforcing the town Tow Truck Law (Local Law No. 3-74 of the Town of Huntington) on the ground that such law is unconstitutional and void. Among other things, the law provides for the licensing of all tow vehicles operating in the town, and for inspection, regulation and identification of licensed vehicles. The law also provides for maintenance of an approved list of tow truck operators which the Suffolk County Police Department must call to remove disabled motor vehicles, and prescribes [***2] requirements which must be met before a tow truck operator can qualify for inclusion on the list. Section 54-4(A) of the law which prohibits the operation of all tow trucks within the town without first obtaining a license from the town clerk is unconstitutional and void since regulation of all licensed tow trucks using the public highways is subject to exclusive control by the State and the town exceeded its authority in enacting this section. The town is therefore permanently enjoined from enforcing this section.

2. In addition, the Town of Huntington is permanently enjoined from enforcing the following sections of Local Law No. 3-74 since they are invalid and void: section 54-4(B)(8) (delegation to Town Clerk of authority to require disclosure of additional information without guidelines or standards); section 54-9(A) (forbidding any person, firm, or corporation to drive on any street or highway within the town for purposes of soliciting tow work); section 54-11 (requiring licensed tow trucker not engaged in automobile body repair work to have a written agreement with an owner of an automobile body shop allowing him to tow vehicles to such establishment for at least one year);  [***3] sections 54-13 and 54-14 (establishment of maximum rates or charges permitted for a written estimate of the damages to a vehicle and storage charges).

3. Since the requirements for the approved list have been struck down, the Suffolk County Police Department is permanently enjoined from maintaining an approved list and otherwise implementing the applicable provisions of section 54-15.

 

1410 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Taylor v. Dumpson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


79 Misc. 2d 379; 362 N.Y.S.2d 888; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1668



October 16, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff grandmother brought suit on her own behalf, on her grandchildren's behalf, and on behalf of those similarly situated against defendants, city and state agencies and officials. The grandmother sought a declaratory judgment that N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 371(14) violated the Equal Protection Clause as well as the Social Security Act. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The grandmother, whose seven grandchildren had been placed with her by social services and the family court, was denied assistance from a foster care program under § 371(14) because she was the children's grandmother. The court first stated that other relatives of a child would qualify for aid under the statute, while grandparents were arbitrarily excluded, even though under N.Y. Family Ct. Act § 415 grandparents had no legal obligation to support their grandchildren. Such an distinction, the court stated, violated 42 U.S.C.S. § 601 of the Social Security Act and 45 C.F.R. § 233.30, which required that states determine the need for assistance and the amount of assistance on an objective and equitable basis. Holding that N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 371(14) was unconstitutional as applied to the grandmother and those similarly situated, the court found that defendants had not shown a compelling governmental for the distinction. Indeed, it stated, without the grandmother providing care for the children, some would likely have to be placed in institutions, costing the state and city more than it would to give the grandmother assistance and contributing to the breakdown of the family.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment for the grandmother.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social services -- ADC-Foster Care payments -- grandmothers cannot constitutionally be excluded from eligibility for ADC-Foster Care payments.

After a mother abandoned her seven children, they were placed in the care of the maternal grandmother, whose only income was a social security pension. The grandmother thereupon received "Aid to Dependent Children" (Social Services Law, § 343) amounting to $ 588 a month. However, she is entitled to be paid, instead, "Aid to Dependent Children-Foster Care" (Social Services Law, § 392) amounting to $ 1,155 a month. Insofar as the Social Services Law ( § 371, subd. 14) excludes a grandparent from those persons who are entitled to be paid for foster care, said subdivision 14 contravenes the Family Court Act ( § 415), the Social Security Act (U.S. Code, tit. 42, § 601), the Code of Federal Regulations (tit. 45, § 233.20), and the equal protection clause and the due process clause of the State and Federal Constitutions. The court enjoins the State and City Commissioners of Social Services from denying ADC-Foster Care payments to this grandmother and to all others similarly situated.

 

1411 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hollingsworth


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Albany County


79 Misc. 2d 468; 360 N.Y.S.2d 765; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1688



October 16, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the indictment against him under N.Y. Penal Law § 220.39(1), that charged him with the crime for the criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the indictment against him. He challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.39(1) and asserted that his due process rights, equal protection rights, and safeguards against cruel and unusual punishment were violated by his prosecution under it. The court denied the motion. The court found itself particularly troubled with that aspect of the drug law that mandated the imposition of life sentences for certain drug crimes for all defendants. All mandated sentencing removed the flexibility of making the punishment fit the facts of each individual case and eroded the basic strength from the criminal justice system. However, the court's concerns did not support finding the statute unconstitutional. The standard by which the court determined whether a particular punishment was cruel and unusual was based upon the modern concept of cruelty and unusualness prevailing in society at the time that the question was raised. Considering the seriousness of dealing drugs, the statute's provisions mandating life sentences for sellers was not unconstitutional. Defendant's other claims were meritless. The court denied the motion to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment against him.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- drug law -- mandatory sentences -- mandatory sentences for violation of section 220.39 of Penal Law are not unconstitutional as cruel and unusual punishment in view of connection between drug abuse and violent crime and provision is proper exercise of legislative authority.

The mandatory sentencing provisions for violation of section 220.39 of the Penal Law, although precluding consideration of the defendant's individual case, are not unconstitutional as cruel and unusual punishment in view of the connection between drug abuse and violent crime, and the provisions are a proper exercise of legislative authority.

 

1412 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vitale


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


80 Misc. 2d 36; 360 N.Y.S.2d 375; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1836



October 10, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the foregoing, the defendant's motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court previously imposed a concurrent sentence on defendant under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.25 pursuant to his guilty plea for certain charges. Defendant filed an application contending that the sentence was illegal and must be set aside pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20(1), urging that he be resentenced to a conditional or an unconditional discharge in order that the same effect as a concurrent sentence be achieved.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's concurrent sentence was imposed under § 70.25(a). However, since he was sentenced, the department of correctional services had begun to rely on N.Y. Penal Law § 70.30(1), which provided that an indeterminate sentence of imprisonment commenced when the prisoner was received in an institution under the jurisdiction of the state department of correction, to refuse to honor concurrent dispositions of federal prisoners subject to state sentences. Defendant thus argued that the good spirits in which he agreed to plead guilty and in which the court agreed to sentence him would be violated if the relief he requested were not granted. Denying defendant's motion to set aside his sentence under N.Y. Penal Law § 440.20(1), the court held that no constitutional nor statutory barrier prevented the department from refusing to honor concurrent sentences. Thus, when a federal sentence was overturned, a federal prisoner's state sentence would still take full force and effect.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- State sentence to run concurrently with Federal sentence -- defendant was properly sentenced in State court to indeterminate sentence of seven years to be served concurrently with prior Federal sentences; it is denial of equal protection if prisoners serving sentences in Federal prison may not be sentenced in State court to terms to run concurrently with their sentences -- sentencing provisions (Penal Law, § 70.20, subd. 1; § 70.30, subd. 1) should not be read to exclude service of concurrent State sentence at Federal institution; Federal prisoners should receive same treatment as State prisoners subject to concurrent sentences.

1. While serving three concurrent terms of 15, 10 and 5 years in the Federal penitentiary pursuant to sentences imposed by the Federal court in the Eastern District of New York, defendant was sentenced pursuant to section 70.25 of the Penal Law to an indeterminate term of seven years to be served concurrently with the Federal sentences. Although it was the practice of the County Court of Nassau County to sentence Federal prisoners to State terms [***2] to be served concurrently with their Federal sentences, the Department of Correctional Services pursuant to a literal interpretation of section 70.30 of the Penal Law adopted the position that State sentences concurrent with Federal sentences could not commence until the prisoner's release from Federal prison and his arrival at an institution under the jurisdiction of the department which has the effect of eliminating the purpose of imposing a concurrent State sentence. Defendant seeks to be resentenced to a conditional or unconditional discharge in order to achieve the same effect as a concurrent sentence. Since prisoners serving indeterminate sentences in State prisons may be sentenced to a concurrent term if later convicted in a State or Federal court and since prisoners serving sentences in Federal prison may be sentenced to a concurrent term if later convicted in Federal court, it is a denial of equal protection if prisoners serving sentences in Federal prison may not be sentenced to terms to run concurrently with their sentences in a State court.

2. To deny a State court the right to impose concurrent sentences upon Federal prisoners runs contra to the rationale and policy [***3] of section 70.25 of the Penal Law which is to permit concurrent sentences in every appropriate case. The insistence upon arrival and custody at State institutions in subdivision 1 of section 70.20 and subdivision 1 of section 70.30 of the Penal Law is grounded upon an intent to allocate responsibilities within the State system and should not be read to exclude service of a concurrent State sentence at a Federal institution. Federal prisoners should receive the same treatment as State prisoners subject to concurrent sentences. The imposition of defendant's concurrent sentence was proper. Accordingly, defendant's motion is in all respects denied.

 

1413 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hines


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Yonkers


79 Misc. 2d 330; 360 N.Y.S.2d 180; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1659



October 9, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to the provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 270.10, prior to the selection of the jury, defendant challenged the entire panel of jurors for his then pending trial upon misdemeanor charges. Defendant argued that the panel was composed solely of individuals over 21 years of age, and that persons between 18 years and 21 years had been systematically excluded. The People submitted an affidavit in opposition thus requiring a hearing.


OVERVIEW: At the hearing, county court officials testified that pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 657, each city or town in the county was given a roster of the names of qualifying jurors resident in the city or town. When the statute lowering the minimum age for jury duty became effective on September 1, 1974, the county began to integrate the names of the 18-year-olds to 21-year-olds onto its master list. The court held that the preparation of the jury list by the county followed the procedure and requirements set forth in the Judiciary Law. There was no deliberate exclusion of eligibles in the preparation of the master list.The fact that the jury empaneled to try defendant did not contain jurors of his age group was not proven to be constitutionally prejudicial to him, nor had any evidence been submitted of a probability of unfairness in the prospective actions by the panel. Defendant failed to demonstrate a purposeful pattern of discrimination in the selection of the challenged panel or of its source. Therefore, the challenge to the panel was disallowed.


OUTCOME: The court disallowed defendant's challenge to the jury panel.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- challenge to panel of jurors -- challenge to panel of jurors by 18-year-old defendant for his pending trial upon misdemeanor charges on ground that exclusion of persons between 18 years and 21 years from panel violated his constitutional rights is disallowed; defendant has failed to demonstrate purposeful pattern of discrimination in selection of challenged panel or of its source -- fact that Judiciary Law was amended effective September 1, 1974 qualifying individuals between 18 years and 21 years to sit as jurors did not mandate that these newly qualified persons be included immediately in panel used.

1. Pursuant to CPL 270.10, defendant, who is 18 years old, challenged the entire panel of jurors for his pending trial upon misdemeanor charges, on ground that persons between 18 years and 21 years had been excluded from the panel, denying him his right to a trial by his peers and due process of law. The fact that the jury empaneled to try defendant did not contain jurors of his age group was not proven to be constitutionally prejudicial to him, nor has any evidence been submitted by him of a probability of unfairness in the prospective actions by the panel. In addition,  [***2] defendant has failed to demonstrate a purposeful pattern of discrimination in the selection of the challenged panel or of its source.

2. The fact that the Judiciary Law was amended, effective September 1, 1974 (L. 1974, ch. 890), qualifying individuals between 18 years and 21 years to sit as jurors, did not mandate that these newly qualified jurors must be included immediately in the jury panel. Eligible 18-year-olds to 21-year-olds will be incorporated into the over-all roster of jurors and will be on future panels. Since defendant has not borne the burden of demonstrating that he has been constitutionally deprived of his rights, nor has he shown any substantial prejudice in the drawing and return of the panel of jurors, the challenge to the panel is disallowed.

 

1414 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lotto v. Long Island Lighting Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


80 Misc. 2d 8; 361 N.Y.S.2d 961; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1828



October 1, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant utility provider sought a ruling on its motion to dismiss plaintiff customers' class action suit for a declaration that the utility provider's rate schedule was contrary to law, illegal, and invalid, and for a judgment for damages.


OVERVIEW: The customers filed a class action suit against the utility provider seeking a declaration that the utility provider's rate schedule was contrary to law, illegal, and invalid, and for a judgment for damages. The utility provider filed a motion to dismiss the suit, and the court granted it. The court held that the complaint failed to state a cause of action because N.Y. Tax Law § 186-a(12) was not one of the provisions of that section that was or could be made applicable to N.Y. Gen. City Law § 20-b, or N.Y. Village Law § 5-530.


OUTCOME: The court granted the utility provider's motion to dismiss the customers' class action suit for a declaration that the utility provider's rate schedule was contrary to law, illegal, and invalid, and for a judgment for damages.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Public utilities -- city and village gross revenues taxes -- allocation of tax expense -- public utility rate schedule, which allocates to utility customers in villages and cities expense of their respective gross revenue taxes, is valid, since purpose of tax is local benefit and determination of Public Service Commission should be upheld where not unreasonable -- although provisions of State gross revenues tax on utilities are to be applied where possible to local gross revenues taxes, State measure apportioning tax over entire body of users should not be so incorporated, since local tax allocated over entire body of users would unjustly increase rates of customers residing outside area upon which tax is based -- since issue is solely one of statutory construction involving no challenge to factual findings of commission, court has jurisdiction to determine allocation of utility tax expenses.

1. The rate schedule of the Long Island Lighting Company, which allocates to utility customers in the respective villages and cities the expense of city and village gross revenues taxes on utilities assessed pursuant to section 20-b of the General City Law and section 5-530 of the Village [***2] Law, is valid and legal, and such expenses are not required to be apportioned over the entire body to customers in the utility system. Since the purpose of the tax is strictly local benefit, it is appropriate that the tax be by way of local surcharge, and, since the Public Service Commission determination is not irrational or unreasonable, it should be upheld.

2. Subdivision 12 of section 186-a of the Tax Law, which, in effect, directs that the State gross revenues tax on utilities be apportioned over the entire body of users, is not to be incorporated into the local utility revenue laws under the enabling legislation of those laws, which make section 186-a applicable to the extent that it can be made so. The State taxing measure apportions the expense over the same body upon which the tax is based, whereas a local tax apportioned over the entire body of users would unjustly increase the utility rates of customers not residing in cities and villages.

3. Although the unreasonableness of utility rates is a matter reserved to the Public Service Commission, the court has jurisdiction to determine allocation of utility tax expenses, since the issue presented is solely one of construction [***3] of the taxing statutes and involves no challenge to factual findings made in the commission's prior administrative proceedings.

 

1415 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Winejournal, Inc. v. State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


82 Misc. 2d 304; 368 N.Y.S.2d 366; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2034



October 1, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent State Liquor Authority filed a motion to dismiss petitioner publisher's N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against the Authority that sought a declaratory judgment that the Authority's bulletin was unenforceable under the Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The publisher produced a bimonthly magazine which it sold to package liquor stores who in turn distributed it without cost to their customers. The Authority issued a bulletin directed to manufacturers and wholesalers of liquor and wine informing them that in the future package stores were permitted to distribute none but "permitted" publications and that a magazine could be so designated when the Authority accepted for filing a "stipulation" signed by the publisher agreeing to the terms of this bulletin. The publisher twice offered its stipulation for filing and was twice rejected. A demand for a hearing was refused. The court held that the proceeding was warranted to compel the Authority to file the stipulation. There could have been little doubt that on its face the bulletin, in the guise of regulating liquor dealers might have been abridging the freedom of the press.


OUTCOME: The court denied the Authority's motion to dismiss the publisher's petition and the Authority was stayed from implementing its bulletin with respect to the publisher or its publication.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Intoxicating liquors -- "permitted" wine publication -- petitioner wine magazine publisher may, in article 78 proceeding, properly challenge legality of bulletin issued by respondent State Liquor Authority which limited publications that liquor stores could distribute to only "permitted publications"; "permitted publications" must limit its editorial content, may not recommend any brand of wine or liquor, and may only accept "standard brand" and not "private label" advertising; question of whether respondent's bulletin unconstitutionally abridges freedom of press is appropriately raised in article 78 proceeding -- respondent is stayed from implementing its bulletin.

1. Petitioner publishes a bimonthly magazine which it sells to liquor stores who in turn distribute it without cost to their customers. Half of petitioner's revenues comes from sales and the balance from advertising equally divided between liquor distributors and others. The editorial content deals with wines, appraising them, recommending vintages and labels, and other foods and merchandise related to wine. Petitioner may, in an article 78 proceeding, properly challenge the legality of a bulletin issued by respondent [***2] State Liquor Authority which limited the publications that liquor stores could distribute to only "permitted publications". Pursuant to said bulletin, a "permitted publication" must limit its editorial content to such things as recipes, needlework and gossip columns. The magazine "may not contain any other material without the prior approval of the Authority". In addition, the magazine may only accept "standard brand" and not "private label" advertising. The publication may not recommend any brand of wine or liquor; and to be "permitted" it must give the authority access to its books and records "at any time or from time to time." Petitioner's application to become a "permitted publication" was twice denied without a hearing. The question of whether respondent's bulletin unconstitutionally abridges freedom of the press is appropriately raised in an article 78 proceeding.

2. Even if designating "permitted publications" does not involve licenses or permits ( Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 121), a dismissal is not warranted since the right granted by the statute to bring the proceeding is not exclusive.

3. Since petitioner will be irreparably damaged by implementation of the [***3] bulletin, pending the adjudication of this controversy, respondent is stayed from interfering with the circulation or the insertion of advertising or editorial matter with respect to petitioner's publications; and is further stayed from issuing any lists, data or other information from which persons in the wine and liquor industry may conclude that publications exclusive of petitioner's are "permitted publications." The duration of the stay ( Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 121) is not limited to 30 days.

People v. Harris


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Westchester County


79 Misc. 2d 181; 359 N.Y.S.2d 650; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1617



September 27, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for criminally selling a dangerous drug in the third degree, former N.Y. Penal Law § 220.35, and criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the fourth degree, former N.Y. Penal Law § 220.15(1). Of his omnibus motion requesting relief, only his challenge, under both the federal and New York constitutions, to N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06 and N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 220.10(6)(b) remained for disposition.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the sections as they applied to him as a potential second felony offender. He argued that they violated his rights relative to ex post facto laws, double jeopardy, due process, equal protection, and cruel and unusual punishment. Defendant was charged with drug sale and possession offenses which were spelled out under former sections of the criminal statutes which were repealed in 1973. The grand jury which indicted defendant said that he committed the offenses in 1972. The court concluded therefore that neither of the challenged sections had any application to him or to the situation in which he found himself. Clearly then, the court ruled that the laws which defendant challenged had no impact on him. Because the plain truth of the matter was that defendant had no special or personal grievance with these statutes which demanded redress, the court concluded that he lacked the interest which was requisite to standing to question the constitutionality of the statutes. The court added that it was not necessary to wait to for the attorney general to intervene to defend the sections before deciding the challenge.


OUTCOME: The court, on its own motion, recalled so much of its preliminary decision and modified so much of its preliminary order as required intervention by the attorney general. The court denied that part of defendant's motion seeking relief because of asserted unconstitutionality of sections of the criminal law as applied to defendant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- sentence -- second felony offender -- defendant charged with crimes committed in 1972 lacks standing to challenge second felony offender statutes enacted in 1973 since they have no application to crimes committed prior to September 1, 1973.

1. Defendant as a potential second felony offender, charged with crimes committed in 1972, lacks standing to challenge the constitutionality of section 70.06 of the Penal Law and CPL 220.10 (subd. 6, par [b]) since they were enacted in 1973 and have no application to crimes committed prior to September 1, 1973 which are governed by the statutes then in effect.

2. Although it is general policy to call upon the Attorney-General to defend the constitutionality of challenged State statutes, intervention by the Attorney-General is not required in this case.

In re S.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


79 Misc. 2d 519; 359 N.Y.S.2d 645; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1698



September 27, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Respondent's motion to suppress the evidence against him is granted and, there being no other evidence in support of the petition, the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state filed a delinquency proceeding in which respondent minor was charged with possession of heroin. The minor filed a motion to suppress the evidence.


OVERVIEW: The minor was stopped and frisked when a policeman's observations constituted grounds for a reasonable suspicion that he was fleeing from the police. The evidence, a glassine envelope of heroin, was discovered when the policeman demanded to know what was in the minor's clenched fist. The minor sought to suppress the alleged heroin. Granting the motion, the court held that the stop and frisk law, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 140.50, envisaged a reasonable suspicion of a particular crime, but under the circumstances, there were no circumstances pointing to any particular crime. The court found that as the stop was invalid, so was the frisk. The court held that the policeman had no basis for a self-protective frisk for weapons and a frisk for finding evidence was impermissible.


OUTCOME: The court granted the minor's motion to suppress and, there being no other evidence in support of the petition, dismissed the petition charging the minor with possession of heroin.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- juvenile delinquency -- stop and frisk -- 15-year-old boy was obviously walking away from approaching policeman; when told to stop and put his hands against wall, one hand was clenched; on demand, he opened his hand and revealed glassine envelope; in it was heroin; such unconstitutionally seized evidence is ordered suppressed, and juvenile delinquency proceeding is dismissed.

A policeman in a marked police car saw a 15-year-old youth get out of a double-parked car and walk into a basement and, after about a minute, he saw the youth walking back to the car. The policeman drove up to the double-parked car, and thereupon the other youths in that car gestured to the juvenile to walk to the corner. This, the juvenile did, while glancing back. The policeman ordered him to stop and put his hands against a wall. The juvenile did stop, but one hand was clenched. The policeman demanded to know what the juvenile was holding. The boy opened his hand and disclosed a glassine envelope. The envelope contained heroin.  [***2] Nevertheless, this was an unconstitutional stop and frisk (cf. CPL 140.50). The unconstitutionally seized evidence is suppressed; and, since there is no other evidence, the juvenile delinquency proceeding is dismissed.

 

1418 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gyory v. Radgowski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


82 Misc. 2d 553; 369 N.Y.S.2d 583; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2043



September 24, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff passenger sought dismissal of the Ohio Guest Statute and the Ohio Workmen's Compensation Law defenses pleaded by defendants, a driver and a rental car company, in the passenger's suit to recover for injuries sustained in an Ohio automobile accident.


OVERVIEW: The passenger and driver were in Ohio on a business trip. The passenger decided to extend his stay to complete his business. That evening, the passenger was injured when the driver ran a rental car off the road on the way to dinner. The court dismissed the driver's Ohio Guest Statute defense, but not the Ohio Workmen's Compensation Law defense. Because the passenger and driver were domiciled in New York and the car was registered there, New York law controlled. New York public policy was opposed to the exoneration of liability extended by foreign guest statutes. But questions of fact on whether the employer approved the passenger's decision to extend his stay precluded dismissal of the Workmen's Compensation Law defense. The employer's authorization would connect the trip to the passenger's employment and bring the trip within the course of employment, making workmen's compensation the exclusive remedy.


OUTCOME: The court granted the passenger's motion to dismiss as to the Ohio Guest Statute defense, but denied it as to the Workmen's Compensation Law defense.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor vehicles -- foreign guest statute -- workmen's compensation remedy -- plaintiff's employer sent him and coworker to Ohio, for business reasons, and, although trip was scheduled to be of one day's duration, he elected to extend stay when he found mission incomplete at end of first day, and that evening, he was injured when car in which coworker was driving him to dinner, and which was registered in Ohio and rented from corporation licensed to do business in New York, ran off road -- in his personal injury action against coworker and lessor of car, affirmative defense based upon Ohio guest statute is dismissed -- however, questions of fact exist which preclude dismissal of affirmative defense based upon Workmen's Compensation Law.

1. Plaintiff's employer sent him and a coworker to Ohio, for business reasons, and, although plaintiff's trip was scheduled to be of one day's duration, he elected to extend his stay for an additional day when he found his mission incomplete at the end of the first day. That evening, he was injured when the car in which his coworker was driving him to dinner ran off the road. The car was registered in Ohio, had been reserved by the employer,  [***2] and was rented by the coworker from a foreign corporation licensed to do business in New York. In his present personal injury action against his coworker and the lessor of the car, an affirmative defense based upon the Ohio guest statute, which precludes recovery by a gratuitous passenger in the absence of a showing of willful or wanton misconduct on the part of the vehicle's operator or owner, is dismissed, on the authority of Pahmer v Hertz Corp. (36 AD2d 252, affd 32 NY2d 119).

2. An affirmative defense based upon the Workmen's Compensation Law must stand if plaintiff's injury was incurred in the course of his employment. Where business trips are concerned, a slight and momentary deviation from the work routine will be distinguished from purely personal activity, and the risk of travel to a place of rest or refreshment may be compensable. Questions of fact exist which preclude dismissal of the defense.

 

1419 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Anonymous


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Nassau County


79 Misc. 2d 280; 359 N.Y.S.2d 738; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1646



September 23, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner stepfather sought to adopt the children of the mother and respondent father, who was incarcerated for an indeterminate term. The father was cited to show cause why his consent should not have been dispensed with on the grounds of his abandonment of the children and his loss of civil rights, pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111.


OVERVIEW: The father's civil rights had been suspended during his sentence pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 79(1). N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111 provided in part that, in an adoption proceeding, consent was not required of a parent who had abandoned the child or of a parent who had been deprived of civil rights. The father contended that the language of § 111, which referred to the deprivation of his civil rights, was void for vagueness because it did not specify which civil rights an imprisoned person must be deprived of in order to fall within the operation of § 111. The court ordered that the matter was to be calendared for a hearing on whether the proposed adoption was in the best interest of the children. The court held that § 111 was not in conflict with N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 79(1) or with the historical development of the New York Civil Rights Law. Further, N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111 was not void for vagueness. There was no violation of due process, equal protection, or the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment because the United States Constitution did not forbid the selection of an alternative that favored the welfare of the public, and specifically, a child.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the matter of the stepfather's petition for adoption was to appear on the calendar for a hearing on the issue of whether the proposed adoption was in the best moral and temporal interests of the children.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Adoption -- consent of natural father -- prisons and prisoners -- civil rights -- restoration to prisoners of right to commence action does not remove respondent, deprived of all civil rights by sentence to indeterminate prison term, from operation of section 111 of Domestic Relations Law, which dispenses with parental consent for adoption of child of person deprived of all civil rights -- respondent's due process and equal protection rights are not contravened by adoption of his children without his consent, since State has power to cause separation where interests of public or child and parent conflict through indefinite disruption of parent's relationship with child.

1. The enactment of subdivision 2 of section 79 of the Civil Rights Law, which restores to prisoners sentenced to an indeterminate prison term and thereby deprived of all civil rights, the right to commence an action or proceeding, does not remove respondent prisoner from the operation of section 111 of the Domestic Relations Law, which dispenses with parental consent for adoption of the child of a person deprived of his civil rights.

2. The elimination of the necessity of respondent's consent to his children's [***2] adoption pursuant to section 111 of the Domestic Relations Law does not contravene respondent's due process and equal protection rights. Where the best interests of the child or the public unavoidably conflict with those of the parent because the parent's status creates an indefinite disruption of his relationship with his children, the State has the power to cause separation without parental consent.

Harren v. Middle Island Central School Dist. No. 12


No. 74-12656


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2074; 19 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 319
