1421 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Remeny


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


79 Misc. 2d 160; 359 N.Y.S.2d 504; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1609



September 19, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Thus, the defendant's motion to have the charge of violating subdivision 5 of section 755 (2)-7.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York dismissed and to have this court declare said statute unconstitutional is denied. The defendant is found guilty of violating subdivision 5 of section 755 (2)-7.0 of the Administrative Code.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to have dismissed and declared unconstitutional a charge brought under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 755(2)-7.0, which prohibited littering and distribution of commercial or business advertising material.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was distributing handbills to pedestrians, which contained information that a concert was to be performed. The various prices of admission tickets were displayed on the handbill. No other information appeared on the handbill. The State indicted defendant for littering and distribution of commercial or business advertising material, and defendant sought dismissal of the charges on grounds that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 755(2)-7.0 was an unconstitutional abridgment of the freedom of press and speech protected by the First Amendment. The court found defendant guilty and denied defendant's motion to dismiss the charges. The court held that the United States Constitution imposed no restraint on a government as respecting purely commercial advertising. The court reasoned that the handbills were commercial advertising in a business context and held that the fact that the a concert represented a form of expression did not cause its purely commercial advertising leaflets to be protected as free speech.


OUTCOME: The court found defendant guilty and denied defendant's motion to have the charges dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- littering -- freedom of speech -- while musical concert represents expression protected by First Amendment, purely commercial advertising leaflets giving time of concert, place, date, names of performers and admission price, are not also protected -- Administrative Code of City of New York (§ 755 [2]-7.0, subd. 5), which prohibits distribution in public place of only purely commercial and business advertising matter, does not abridge individual freedoms and is therefore constitutional.

1. Defendant, while in front of Madison Square Garden, distributed handbills to pedestrians which contained information with respect to a concert and listed its date, time, place, name of performers and admission price. He was charged with violating subdivision 5 of section 755 (2)-7.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which [***2] prohibits littering and, specifically states "this section is not intended to prevent the lawful distribution of anything other than commercial and business advertising matter." Upon defendant's motion to have the charge of violating the statute dismissed and to have the statute declared unconstitutional, the motion is denied. Defendant's leaflets are purely commercial or business advertising, and while a concert represents "speech" protected by the First Amendment, the fact that a concert represents a form of expression does not cause its purely commercial advertising leaflets to be also protected. By prohibiting such leaflets from being distributed, the city is not preventing the concert from taking place or in any way inhibiting it.

2. The statute is not unconstitutional since a municipality may enact local rules and regulations in the interest of public safety, health, welfare or convenience of its citizenry, so long as the enactments do not abridge the individual liberties to speak, write, print or circulate information or opinions. The statute, which prohibits only purely commercial and business advertising matter, does not abridge individual freedoms.

 

1422 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Maryland


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Westchester County


79 Misc. 2d 69; 359 N.Y.S.2d 477; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1585



September 9, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant made application to the court for leave to serve a notice of alibi to the state. The state opposed the application because defendant failed to comply with the provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.20 by timely responding to the alibi demand the state served on defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted on several counts. The state served an alibi demand on defendant, but he did not respond within the time allowed. Instead, more than six months later, he sought leave to serve a notice of alibi on the state. Defendant offered no excuse for his failure to respond earlier to the demand, but claimed it was crucial to his defense that the alibi witnesses be allowed to testify. Although the state opposed the application, the court granted the state time to serve a new alibi demand and for defendant to respond by service a copy of the notice. It noted that the section under which the demand was served was determined to be unconstitutional because it lacked a reciprocal provision allowing a defendant to discover the identity of the state's alibi rebuttal witness. As such, the alibi demand served by the state was a nullity and defendant was under no obligation to pay it any head.


OUTCOME: The court granted the state time to serve a new alibi demand as the prior notice was rendered a nullity when the statute under which it was issued was deemed to be unconstitutional and granted defendant time to respond by serving a notice of alibi on the state.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- alibi -- since former CPL 250.20 was unconstitutional, defendant is allowed to serve notice of alibi upon prosecutor more than six months after defendant was served with alibi demand -- amendment which cured former CPL 250.20 (L. 1974, ch. 420, eff. May 23, 1974) has no retrospective application as to revitalize demand made prior to its effective date -- constitutional challenge to former CPL 250.20 cannot be turned aside by reading reciprocal discovery right into it -- based on court's inherent authority to supervise discovery, People's time to serve new alibi demand is extended.

1. Upon an application by defendant for leave to serve a notice of alibi upon the prosecutor more than six months after defendant was served an alibi demand, even though at the time CPL 250.20 mandated that defendant's notice be served within four days after he was served with demand, the application is granted. Since section 250.20 was unconstitutional for lack of a reciprocal provision allowing a defendant to discover the identity of the People's alibi rebuttal witnesses, the alibi demand so served was a nullity.

2. Although the infirmity in former CPL 250.20 was cured by inserting in [***2] it a reciprocal discovery right for criminal defendants (L. 1974, ch. 420, eff. May 23, 1974), the amendment has no such retrospective application as would revitalize a demand made prior to the date upon which it became effective.

3. The demand is not saved by virtue of the fact that it contained an offer to disclose the People's alibi rebuttal witnesses, since a constitutional challenge to former CPL 250.20 cannot be turned aside by reading a requirement for reciprocal discovery into it.

4. Exercising the court's inherent authority to supervise discovery and disclosure, the People's time to serve a new alibi demand is extended until five days after the District Attorney is served with a copy of the decision and order with notice of entry.

 

1423 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gardner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Westchester County


78 Misc. 2d 744; 359 N.Y.S.2d 196; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1487



September 6, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the second branch of defendant's omnibus motion is denied in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with heroin trafficking and heroin possession, moved to have the life imprisonment maximum sentence portion of certain sentencing statutes declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession and sale of a heroin. Defendant moved to have the penalty provisions of the statutes under which he was prosecuted, N.Y. Penal Law §§ 65.00(1)(b), 220.16(1), 220.39(1), and N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 220.10, declared unconstitutional on the grounds that the maximum possible punishment, life imprisonment, was cruel and unusual punishment. Defendant also alleged that the prohibition on plea-bargaining violated his due process rights. The trial court denied defendant's motion, holding that life imprisonment in the abstract was neither inherently severe nor excessive and that indeterminate sentences were neither cruel nor unusual. The court also held that potential life sentences for drug crimes were not cruel or unusual, given the attendant violence that often accompanied drug trafficking and the detrimental effect on all areas of life associated with drug abuse. Finally, the court found no due process violation in an anti-plea bargaining clause because the legislature was empowered to restrict the prosecuting attorney in that fashion, and defendants lacked any due process rights to enter plea negotiations.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to have a sentencing statute declared unconstitutional was denied. The court held that life imprisonment was not cruel and unusual punishment in the abstract, and it was not disproportionate for drug offenses, given the violence associated with such crimes and the decay in social order caused by drug abuse.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- drug law -- provisions in Penal Law which classify certain drug offenses as A-I, A-II and A-III felonies and provide for imposition of mandatory maximum sentence of life imprisonment upon person convicted of such offense, do not violate constitutional prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment -- claim that provision requiring recommendation by prosecutor before offender convicted of drug offense classified as A-III felony may be sentenced to probation transfers undue amount of power of punishment to prosecutor raises no justiciable constitutional issue, and, in any event, final decision still rests with judicial arm -- provision which forecloses defendant from pleading guilty to lesser offense than class A felony where indictment charges him with any class A drug crime does not unconstitutionally discriminate against class of individuals indicted for A-III drug crime -- no justiciable constitutional issue is raised by claim of inequality in plea bargaining rights based upon fact that criminal action may be commenced either by indictment or by filing of felony  [***2] complaint in inferior court.

1. Provisions in the Penal Law, including sections 70.00 (subd. 2, par. [a]), 220.16 and 220.39 thereof, which were enacted in 1973 as part of the State's new drug law, and which classify certain drug offenses as A-I, A-II and A-III felonies and provide for the imposition of a mandatory maximum sentence of life imprisonment upon a person convicted of such an offense, do not violate the constitutional prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment.

2. A claim that section 65.00 (subd. 1, par. [b]) of the Penal Law, providing for the sentencing of an offender to lifetime probation upon conviction of a drug offense classified as an A-III felony and requiring, as one of the conditions for imposition of such a sentence, that the prosecutor recommend it, transfers an undue amount of the power of punishment to the prosecutor, amounts to a baseless onslaught against prosecutorial discretion and raises no justiciable constitutional issue. Moreover, under the terms of that section, the final decision as to the propriety of lifetime probation rests with the judicial arm.

3. CPL 220.10 (subd. 6, par. [a]), which forecloses a defendant from pleading guilty [***3] to any lesser offense than a class A felony where the indictment charges him with one of the class A felonies defined in article 220 of the Penal Law, dealing exclusively with drug offenses, does not constitute constitutionally impermissible discrimination in favor of the class of individuals who have been indicted for A-I, A-II, B, C, D and E felonies, drug oriented or not, and against the class of individuals who have been indicted for A-III felonies. The proscription against guilty pleas to lesser offenses where the indictment charges an A-III drug crime rationally promotes the legislative objective of deterring drug abuse and traffic.

4. No justiciable constitutional issue is raised by a claim of inequality in plea bargaining rights based upon the fact that a criminal action may be commenced either by an indictment or by the filing of a felony complaint in an inferior court. While CPL 180.50 permits a Judge of an inferior court, with the consent of the prosecutor, to reduce or convert a felony complaint to one charging a nonfelony offense, that section is not a plea bargaining statute but is designed to take care of the case where the charge of any felony is unwarranted.

 

1424 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Greenspun v. Shilling


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


78 Misc. 2d 517; 359 N.Y.S.2d 189; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1435



September 4, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the application is denied and this proceeding is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidates filed an action against respondents, a candidate and others, in which petitioners sought to require the board of elections to set up the primary ballot in such a manner as to assure that the names of each of the candidates for the nomination for a judge's office appeared in the first and each succeeding position in an equal number of election districts.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners had circulated joint designating petitions. Respondent candidate filed his own designating petition. Based on these petitions, the board of elections set up the voting machines pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law § 242-a(7), which provided that where two or more candidates were to be elected to a position, the names of the candidates for the position that appeared on the same designating petition were to be grouped together on the ballot in the order in which their names appeared on the designating petition and the group rotated alphabetically in relation to other groups or individual candidates according to the surname of the first person on the designating petition of such group. Stating that the statute clearly spelled out the legislature's intention that candidates on jointly filed petitions be considered as groups, the court rejected petitioners' argument that the statute violated petitioners' equal protection rights. It stated that petitioners could have filed individual petitions but chose to avail themselves of the benefits of circulating joint petitions, such as the ability to qualify with fewer signatures and voter identification as a "slate."


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners' application and dismissed the proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- primary elections -- alphabetical order of candidates -- in primary election in which five candidates were running for four New York City Judgeships, independent candidate's name properly appeared first, on voting machines in 50% of election districts, and last on voting machines in other 50% of election districts; and in all election districts, names of other four candidates properly appeared in same alphabetical order in which they had been designated by their joint petition (Election Law, § 242-a, subd. 7).

For a Democratic primary election to nominate four candidates for election as Judges of the New York City Civil Court, four candidates were designated by one joint petition, and one independent candidate was designated by a separate petition. The independent candidate's surname began with S. The other four candidates had surnames which began with L, G, T, and O, respectively; that is, that was the order in which their names appeared on their joint petition. The Board of Elections properly, lawfully and constitutionally set up the names of these five candidates in the following [***2] order on the voting machines in 50% of the election districts: L, G, T, O, S; and in the following order on the voting machines in the other 50% of the election districts: S, L, G, T, O. (Election Law, § 242-a, subd. 7, as amd. by L. 1973, ch. 391.)

 

1425 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


State v. Reed


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


78 Misc. 2d 1004; 359 N.Y.S.2d 185; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1546



September 3, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff the State of New York filed an action complaining that defendant landowners were constructing a driveway through wetlands, in violation of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 25-0202, which prohibited the altering of any tidal wetlands in the State without first obtaining a permit from the Department of Environmental Conservation.


OVERVIEW: The landowners argued that since they did not need a permit under N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 15-0505 this was tantamount to one having been issued prior to the enactment of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 25-0202; therefore, they were not barred by § 25-0202 from constructing the driveway. The court noted however that § 25-0202 provided that if one had a permit under § 15-0505, one could alter the marsh. As the landowners did not have a permit, the court ruled that § 25-0202 controlled. The landowners also argued that the town was not subject to the general controls of the State over its wetlands because of early patents. The court maintained, however, that the State's general regulatory powers applied to all lands within the county whether proprietary or not. The court rejected the landowners' argument that the state was not a proper party. The court held that the landowners should follow procedures set forth in § 25-0202 including a hardship hearing before the commissioner, in order to bring a claim alleging that the statute was unconstitutional. The court finally rejected the landowners' argument that the State's complaint failed to state a cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court found that the State was a proper party and that the State's complaint stated a cause of action.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Environmental conservation -- tidal wetlands -- all tidal wetlands in State are covered by ECL 25-0202 -- defendants are not entitled to alter wetlands without permit, despite fact that they did not need permit under ECL 15-0505, since escape clause in ECL 25-0202 operates only where permit under ECL 15-0505 had been secured -- State regulatory power over wetlands applies regardless of proprietary rights -- if ECL 25-0202 is alleged to be confiscatory, defendants should avail themselves of procedures therein -- State is proper party to bring action under ECL 25-0202.

1. All tidal wetlands in the State are covered by ECL 25-0202, notwithstanding that ECL 15-0505 has a narrower application, since the latter covers only lands adjacent to navigable waters, defined as excluding the waters off Nassau and Suffolk counties.

2. The defendants are not entitled to alter their wetlands without a permit under ECL 25-0202, since the escape clause in the statute operates only where the landowner had already secured a permit under ECL 15-0505. The fact that defendants did not need a permit under ECL 15-0505 does not bring them within the clause.

3. State regulatory powers over wetlands [***2] apply regardless of State proprietary rights.

4. If ECL 25-0202 is alleged to be confiscatory, the defendants should first avail themselves of the procedures in the statute.

5. The State is a proper party to bring suit under ECL 25-0202.

 

1426 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. D'Arcy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Allegany County


79 Misc. 2d 113; 359 N.Y.S.2d 453; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1595



August 29, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion of the defendant for dismissal of the indictment and counts therein is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A criminal defendant, indicted on multiple counts related to sexual abuse of children, moved to dismiss his indictment on various grounds.


OVERVIEW: A criminal defendant, indicted on numerous counts related to sexual assaults on children, moved to dismiss his indictment. The trial court denied his motion, holding that defendant lacked standing to argue that the gender-based protection scheme of N.Y. Penal Law § 260.10 was unconstitutional since he was not affected by that scheme. The court found that the indictment adequately set out violations of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 260.10 and 195.00 since it tracked those statutes, and that any complaint defendant had over the lack of specificity was best cured via a bill of particulars. The court further found that the indictments were not subject to dismissal under N.Y. Penal Law § 135.05 since validity of the victims' written consent was in dispute. The court held that double jeopardy did not bar both unlawful imprisonment and child endangerment charges, since the elements of the crimes were different. Finally, the court held that the trial court did not err in joining multiple charges and victims given the commonality in the evidence and the allegations.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss his indictment holding, inter alia, that defendant lacked standing to bring an equal protection claim, that the indictment adequately tracked statutory language, and that there was no double jeopardy bar because the crimes contained different elements.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- joinder of offenses -- District Attorney is properly charged, in one indictment, with 29 counts of official misconduct, 16 counts of endangering welfare of children, 29 counts of unlawful imprisonment, 8 counts of criminal possession of forged instruments, 2 counts of forgery, and 1 count of assault -- no double jeopardy -- allegations are sufficiently concise and precise -- violation of Penal Law ( § 195.00) is properly alleged in disjunctive -- allegation of "without consent" includes consents which were obtained by intimidation or deception (Penal Law, § 135.00, subd. 1) -- Penal Law ( § 260.10, subd. 1) is constitutional.

1. The Penal Law ( § 260.10, subd. 1) validly and constitutionally provides that "A person is guilty of endangering the welfare of a child when: 1. He knowingly acts in a manner likely to be injurious to the physical, mental or moral welfare of a male child less than sixteen years old or a female child less than seventeen years old". The equal protection clause of the Federal and State Constitutions applies to the rights of the children, not the offender's.

 [***2] 2. The indictment properly and concisely ( CPL 200.50, subd. 7) uses the above statute's words, plus the following: "by physically restraining, binding and gagging said child; and, to these words, one of the counts properly adds "and exhibiting to her photographs, written material and moving pictures which were sexually stimulating."

3. The indictment further charges, properly so in the disjunctive words of the Penal Law ( § 195.00), that the defendant, while he was the District Attorney, did, "with intent to obtain a benefit or to injure or deprive another person of a benefit", commit acts "relating to his office but constituting an unauthorized exercise of his official functions, knowing that such" acts were "unauthorized" (Penal Law, § 195.00, subd. 1), in that, under pretense of official authority, he did restrain one, etc., and in that he used an official letter and subpoena for a specified purpose which was wholly outside the scope of his functions as District Attorney. These allegations are sufficient ( CPL 200.50, subd. 7, and 210.25, subd. 1).

4. The indictment further properly charges that the defendant committed the crime of unlawful imprisonment in the second degree [***3] (Penal Law, § 135.00, subd. 1; § 135.05; cf. CPL 200.50, subd. 7, and 210.25, subd. 1) "by confining her limbs and body without her consent and with knowledge that such restriction was unlawful". If a child over 16 signed a written consent through "intimidation or deception", then she was indeed "confined 'without consent'" (Penal Law, § 135.00, subd. 1).

5. Even if the charges of unlawful imprisonment and endangering the welfare of children are proved at the trial by much the same acts or conduct, the two crimes "have substantially different elements" ( CPL 40.20, subd. 2, pars. [a], [b]) and hence the defendant will not be in double jeopardy for the same crime (cf. Penal Law, § 260.10, subd. 2, and § 120.10).

6. The indictment against defendant properly contains 29 counts of official misconduct (Penal Law, § 195.00), 29 counts of unlawful imprisonment in the second degree (Penal Law, § 135.05), 16 counts of endangering the welfare of children (Penal Law, § 260.10), 8 counts of criminal possession of forged instruments in the third degree (Penal Law, § 170.20), 2 counts of forgery in the third degree (Penal Law, § 170.05), and 1 count of assault in the third degree [***4] (Penal Law, § 120.00). These 85 counts are properly joined in one indictment ( CPL 200.20, subd. 2; CPL 40.10).

People v. Hertz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Nassau County


78 Misc. 2d 1033; 359 N.Y.S.2d 182; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1551



August 28, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss the information herein is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with unlawfully possessing noxious material and filed a motion to dismiss the information.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 270.05 for unlawfully possessing noxious material. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information against him. The court determined that it was not the legislature's intention to place persons in possession of common household and garden insecticides and sprays in a situation that could make them subject to arrest and prosecution by reason of the presumption that possession was evidence of intent to use or cause it to be used in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 270.05. Instead, the court determined that the legislature's intention was that of prohibiting the possession and possible use by private persons of such products known as tear gas, mace, or other products similar thereto, which were used by law enforcement agencies. Therefore, the court held that N.Y. Penal Law § 270.05 as written, was unreasonably and unconstitutionally overbroad. Accordingly, defendant's motion to dismiss the information was granted.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawfully possessing noxious material -- section 270.05 of Penal Law, which prohibits possession of containers holding substances capable of generating noxious gases under circumstances evincing intent to cause injury, intent to be presumed from possession, is unconstitutionally overbroad, since statute includes common household insecticides; defendant's motion to dismiss information is, accordingly, granted.

 Section 270.05 of the Penal Law, which prohibits possession of any container holding any substance capable of generating offensive, noxious or suffocating gases under circumstances evincing intent to use such material to cause injury, annoyance or to disturb the public peace, such intent to be presumed from the fact of possession, is unreasonably and unconstitutionally overbroad. Although the legislative intent was to prohibit use by private persons of "tear gas", "mace" and similar products, the statute includes such products as common household insecticides. Accordingly, the defendant's motion to dismiss the information is granted.

 

1428 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Broome County


79 Misc. 2d 172; 359 N.Y.S.2d 446; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1613



August 26, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, we believe the lower court's ruling to be erroneous and we reverse the trial order of dismissal based thereon.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People challenged the order of the Village Court of Johnson City (New York), which dismissed a simplified information charging defendant with driving while intoxicated in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(3).


OVERVIEW: Prior to the commencement of a trial on the charge of driving while intoxicated, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(3), the prosecutor made an offer of proof with respect to his intention to present evidence pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1194(2) of defendant's refusal to take a blood test. The village court refused to allow the prosecution to introduce evidence of refusal and declared § 1194(2) unconstitutional. The court held that: (1) there were no constitutional rights or privileges to prevent a state from compelling a person suspected of driving while intoxicated to submit to a blood test; (2) a person was not entitled to Miranda warnings prior to the extraction of his blood; (3) because the statute equated a refusal with guilt by revoking the driver's license and expressed a strong policy to protect the public from the threat of drunken driving, there was no compelling reason to forbid comment on a person's refusal to take a blood test; and (4) to forbid comment upon such a refusal unduly restricted the legislative intent and unnecessarily prevented the use of competent and probative evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order of dismissal.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- operating vehicle while intoxicated -- refusal to submit to blood test -- in defendant's trial for driving while intoxicated, ruling prohibiting prosecution from introducing evidence of defendant's refusal to submit to blood test by declaring that part of subdivision 2 of section 1194 of Vehicle and Traffic Law permitting introduction of such evidence unconstitutional is erroneous; defendant suspected of driving while intoxicated has no constitutional right to refuse to submit to such test, and it necessarily follows that there can be no constitutional prohibition preventing comment upon accused's failure to take test.

Defendant, arrested for operating a motor vehicle while intoxicated, refused to submit to a blood test. At defendant's trial, the prosecution pursuant to subdivision 2 of section 1194 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law sought to introduce evidence of this refusal, but the trial court declaring that part of the statute permitting introduction of such evidence unconstitutional, would not allow such evidence introduced and [***2] dismissed the information. There are no constitutional rights or privileges preventing a State from compelling a person suspected of driving while intoxicated to submit to a blood test, and furthermore a person is not entitled to Miranda warnings prior to the extraction of blood. Consequently, since there is no constitutional right to refuse to submit to such a test, it necessarly follows that there can be no constitutional prohibition to prevent comment upon the accused's failure to take the test. The defendant's right of refusal is not a constitutional right but rather is a qualified statutory right whereby the defendant as well as the police is able to avoid the unpleasantness connected with adminstering a blood test on an unwilling subject. Accordingly, the lower court's ruling is erroneous and the trial order of dismissal is reversed.

People v. Mallard


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


79 Misc. 2d 270; 359 N.Y.S.2d 622; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1644



August 21, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In addressing itself to the immediate motion, the application and motion to strike the testimony of the witness Jackson is denied. The application and motion of the District Attorney for an immediate Wade or identification hearing at this juncture is granted and the court directs that the motion proceed toward a hearing forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought to suppress certain identification evidence pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 710.30 during a jury trial in which plaintiff State had not previously given notice of its intent to use the evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that his rights would be seriously prejudiced if the State were permitted to introduce identification evidence during his trial, despite the prosecutor's failure to comply with the notice provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 710.30. The court weighed the issues of defendant not having the use of a transcript of a hearing on the one hand, as opposed to the dismissal of an indictment and the denial of the ability of the State to proceed to trial on the other hand. The court found that the prejudice would not be of such a nature as to direct the court to grant the motion to suppress on the issue of prejudice. The court noted that the procedure set forth in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 710.30 was in form only and that the substantive constitutional right which was activated by the procedure was given to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of the State and denied defendant's motion to strike the testimony of the identification witness.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- identification -- robbery -- complainant was People's sole witness; District Attorney had not notified defendant that complainant had identified defendant prior to trial; nevertheless, trial court will conduct identification hearing ( CPL 710.30, subd. 2).

On this jury trial for robbery, the People's sole witness was the complainant. He testified that defendant had robbed him at knifepoint. Apparently the complainant had gone at once to the police station and had returned to the scene of the holdup and there pointed out the defendant, who was then playing dice with some others. However, the District Attorney had not given the defendant a pretrial notice of such intended evidence ( CPL 710.30). Nevertheless, the trial court will conduct a hearing to determine whether or not the out-of-court identification was so unconstitutionally [***2] suggested to the witness as to require that all testimony identifying the defendant be excluded ( CPL 710.30, subd. 2).

People v. Mosley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


78 Misc. 2d 736; 358 N.Y.S.2d 1004; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1486



August 20, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who pled guilty to the charge of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree, brought a motion to declare N.Y. Penal Law § 70.00(2)(a)(3) unconstitutional as violative of U.S. Const. amend. VIII.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that N.Y. Penal Law § 70.00(2)(a)(3) violated the ban on cruel and unusual punishment in U.S. Const. amend. VIII. The court held that the constitutionality of a sentence may be measured by means of the following tests: (1) nature of the offense, taking into consideration the violent or nonviolent nature of the crime, the aggravated or non-aggravated nature of the particular circumstances of the case, the degree of dangerousness of the crime to society; (2) the nature of the offender; (3) the punishment compared with punishment for other offenses in the same jurisdiction; (4) the punishment compared with the punishment for the same offense in other jurisdictions; (5) the recommendations of model legislation; and (6) the conscience of the court. The court found that the punishment imposed under the statute for narcotics offenders was disproportionate to other sentences imposed on offenders who committed more violent crimes. The court reclassified defendant's crime as a class C felony for sentencing purposes.


OUTCOME: The court held the mandatory sentencing provision classifying the crime of selling a narcotic drug as a felony as unconstitutional. The court reclassified the crime as a class C felony and ordered defendant resentenced.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- drug law -- provision in section 70.00 (subd. 2, par. [a]) of Penal Law for imposition of mandatory maximum sentence of life imprisonment upon person convicted of drug offense classified as A-III felony is unconstitutional as violative of prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment -- defendant who has pleaded guilty to such offense will be sentenced under law controlling class C felony, classification of his offense prior to enactment of State's new drug law in 1973.

1. The provision in section 70.00 (subd. 2, par. [a]) of the Penal Law for the imposition of a mandatory maximum sentence of life imprisonment upon a person convicted of a drug offense classified as an A-III felony is unconstitutional as violative of the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment (U. S. Const., Eighth Amdt., N.Y. Const., art. I, § 5).

2. A defendant who has pleaded guilty to the criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree, a class A-III felony, will be sentenced under the law controlling a class C felony, the classification of that crime prior to the enactment of the State's new drug law in 1973.

People v. Cudak


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Suffolk County


78 Misc. 2d 893; 358 N.Y.S.2d 909; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1517



August 16, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of this fact and all the foregoing, the court has no alternative other than to find that the People have failed to prove the defendants guilty beyond a reasonable doubt and hence, find the defendants not guilty.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged by plaintiff State with violating N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 25-0202, altering lands adjacent to a wetland.


OVERVIEW: Defendants argued that they could not be convicted of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 25-0202 for altering adjacent lands, because the term adjacent was not described in the statute, and the determination of the Commissioner of Environmental Conservation was not published and, therefore, not accessible to defendants. The court stated that it would be unconstitutional to subject defendants to criminal liability due to the commissioner's unpublished determination that adjacent lands included any lands within 300 feet of the high tide line. Although the statute created a means whereby actions such as those engaged in by defendants could be proscribed and made a violation of law, the Commissioner of Environmental Conservation failed to file a document with the Secretary of State for publication, which would have made the acts unlawful.


OUTCOME: The court ruled against the State and found defendants not guilty of altering lands adjacent to a wetland.


HEADNOTES

Environmental conservation -- dumping on wetlands -- defendants are not guilty of dumping fill on wetlands or adjacent areas, since there is no clear showing of dumping on wetlands and since discretion is vested in commissioner to determine extent of adjacent areas, and he neither filed nor published his determination, which was therefore not effective.

The defendants are not guilty of violating section 25-0202 of the Environmental Conservation Law, which prohibits dumping of fill on wetlands or upon adjacent areas "as the commissioner may reasonably deem necessary to preserve". There is no clear showing that defendants dumped on the wetlands, and with respect to the adjacent areas, the commissioner failed to file a purported determination that adjacent areas shall be deemed to extend 300 feet from tidal wetlands. Since discretion to determine what is an adjacent area to a wetland is vested solely in the commissioner, the court may not make such a determination.  [***2] The defendants may not be convicted for violating a regulation which was not accessible because not filed or published and which was not effective, therefore, under section 8 of article IV of the New York State Constitution. 

 

1432 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Jones v. Banner Moving & Storage, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


78 Misc. 2d 762; 358 N.Y.S.2d 885; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1489; 15 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 1



August 12, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff personal property owner (owner) filed suit against defendant storage company for replevin and for a declaratory judgment that N.Y. U.C.C. Law §§ 7-209, 7-210 were violative of her constitutional rights to due process. The owner filed a motion for partial summary judgment on her constitutional claim and the storage company filed a cross-motion to dismiss for failure to state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The owner alleged that she was evicted while she was out of town. Upon her return, the owner found that all of her furniture and household possessions had been removed by the storage company and taken to its warehouse for storage. The owner was notified that the storage company intended to keep her property until all charges were paid and that the property would be sold at public sale if she failed to pay. The court denied the storage company's cross-motion to dismiss because the complaint posed the stated causes of action. The court granted the owner's motion for partial summary judgment that §§ 7-209, 7-210 were unconstitutional because they offered no protection against an illegal detention and sale, as there was no provision for a hearing, at any time, or any other judicial control of due process. The court also held that it had jurisdiction over the constitutional issue concurrently with the federal courts and that the owner had posed a justiciable controversy that was substantial and ripe for judicial determination.


OUTCOME: The court denied the storage company's cross-motion to dismiss and granted the owner partial summary judgment on her constitutional claim.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- warehouseman's lien and sale -- Uniform Commercial Code ( §§ 7-209, 7-210), providing for warehouseman's lien and sale, does not provide for hearing or judicial control (Uniform Commercial Code, §§ 7-209, 7-210) and hence is unconstitutional as to evicted tenant whose possessions were moved and stored.

A tenant was evicted from her apartment and, for her furniture and possessions, she was given a receipt or inventory by a moving and storage company, which subsequently sent her a bill for $ 697.05 and informed her that, unless paid, it would sell her property at public auction. The statute providing for such warehouseman's lien and sale (Uniform Commercial Code, § 7-209, subd. [1]; § 7-210, subds. [2], [3]) does not give the lienee any opportunity for a hearing, nor does the statute provide for judicial control of the process of seizure and sale. Therefore, the statute is unconstitutional (U.S. Const., 14th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6). The moving and storage company is ordered to return the evictee's property, at the warehouse.

 

1433 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Broidrick v. Lindsay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


78 Misc. 2d 933; 358 N.Y.S.2d 879; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1527; 8 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 607; 8 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9733



August 6, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens filed a motion for summary judgment in their declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of rules promulgated by respondent city administrator.


OVERVIEW: The city administrator promulgated rules requiring bidders on city contracts to provide a specified minimum percentage of minority man-hours of work in each specified building and construction trade. The citizens filed a declaratory judgment action against the city administrator challenging the constitutionality of the rules. The citizens filed a motion for summary judgment. Upon review, the court granted the motion. The court determined that the action was not barred by the statute of limitations because it challenged the constitutionality of the rules rather than the propriety of an administrative body. The court ruled that the citizens had standing to file the action because it involved an issue of general public interest.The court held that the rules were legislative in nature rather than administrative, so the promulgation of the rules by the city administrator was unconstitutional. The court denied the city administrator's motion to stay the action pending the outcome of a federal action.


OUTCOME: The court entered a summary judgment for the citizens in their declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of the rules.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- regulations relating to employment of minority groups -- rules, regulations and orders promulgated by Deputy Mayor-City Administrator of City of New York, requiring bidder on city contract to provide minimum percentage of minority man-hours of work in each specified trade and to agree that same percentages would apply to all its other projects in city, are legislative in nature, beyond scope of authority vested in him, and unconstitutional -- action attacking their constitutionality, brought as article 78 proceeding, is treated as action for declaratory judgment, not barred by four-month period of limitation -- assuming some petitioners lack standing to sue, others have standing, and, in any event, case involves public interest -- no showing made of any need to stay present determination pending possible settlement of related actions in Federal court.

1. Rules, regulations and orders promulgated by the Deputy Mayor-City Administrator of the City of New York, requiring a bidder on a city contract to provide a minimum percentage of minority man-hours of work in each specified building and construction trade, and compelling the contractor to agree that the [***2] same percentages would apply to all the contractor's other projects in the city, whether Federal, State or private, during the life of the city contract involved, are legislative in nature, clearly beyond the scope of the authority vested in him, and unconstitutional.

2. The present action attacks the constitutionality and validity of those rules, regulations and orders, but was brought as an article 78 proceeding. It is treated as an action for a declaratory judgment, which is the appropriate remedy under the circumstances, and, as such, the four-month period of limitation applicable to article 78 proceedings is no bar to it.

3. Assuming that some of the petitioners lack standing to sue because they are not contractors who have submitted bids on city projects, others clearly have standing. In any event, in cases, such as this, involving the public interest, a proceeding directed against public officials may be instituted without a showing that the petitioner is an aggrieved party.

4. There has been no showing made to the court's satisfaction of any need to stay its present determination pending a possible settlement of related actions pending in the Federal court.

 

1434 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gotbaum v. Sugarman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


78 Misc. 2d 827; 358 N.Y.S.2d 635; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1504



July 31, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, this court finds that petitioners have failed to state a cause of action, and the respondents' motion to dismiss the petition is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a union official, civil service employees, and persons on civil service eligibility, brought an action to enjoin the implementation of the Work Relief Employment Program (WREP), a demonstration project planned and administered by respondent, city human rights administrator.


OVERVIEW: The WREP project provided for the employment of recipients of home relief by requiring their assignment to various city governmental agencies. Pursuant to the plan, a number of WREP titles had been created which were comparable, so far as job specifications and qualifications were concerned, with a number of positions in the competitive class of civil service. Petitioners asserted in their claim that the WREP program was a subterfuge to evade the constitutional requirements of competitive civil service examinations. The court stated the principle that a legislative policy which reflected a humane concern for developing meaningful work experience for relief recipients and which provided incentives for employment rather than for subsistence on a dole, was not to be discarded unless in clear conflict with constitutional imperatives. The court concluded that the WREP program did not conflict with the New York Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court granted respondent's motion to dismiss petitioner's cause of action to enjoin the implementation of the Work Relief Employment Program.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- work relief employment program -- social services -- Work Relief Employment Program does not unconstitutionally evade competitive civil service examinations in assigning home relief recipients to public agency employment, since employment is temporary, without tenure or civil service status and will not reduce budget for regular civil service positions -- executive director of district council of principal civil service union, civil service employees and persons on eligible lists have standing to sue.

1. The Work Relief Employment Program (WREP) implementing chapter 603 of the Laws of 1972 does not unconstitutionally evade the requirements of competitive civil service examinations in assigning recipients of home relief to employment in public agencies. WREP participants are untenured, temporary employees, ineligible for promotional examination and without civil service status, and there is no true employer-employee status, since their wages come from relief funds. There is no indication that the budgetary [***2] allocation for regular civil service positions will be reduced. The humane legislative policy of developing employment incentives for relief recipients should not be discarded unless in clear conflict with constitutional imperatives. Accordingly, the petition to enjoin the implementation of the WREP program is denied.

2. The executive director of district council of the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees, which is the principal civil service union in New York City, civil service employees and persons on eligible lists have standing to sue.

Muncie Constr. Corp. v. Mid--Atlantic Development Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


79 Misc. 2d 615; 360 N.Y.S.2d 543; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1719



July 29, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] That branch of the motion seeking to dismiss the "Eighth", "Ninth" and "Tenth" causes of action of the amended complaint is granted. Since the first seven causes of action of the amended complaint are totally dependent upon the plaintiff being sustained in the last three causes of action of the amended complaint, and those having been found to be wanting in sustainable allegations, the entire complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner brought a claim against defendants, development corporation, city, and the mayor, seeking a judicial declaration that an ordinance enacted by the Common Council be declared null and void as being unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A city ordinance directed that a new road be constructed and that the cost of the improvement was to be assessed against the realty to be benefited, which included the lands owned by plaintiff. Plaintiff filed a claim seeking a judicial declaration that the ordinance was unconstitutional because it created a special assessment upon his property to help defray the cost of the improvement and provided no notice or opportunity to be heard with respect to the levy of that special assessment. The court held that no notice or opportunity to be heard was constitutionally required to be given by a municipality until such time as the improvement had been completed and the special assessment to defray the cost of the improvement had been levied.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of defendants and dismissed plaintiff's cause of action seeking a judicial determination that the city ordinance was null and void.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- street assessments -- City of Albany's ordinances directing that new road be constructed and that cost of such improvement be assessed against realty to be benefited, valid.

The Common Council of the City of Albany properly adopted ordinances directing that a new road be constructed and that the cost of such improvement be assessed against plaintiff's realty, among other properties to be benefited thereby, and accepting a fee conveyance from others of certain lands for such improvement (L. 1883, ch. 298, tit. IX, § 29; L. 1929, ch. 523, as amd. by L. 1943, ch. 710). The 30-day Statute of Limitations (set forth in L. 1929, ch. 523, § 2), was validly applied to plaintiff. Plaintiff's amended complaint is dismissed.

 

1436 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Stewart v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Wyoming County


78 Misc. 2d 728; 358 N.Y.S.2d 305; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1482



July 25, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly it is ordered, that petitioner be released from custody forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court considered whether petitioner juvenile should have been released after his reformatory sentence had been abolished by 1974 N.Y. Laws 652 and he had been sentenced to prison.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile was convicted of misdemeanor sexual abuse, and the maximum sentence for this offense was one year in prison. However, because the juvenile was a youth, he was given a reformatory sentence having a maximum of four years. Subsequently, reformatory sentences were abolished by 1974 N.Y. Laws 652, and the juvenile was placed with the general prison population. The juvenile petitioned for his release from prison. The court held that any further imprisonment of the juvenile under N.Y. Penal Law § 75 was unconstitutional as not affording him equal treatment under the law. The juvenile was not a felon and was a misdemeanant who had already served more than the year in prison that he could have received as an adult. He had long since ceased to receive the separate treatment he should have had as a reformatory prisoner and was being treated identically with all of his older fellow inmates.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the release of the juvenile from prison.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentences -- reformatory sentence -- in view of abolition of reformatory sentences in 1974 and admission by State that inmates serving such sentences were treated in same manner as other inmates and that such sentences unfairly treated young adults more harshly than adults, further imprisonment of youth convicted in 1972 of misdemeanor punishable by prison sentence of one year and given reformatory sentence, having maximum term of four years, is unconstitutional.

Although the imposition of a reformatory sentence, having a maximum term of four years, upon a youth between 16 and 21 years of age who had been convicted in 1972 of a misdemeanor punishable by a prison sentence of one year was constitutionally permissible prior to 1974, in view of the abolition of reformatory sentences in that year, and the admission by the State that inmates serving such sentences were treated in the same manner as other inmates and that such sentences unfairly treated young adults more harshly than adults, further imprisonment of the youth under his reformatory sentence is unconstitutional.

 

1437 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mazzie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


78 Misc. 2d 1014; 358 N.Y.S.2d 307; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1548



July 25, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who entered a plea of guilty to the crime of attempted bribery, challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06 known as the Rockefeller Law, which provided for mandatory minimum State prison sentences for any one convicted of a felony in New York who, within ten years of the present offense, had been convicted of a felony in New York or any other jurisdiction. Defendant entered a plea of guilty to the crime of attempted bribery, a class E felony, and conceded that he had been previously convicted in the United States District Court for the Southern District of New York of a felonious conspiracy to violate the Federal narcotics laws. The court held that the application of the Rockefeller Law violated New York Const. art. III, §§ 1, 16 in that it improperly delegated legislative power to the legislatures of other jurisdictions and incorporated by reference the laws of those jurisdictions which would not be felonies in New York. Additionally, the court held that the statute violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const., art. I, § 11.


OUTCOME: The court ruled against the state and sustained defendant's constitutional challenge to the sentencing statute, under which he was due to be sentenced for the crime of attempted bribery.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- sentence -- second-felony offenders -- mandatory second-felony offender sentence is unconstitutional when prior felony (Federal, in this case) would merely have been misdemeanor under New York law.

The Penal Law ( § 70.06, subd. 1, par. [b], cl. [i]) now requires mandatory minimum State prison sentences for anyone convicted of a felony in New York who, within 10 years of the present offense, was convicted in New York of a felony "or in any other jurisdiction of an offense for which a sentence to a term of imprisonment in excess of one year * * * was authorized irrespective of whether such sentence was imposed". In 1973 defendant allegedly committed attempted bribery, a class E felony, to which he has now pleaded guilty. The presentence report reveals that, prior thereto, he was convicted in the United States District Court of a felonious conspiracy to violate the Federal narcotics laws (U. S. Code, tit. 21, former §§ 173, 174) for which he was incarcerated for seven years. However, the New York equivalent of his Federal conviction would have been merely the misdemeanor of conspiracy (former Penal Law, § 580). As applied to this defendant, our new mandatory [***2] minimum State prison sentence statute is unconstitutional (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 11; art. III, §§ 1, 16).

In re Estate of Hoard


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Westchester County


78 Misc. 2d 1083; 358 N.Y.S.2d 625; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1564



July 24, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother of an infant decedent brought an application for letters of administration to allow her to become administrator of the infant's estate. Respondent father opposed the application and requested that the letters be issued to him, or in the alternative to both parties.


OVERVIEW: The parties lived together but were not married. After the infant was born, he lived with the parties until the father left four years later. The mother then initiated a filiation proceeding, and the family court issued an order declared the father's paternity. The mother then petitioned the court for letters of administration, alleging that the father was barred from receiving letters under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2, which provided that the father of an illegitimate child was not entitled to letters unless paternity was established in a proceeding brought within two years of the infant's birth. The father argued that § 4-1.2 was unconstitutional based on the facts because it deprived him, as an acknowledged father, of equal protection. The court held that the father was entitled to letters, along with the mother, because application specifically of N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2(a)(2)(b) to the father's case would be a denial of his constitutional rights as the proven father. Having found that both parties were equally entitled to letters, the court in its discretion decided to grant limited letters to the mother upon her duly qualifying.


OUTCOME: The court held that the mother and the father were equally entitled to letters of administration under state law. In its discretion, the court decided to grant limited letters to the mother upon her duly qualifying.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children born out of wedlock -- right of declared father to receive letters of administration -- application of EPTL 4-1.2, which allows father of illegitimate decedent to receive letters of administration provided order of filiation declaring paternity is made in proceeding instituted within two years of child's birth, to respondent who was declared father in filiation proceeding brought four years after decedent's birth, is unconstitutional.

Four years after decedent, an infant, was born, his unmarried parents were parties to a filiation proceeding in which respondent was declared to be the father of decedent and was directed to pay funeral expenses if the child should die prior to the age of 21. Upon a petition for letters of administration, although both parties are equally entitled, the court in its discretion grants limited letters to petitioner upon her duly qualifying. The application of EPTL 4-1.2 which allows a father of an illegitimate decedent to receive letters of administration provided an order of filiation declaring paternity is made in a proceeding instituted "within two years of the child's birth", to the particular facts would constitute a denial of the [***2] constitutional rights of the father. The statute sets up an invidious discrimination between legitimates and illegitimates by requiring that an order of filiation be entered before an illegitimate or its father may be treated as a distributee of the other.

Dallas v. Lavine


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


79 Misc. 2d 395; 358 N.Y.S.2d 297; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1671



July 23, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner alien, a Bahamian national illegally in the country, brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against respondent commissioners that challenged their denial of medical assistance to her and sought a declaratory judgment, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3001, that the denial of medical assistance to needy applicants on grounds of nonresidence, citizenship, or visa status was in violation of law.


OVERVIEW: The alien entered the country on a tourist visa, overstayed her visa, illegally obtained employment, and became ill. Being indigent, she claimed public medical assistance but the commissioners denied her request. The alien brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against the commissioners that challenged their denial of assistance and sought a declaratory judgment. The court reversed the denial of benefits and held that: (1) under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 363 et seq., prior to its 1974 amendment, provided that each public welfare district was responsible for furnishing medical assistance to eligible persons found in its territory who were temporarily in the state; (2) the statute effectively made New York an international relief agency for any needy persons found in the state; and (3) although the state's prospects for federal reimbursement were questionable, there was no prohibition against the state awarding benefits on its own.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment for the alien against the commissioners, which had denied medical assistance benefits to the alien. The court denied the alien's request for a declaratory judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social services -- aliens -- alien whose tourist visa had expired was nevertheless entitled to Medicaid; however, since June 7, 1974 (Social Services Law, § 131-k), such illegal residents may obtain only temporary relief.

The Social Services Law ( § 131-k, eff. June 7, 1974) provides that "Any inconsistent provisions * * * notwithstanding, an alien who is unlawfully residing in the United States * * * shall not be eligible for aid to dependent children, home relief or medical assistance, except for a temporary period of thirty days". However, this does not apply to such an alien -- in this case, a Bahamian citizen whose tourist visa had expired -- who became ill and was hospitalized in 1973. Determinations by the State and County Commissioners of Social Services, denying Medicaid to her, are reversed by the court.

In re Estate of Flores


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Monroe County


78 Misc. 2d 481; 357 N.Y.S.2d 825; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1429



July 18, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county public administrator brought a proceeding to determine the distribution of a wrongful death settlement, which constituted the decedent's entire estate. The guardian ad litem of the decedent's illegitimate children (first guardian) sought a share of the proceeds for such children, while the guardian ad litem for the decedent's legitimate children (second guardian) alleged that only they were entitled to share in the proceeds.


OVERVIEW: The decedent had several legitimate children with his wife prior to their divorce. He later lived with two other women, and had illegitimate children with both. After the decedent's death, his estate brought a wrongful death action and later received settlement proceeds. The administrator brought a proceeding to determine the proper distribution of the settlement proceeds, which constituted the entire estate. The first guardian argued that the illegitimate children were entitled to a proportionate share of the proceeds, while the second guardian contended that only the legitimate children were entitled to share in the proceeds. The court held that based on federal and state law, both the legitimate and illegitimate children had a right to the wrongful death proceeds; however, such entitlement by both groups did not mean that each child shared equally. Under state law, each child's share was to be determined based on the period during which he or she might have reasonably looked to the decedent for support.


OUTCOME: The court held that both the legitimate and the illegitimate children were entitled to share in the wrongful death proceeds; each child's share was to be determined based on a certain formula in accordance with state law.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children born out of wedlock -- right to wrongful death proceeds -- illegitimate children are not barred from share in wrongful death proceeds, such share to be based upon period they might reasonably have looked to decedent for support.

Illegitimate children of a decedent are not barred by EPTL 4-1.2 (subd. [a], pars. [2], [3]) from taking a share in wrongful death proceeds, such share to be based upon the period they might reasonably have looked to the decedent for support, although the distribution may diminish the shares of legitimate children. The equal right of all children to a share does not mean that all share equally.

 

1441 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Podiatry Soc. of New York v. Regents of University of N. Y.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


78 Misc. 2d 731; 358 N.Y.S.2d 276; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1484



July 17, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly respondents' objections in point of law are sustained, and the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was a proceeding brought by petitioner school, which sought a judgment to declare N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 65.6 null and void, to enjoin respondents, commissioner of education, board of regents, and board of education, from asserting supervision over the school's courses of instruction for continuing education, and to declare that the school's continuing education courses were valid.


OVERVIEW: The school sought a declaratory judgment that respondents' regulation, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 65.6, was null and void. The school argued that the legislature intended that it be allowed to independently establish the necessary criteria for programs conducted in and for continuing education in the podiatry profession. The school contended that N.Y. Educ. Law § 7004(9) did not require their program to be approved by respondents. Respondents argued that all the continuing education programs for the podiatry profession were subject to their approval. The court agreed and dismissed the school's petition. The court concluded that § 7004(9) clearly required that respondents approve all continuing education programs. The court determined that if the school's interpretation were accepted the statute would have been an unconstitutional delegation of governmental powers.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the school's petition challenging respondents' control over continuing education.


HEADNOTES

Physicians and surgeons -- podiatrists -- approval of continuing education programs -- section 7004 (subd. [9]) of Education Law, requiring continuing education in practice of podiatry, authorizes Department of Education to assert supervisory jurisdiction over continuing education programs of Podiatry Society of State of New York as well as over equivalent programs, and, accordingly, that society's programs are subject to approval of State Board of Podiatry in accordance with stated regulations of Commissioner of Education.

1. Section 7004 (subd. [9]) of the Education Law, which requires continuing education in the practice of podiatry and the presentation by an applicant for re-registration of evidence that he "attended the education programs conducted by the podiatry society of the state of New York or the equivalent of such educational programs as approved by the state board for podiatry in accordance with the commissioner's regulations", authorizes the Department of Education to assert supervisory jurisdiction over the continuing education programs of the Podiatry [***2] Society of the State of New York as well as over equivalent programs. Accordingly, that society's programs are subject to the approval of the State Board of Podiatry in accordance with stated regulations of the Commissioner of Education.

2. Notwithstanding the doctrine of the "last antecedent", the general words "as approved by the state board for podiatry in accordance with the commissioner's regulations" refer to and modify both preceding antecedents and not merely the last one. Any other interpretation would give to private professional societies the power, vested by statute in the State's education authorities, to establish standards for the professions, and would result in an unconstitutional delegation of governmental power to a private corporation.

 

1442 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re R.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


79 Misc. 2d 339; 357 N.Y.S.2d 1001; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1662



July 17, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motions to dismiss petitions are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Family Court of New York, New York County, considered truancy petitions filed against respondent children. The children filed a motion to dismiss the petitions.


OVERVIEW: The instant school truancy cases, consolidated because of common issues of law, amounted to an attack on New York's truancy laws, N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 3205(1)(a); 3210(1)(a), (2)(b). The truancy provisions and the procedure for enforcing them were attacked through motions to dismiss, but the court found the laws constitutional and held that the legislature could reasonably have believed that New York's compulsory education laws, as applied by the courts, aided the class of individuals they were intended to benefit. They therefore were a constitutional exercise of the state's parens patriae power. The use of the certified attendance transcripts was also allowed as a hearsay exception for evidence with such reliable and probative value. Thus, the court denied the children's motions to dismiss the truancy petitions.


OUTCOME: The court denied the children's motions to dismiss because it found New York's truancy laws constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- truants -- compulsory education law validly and constitutionally makes certified transcript of record of child's absences competent and presumptive evidence -- only on dispositional hearing may evidence or cross-examination be received as to lack of psychological counselling or inadequacy of school or class.

1. The Education Law ( § 3211, subd. 2) validly and constitutionally provides that "A duly certified transcript of the record of attendance and absence of a child which has been kept, as provided in this section, shall be accepted as presumptive evidence of the attendance of such child in any proceeding brought under the provisions of" the compulsory education law. The certified transcript is "competent" evidence (cf. Family Ct. Act, § 744). The compulsory education law is valid and constitutional, and the Family Court has jurisdiction (Education Law, § 3232; Family Ct. Act, § 712).

2. Evidence, or cross-examination, designed to show that the child has not been offered any psychological counselling, or that the school or class is wholly unsuitable, is irrelevant on the fact-finding hearing but will be relevant [***2] on the dispositional hearing (Family Ct. Act, §§ 742, 743, 745).

People v. Weiss


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Suffolk County


78 Misc. 2d 792; 358 N.Y.S.2d 267; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1495



July 9, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the foregoing, it is the decision of this court that defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment is granted to the extent that a Clayton hearing shall be held to determine whether or not the interests of justice will be served by such dismissal, such hearing to be held in Part I of the County Court on the 7th day of August, 1974, and the motion is denied in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was defendant's motion to dismiss the fifth count of the indictment, which accused him of the criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was accused by indictment with various crimes associated with a controlled substance, including the criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the count that accused him of the criminal sale of a controlled substance and argued that the mandatory minimum penalty was unconstitutional. In the alternative, defendant argued for dismissal of the count in the interest of justice. A psychiatrist, who examined defendant, stated in her affidavit, that he was emotionally disturbed and that the necessary treatment would be unavailable in a penal institution. The court granted defendant's motion to the extent that it ordered a hearing to determine whether or not the interests of justice would be served by dismissal of the count. The court found that the mandatory minimum period of incarceration of one year did not constitute cruel or unusual punishment. The court found that a hearing was required to determine whether the interests of justice would be served by a dismissal because the State had not conceded to the facts in defendant's motion and had not refuted the facts with unquestionable documentary evidence.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment to the extent that it ordered a hearing on whether or not the interests of justice would be served by a dismissal.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- criminal sale of controlled substance -- mandatory prison sentence of section 70.00 of Penal Law is not cruel and unusual punishment as to defendant allegedly in need of care not available in penal institution -- hearing shall be held to determine if interests of justice will be served by dismissal of indictment, since such dismissal cannot be granted without hearing or denied without documentary evidence refuting defendant's allegations.

1. The mandatory minimum prison sentence imposed by section 70.00 of the Penal Law for the crime of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree is not unconstitutional with respect to the defendant, who, it is alleged, is emotionally disturbed, suicidal and in need of care not available in a penal institution.  [***2] Such minimum term does not constitute cruel or unusual punishment.

2. A hearing will be held to determine if the interests of justice will be served by dismissal of the indictment under CPL 210.45. The motion for such a dismissal under that section may neither be granted without a hearing nor denied without unquestionable documentary evidence refuting the defendant's allegations.
