People v. Passero


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


78 Misc. 2d 548; 357 N.Y.S.2d 677; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1444



July 8, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the orders of the Justice Courts of the Town of Brighton and the Town of Henrietta (New York), which entered judgments on the juries' verdicts that found defendant guilty of the crimes of driving while intoxicated. In the second trial defendant was also convicted of a failure to keep right.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted in each of two trials for driving while intoxicated. In each case, defendant claimed that because his jury trial was held at night, he was denied equal protection of the law as guaranteed by the Fourteenth Amendment and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. In one case, defendant objected to the continuation of his trial at a late hour. The court held that a juror's affidavit was admissible to corroborate that at least one of the jurors was exhausted when he voted to convict. The court ruled that defendant's right to trial by jury was so impaired as to amount to a constitutional violation. In the other case, the court held that: (1) defendant's guilt was fully supported by the evidence; (2) it was not error to permit one of the civilian witnesses to express an opinion as to the condition of defendant; and (3) the prosecutor's comment regarding defendant's "refusal" to take either a breathalyzer or a blood test was not so prejudicial as to deprive the defendant of a fair trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed one of defendant's convictions for driving while intoxicated. The court affirmed one of defendant's convictions for driving while intoxicated.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- jury trial -- in Justice Court, trial began at 8:00 p.m., and exhausted jury at 2:50 a.m. found defendant guilty; on appeal, County Court set verdict aside -- in different trial, for driving while intoxicated, defendant's refusal to take either breathalyzer test or blood test was properly made subject of cross-examination and summation.

1. A criminal trial in a Town Justice Court, for driving while intoxicated, began at 8:00 p.m., and the case was submitted to the jury at 1:30 a.m., and at 2:50 a.m. the jury brought in a verdict of guilty. The foreman now avers that he was so tired by such long, late hours that he acceded to the verdict of guilty. Defendant's constitutional right of trial by jury was impaired by such a late night trial. On appeal to the County Court, the verdict is set aside and a new trial is granted.

2. In another, different, criminal jury trial in a Town Justice Court, on a charge of driving while intoxicated, (a) civilian witnesses properly testified that defendant appeared to be intoxicated, (b) defendant's refusal to take either a breathalyzer test or a blood test was properly made the subject of cross-examination and summation, (c) defendant [***2] was properly cross-examined as to a prior incident of intoxication, and (d) the Trial Justice imposed reasonable limitations on the cross-examination of the prosecution's witnesses. The judgment of conviction in this case is affirmed.

New York State School Bus Operators Asso. v. County of Nassau


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


79 Misc. 2d 352; 357 N.Y.S.2d 641; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1664



June 28, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment is denied. Defendants' motions for summary judgment are granted. The complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, Nassau County, New York and New York Metropolitan Suburban Bus Authority (MSBA), and plaintiff bus companies filed motions for summary judgment in an action arising from the county's acquisition by condemnation of the buses and facilities of the bus companies.


OVERVIEW: Local Law No. 14 of Nassau County, N.Y. (1972) (local law) authorized the county to acquire, own, and operate public transportation facilities within its boundaries. Pursuant to the local law, the county acquired by condemnation the buses and facilities of the bus companies. The county agreed to allow MSBA to lease and operate the buses and facilities it had acquired. The bus companies filed an action claiming that the local law was unconstitutional. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment. The court granted defendants' motion and denied the bus companies' motion. The court determined that the bus companies had standing because the local law substantially affected their economic interests. The court held that the local law was not unconstitutional because buses and routes were "transit" and "mass transportation" within the meanings of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(a) and N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 119-r(1), and the purchase and operation of bus lines were within the county's statutory authority. The court held that the county's extension of service to school bussing was permissible, finding that school busing was within the definitions of "mass" and "commuter" transportation.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motion for summary judgment. The court denied the bus companies' motion for summary judgment in an action arising from the county's acquisition by condemnation of the buses and facilities of the bus companies.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- Metropolitan Suburban Bus Authority -- MSBA, subsidiary of MTA, may operate school busses and charter busses within metropolitan commuter transportation district.

The Metropolitan Suburban Bus Authority, a subsidiary of the Metropolitan Transportation Authority, may operate busses and bus lines, not only over street routes in Nassau County, but as school busses and as charter transportation busses within the metropolitan commuter transportation district. (Agreement between Nassau County and MSBA dated Jan. 15, 1973, § 2; Local Laws, No. 14 of 1972 of County of Nassau; General Municipal Law, art. 5-I; Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10, subd. 1, par. [ii], cl. a, subcl. [7]; N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 2, subd. [c], par. [7]; Public Authorities Law, § 1266, subds. 1, 2, 5; § 1264; § 1261, subd. 9; cf. Transportation Law, § 2, subd. 22, and § 142, subd. 17, par. [1].)

People v. Attica Bros.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Additional Special and Trial Term, Erie County


79 Misc. 2d 492; 359 N.Y.S.2d 699; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1693



June 27, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion to strike the jury pool on the grounds that it discriminated against black persons, women, young persons, and poor persons.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were indicted in Wyoming County and then their cases were transferred to Erie County for further proceedings and trial. Defendants filed a motion to strike the jury pool. The court concluded there was discrimination against students and women but not against blacks and young people. Under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 270.10, there was a procedure for challenging the jury panel at the time of trial but there was no statutory procedure for challenging the jury pool. The court concluded that the serious constitutional question raised about the composition of the jury pool justified a hearing on the motion. The court found that the Commissioners of Jurors use of the voters' registry as the source of names for the jury pool was proper under N.Y. Jud. Law § 658, which provided the voters' registry list as one of the sources for prospective jurors. The court found no discrimination in the use of a permanent pool of jurors. The court found that mere mathematical disparities without other evidence did not meet defendants' burden to prove intentional and systematic discrimination of blacks or young people. The court found that the practice of excluding students and women was discriminatory.


OUTCOME: The court granted in part the motion to strike the jury pool and ordered that the jury pool be separated back to the start of the year when the discriminatory practices against women and students had stopped.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Jury -- selection -- after hearing on defendants' challenge to entire jury pool (cf. CPL 270.10), county's Commissioner of Jurors is directed to use separate pool of jurors who qualified since 1974, when discriminatory practices in favor of students and women were discontinued by said commissioner.

1. On defendants' challenge to the entire jury pool of Erie County (cf. CPL 270.10), the court has conducted an evidentiary hearing. In the extensive card files of the Erie County Commissioner of Jurors, were 13 cards marked "negro" or "col"; but these 13 were all in the "qualified" file, not in the "disqualified" file; besides, these 13 cards were all made prior to the year 1952, and the evidence is that any such discriminatory practice has been discontinued since 1952. The commissioner's present practice of excusing operators of one-man businesses, dairy farmers and the like, when done on a case-by-case basis, seems proper under section 678-a of the Judiciary Law.

2. However, up to January 1, 1974, there was an improper practice of exempting, on grounds of hardship, persons who stated in their questionnaires that they were students. Also, up to January 1, 1974, there was an [***2] improper practice of selecting far fewer women than men for the pool, because it was found that so many women claimed their statutory exemption as women ( Judiciary Law, § 665, subd. 7). These discriminatory practices by the commissioner's staff have been discontinued since January 1, 1974. The commissioner's staff uses a sequential numbering system, and hence it is simple to make up a separate jury pool consisting of those persons who have been marked qualified since January 1, 1974. The court directs the Commissioner of Jurors to make up such a separate pool. ( Judiciary Law, §§ 658, 662; Rules of App. Div., 4th Dept. [22 NYCRR 1025.2, 1025.6].)

 

1447 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. R.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


78 Misc. 2d 616; 356 N.Y.S.2d 1006; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1459



June 26, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a youth otherwise eligible for youthful offender treatment, challenged the provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 720.10(2)(a), a provision that precluded the availability of youthful offender treatment to otherwise eligible youths who had been indicted for class A felonies.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted, as that term was defined in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 1.20(3), 200.10, for the commission of a class A felony. Prior to conviction, defendant applied for youthful offender treatment. The State contended that defendant was ineligible for youthful offender adjudication because of the charges. The court held that: (1) the statutory restriction was violative of due process requirements because it gave conclusive weight to the untested allegations of an indictment; and (2) the restriction violated the equal protection clauses of both the state and federal constitutions because it irrationally discriminated against those youths who had been charged with class A felonies but were later convicted of lesser felonies. The court reasoned that there was no rational basis for treating differently youths who have been convicted of the same offense merely because one of them had originally been charged with a higher grade of offense and that a restriction or classification based upon a charge made in an indictment rather than a charge proven in court was capricious and irrational.


OUTCOME: The court directed that upon the guilty plea to the indictment, the conviction was vacated and a youthful offender adjudication was substituted.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- availability of youthful offender treatment -- CPL 720.10 (subd. 2, par. [a]), which provides that youthful offender treatment is not available to otherwise eligible youth who has been "indicted" for class A felony, is unconstitutional.

 CPL 720.10 (subd. 2, par. [a]), which provides that youthful offender treatment is not available to an otherwise eligible youth who has been "indicted" for a class A felony, is unconstitutional. In using the grade of offense charged, as opposed to the grade of offense of which the youth is ultimately found guilty, it gives conclusive weight to the untested allegations of an indictment, in violation of the due process clauses of the State and Federal Constitutions, and, in violation of the equal protection clauses thereof, it irrationally discriminates against those youths charged with class A felonies but convicted of lesser crimes.

People v. Ellison


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Westchester County


78 Misc. 2d 652; 357 N.Y.S.2d 773; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1466



June 20, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion to dismiss is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment, which charged him with selling a controlled substance in the third degree and criminal possession of a controlled substance in the third degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with selling a controlled substance and criminal possession of a controlled substance. The charges arose from his sale of $ 11 worth of methadone and he faced a possible sentence of life imprisonment. The court previously denied defendant's omnibus motion. Defendant then filed a motion to dismiss the indictment and argued that the imposition of severe incarceration under the New Drug Law constituted cruel and inhuman treatment in violation of the Fifth, Eight, and Fourteenth Amendments to the United States Constitution. The court determined that while defendant's constitutional arguments should have been included in his omnibus motion, it would in its discretion consider the merits of his motion. The court found that while neither defendant nor the State specified the section of the law to which the motion was addressed, it was clear that the dealt with N.Y. Penal Law §§ 220.16, 220.39 and 70.00(2)(a). The court denied defendant's motion. Based on the policy that a court of first instance should hesitate to determine that a statute was unconstitutional, the court concluded that the statutes were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- drug law -- motion to dismiss indictment charging crimes involving drugs, made on ground that provisions of 1973 drug law, for severe sentences for such crimes, constitute cruel and inhuman treatment and violates Fifth, Eighth and Fourteenth Amendments to United States Constitution, is denied -- sections under attack are constitutional -- fact that constitutional issue was not raised on prior motion to dismiss might have resulted in summary denial of present motion, but court, in discretion given to it, disposes of motion on merits.

1. A defendant charged with criminally selling and possessing a controlled substance, who, under provisions contained in what is referred to as the "New Drug Law", faces a possible sentence of life imprisonment for an alleged sale of $ 11 worth of methadone, moves to dismiss the indictment on the ground that the provisions of the law, specifically chapters 276 and 278 of the Laws of 1973, for severe sentences of incarceration for crimes involving drugs, constitute cruel and inhuman treatment and violate the Fifth, Eighth and Fourteenth Amendments to the United States Constitution.  [***2] His motion is denied on the merits. The sections under attack are constitutional.

2. The fact that this defendant made a prior motion to dismiss the indictment and did not raise the constitutional issue at that time might have resulted in the summary denial of his present motion under CPL 210.20 (subd. 3), which provides that, on such a motion, a defendant should raise every ground on which he intends to rely or face a summary denial of a subsequent motion, but that statute also gives the court discretion to dispose of the subsequent motion on the merits, and, in view of the importance of the issue now raised, it should not be disposed of on a technicality.

Light v. MacKenzie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Tompkins County


78 Misc. 2d 315; 356 N.Y.S.2d 991; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1391



June 17, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought an order annulling a special election by respondent, Slaterville Board of Fire Commissioners (New York).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner contended that the Board's deviation from the property owning limitations under the Town Law of Caroline, New York, § 175(2) required annulment of a resolution authorizing the issuance of bonds by the district for the purchase of a fire truck. The Board's action in opening the referendum to all resident electors despite the statutory restriction to property owning electors was based upon its belief that the restriction was unconstitutional. The court held that non-property owners in the district had an interest in the referendum equal to that of the property owners. The court stated that petitioner failed to show a compelling State interest requiring limiting the franchise to property-owning electors.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of the Board and dismissed petitioner's cause of action to annul the special election.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- fire district -- restriction of fire district referendum franchise to real property owners is unconstitutional, since benefits of fire protection extend to all residents of district -- typographical error incorrectly itemizing amounts of individual bonds while correctly listing total limitation is innocuous.

1. Subdivision 2 of section 175 of the Town Law, insofar as it confines the right to vote in fire district referendums to real property owners, is unconstitutional. The benefits of fire district protection are not confined to a limited class, but, because of the mobility of fire fighting equipment, are extended to all residents of the district. There is no compelling State interest in limiting the franchise. Petition to annul a special election for noncompliance with real property restrictions is accordingly dismissed.

2. It was an innocuous typographical error for the notice of resolution of the bond issue to correctly list the total limitation while incorrectly stating the amounts of individual bonds.

Antinore v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


79 Misc. 2d 8; 356 N.Y.S.2d 794; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1575; 87 L.R.R.M. 3067



June 12, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought a declaratory judgment with respect to his statutory and contractual rights with respect to his suspension due to felony charges initiated against him and the requirement embodied in his collective bargaining agreement requiring mandatory arbitration.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff public employee (employee) was suspended from his employment following an indictment charging him with felonious acts. Defendant employer warned the employee that he would be permanently terminated unless he invoked the arbitration procedures available to him in accordance with the collective bargaining agreement. The employee sought a declaratory judgment setting forth a judicial clarification of his statutory and contractual rights and his rights to judicial review. The employee instituted his action prior to the commencement of his arbitration hearing. Defendant employer moved for summary judgment. The court denied the employer's motion for summary judgment. Additionally, the court held the employee was entitled to summary judgment upon his action for declaratory judgment. Furthermore, the court held the agreement entered into by the employee's union was unconstitutional and denied the employee due process. The court reasoned the arbitration procedure set forth in the collective bargaining agreement was deficient. Moreover, the court reasoned the employee did not waive his constitutional or statutory rights and thus was entitled to a declaratory judgment.


OUTCOME: The court denied the state's motion for summary judgment in a public employee's action requesting a declaratory judgment with respect to a clarification of his statutory and contractual rights under a collective bargaining agreement requiring arbitration proceedings, following a work-related suspension.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil service -- disciplinary proceedings -- State civil service employee who is faced with discipline and possible discharge cannot constitutionally be relegated to disciplinary procedures set forth in collective bargaining agreement between State and CSEA; at his option, he may insist on disciplinary procedures and safeguards set forth in Civil Service Law ( §§ 75, 76).

The disciplinary procedures and safeguards set forth in sections 75 and 76 of the Civil Service Law cannot constitutionally be "replaced" (Civil Service Law, § 76, subd. 4) by the provisions and procedures for discipline contained in article 33 of a collective bargaining agreement between the Civil Service Employees' Association and the State of New York. The agreed upon provisions are lacking in constitutional due process and equal protection of the laws and are not binding upon State civil service employees. Plaintiff employee, who is faced with discipline and possible discharge from his position, has the choice either of proceeding under article 33 of the collective bargaining agreement or of insisting upon proceeding under sections 75 and 76 of the Civil Service Law.

People v. Simmons


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Kings County


79 Misc. 2d 249; 357 N.Y.S.2d 362; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1640



June 11, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with larceny, prostitution, and criminal impersonation in connection with his arrest while he was dressed as a woman and soliciting.


OVERVIEW: While defendant was dressed as a woman, he solicited the victim, who accused defendant of stealing money from his pocket. When defendant was discovered to be a man, he was charged with criminal impersonation in violation N.Y. Penal Law § 190.25(1) for representing himself to be a woman who did offer to engage in a sexual act in return for money. The court dismissed the criminal impersonation charge because criminal impersonation involved the assumption of a particular person's identity and did not include cross dressing in general.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the charge of criminal impersonation against defendant, who was a cross dresser.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- criminal impersonation -- defendant, dressed as woman, who approached car and asked complainant if he "wanted some action" whereby defendant entered car, and after parking car, complainant discovered that he was missing approximately $ 80, is held for trial only on charges of larceny and prostitution; prohibition against criminal impersonation (Penal Law, § 190.25, subd. 1) does not condemn cross dressing.

Wearing a woman's wig, dress, makeup and shoes, defendant, a male, approached complainant's car and asked if he "wanted some action". After being invited into the car, defendant offered complainant $ 10 "to take care" of him. Complainant then drove to a dead-end street, parked, and then discovered he was missing approximately $ 80. Complainant then had defendant arrested; both complainant and the police officer believed at the time of the arrest that defendant was a woman. While there is sufficient evidence to require holding defendant for trial on charges of larceny and prostitution, defendant cannot be held to answer for the charge of criminal impersonation. The meaning of subdivision 1 of section 190.25 of the Penal Law, which reads: "A person is guilty [***2] of criminal impersonation when he impersonates another and does an act in such assumed character with intent to obtain a benefit or to injure or defraud another", does not condemn cross dressing.

Eber v. Board of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


80 Misc. 2d 334; 362 N.Y.S.2d 304; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1892



June 11, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Wherefore, it is ordered that the petitioner's application is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voter, pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law § 330, sought a declaration of her right to vote in a special election by absentee ballot.


OVERVIEW: The voter commenced a special proceeding to have her right to vote in a special election by absentee ballot declared. The voter alleged that she would be on vacation on the day of the election and that she was told that absentee ballots to people on vacation were not authorized by N.Y. Elec. Law § 117. She argued that the denial of the absentee ballot violated her right to suffrage and deprived her of her rights to equal protection. The court denied her application and determined that the restriction which was not so severe as to constitute an unconstitutionally onerous burden on her right to vote. The court found that the legislature could establish rules regulating the manner of conducting both special and general elections. The court concluded that while there was a constitutional right to vote, there was no such constitutional right to an absentee ballot. The court distinguished the voter's situation from that of military persons and servicemen who are compelled by the government to be away on the day scheduled for voting because denying them the right to vote by absentee ballot would deny them to right to vote.


OUTCOME: The court denied the voter's application to declare that she had a right to vote in the special election by absentee ballot.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- right to absentee ballot -- application by petitioner, who will be on vacation on day of special election and has been refused absentee ballot, for declaration that she has right to vote in special election by absentee ballot, denied -- while there is constitutional right to vote, there is no constitutional right to receive absentee ballot, and Legislature may constitutionally limit absentee balloting to general elections and other specified situations.

Petitioner, who will be on vacation on the day of a special election, and has been refused an absentee ballot, challenges the constitutionality of the statutory provision authorizing absentee ballots for those who are away on vacation in the case of a general election but not in the case of a special election, and seeks a declaration that she has the right to vote in the special election by absentee ballot. Her application is denied. While there is a constitutional right to vote, there is no constitutional right to receive an absentee ballot, and the Legislature may constitutionally limit absentee balloting to general elections and other [***2] specified situations, such as in the case of servicemen and military voters, who are compelled to be away by government authority and not voluntarily taking a vacation.

In re Logel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


78 Misc. 2d 394; 356 N.Y.S.2d 775; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1413



June 6, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parents sought to have respondents, the City and State of New York, assume the cost of tuition, transportation, and maintenance of their handicapped child in a residential setting.


OVERVIEW: The parents of a handicapped child sought to have respondents assume the cost of tuition, transportation, and maintenance of their child in a residential setting. They argued that the statutory requirement that a parent, who was found able to do so, had to assume the cost of tuition, or contribute to such cost, consistent with his means to do so, was unconstitutional. The court found no basis for the conclusion that the provisions of the Family Court Act or the Education Law were violative of the equal protection provisions of the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court rejected the parents' argument that it was unconstitutional for respondents to require them to assume the cost of tuition, transportation, and maintenance of their handicapped child.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- handicapped children -- parent of handicapped child may constitutionally be required to provide maintenance expenses; extension of maintenance expenses to blind or deaf children, as act of public charity, does not violate equal protection of laws -- parent of handicapped child may not be required to contribute to cost of tuition.

1. The parent of a handicapped child may constitutionally be required to provide maintenance expenses for the child while in a residential setting. Although the basic obligation to provide maintenance is generally on the parent, there is no violation of equal protection of the laws in that blind or deaf children are extended full contribution for both tuition and maintenance, since this is an act of public charity and only a clear violation of the Constitution will justify overruling the Legislature.

2. The parent of a handicapped child may not constitutionally be required to contribute to the cost of tuition of a handicapped child, since the basic obligation to provide an education is imposed by the Legislature upon the community.

 

1454 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Webb


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


78 Misc. 2d 253; 356 N.Y.S.2d 494; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1376



June 5, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion against plaintiff state to dismiss the charge against him for the possession of an imitation pistol, in violation of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 436-5.0(g).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with the possession of an imitation pistol, in violation of § 436-5.0(g). He filed a motion against the state to dismiss the charge against him, based on the alleged unconstitutionality of the statute. The court denied defendant's motion, holding that N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05(9) did not preempt New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 436-5.0(g), because it did not so extensively treat the subject of imitation pistols as to evidence an intent or desire by the state legislature that it was to be the sole and exclusive legislation in this area. The court further held that by prohibiting possession of an imitation pistol only in the context of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05(9), the statute did not impliedly permit possession in all other circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the charge against him for the possession of an imitation pistol.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- possession or use of imitation pistol -- section 436-5.0 (subd. g) of Administrative Code of City of New York, rendering unlawful possession or use of imitation pistol which substantially duplicates actual pistol, unless it is colored other than black, blue, silver or aluminum and has closed barrel, is constitutional -- Penal Law does not pre-empt local legislation in regard to imitation pistols, nor is there conflict between provisions of it and of code -- enactment was not only proper but necessary exercise of city's police powers.

1. Section 436-5.0 (subd. g) of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which renders unlawful the possession or use of any imitation pistol which substantially duplicates an actual pistol, unless it is colored in colors other than black, blue, silver or aluminum and has a closed barrel, is constitutional.

2. Article 265 of the Penal Law, which is aimed at the control and regulation of firearms and other dangerous weapons, and which includes imitation pistols only when they are possessed with intent to use the same unlawfully against another (§ 265.05, subd. 9), and is silent as to any other use or [***2] possession of them, does not pre-empt local legislation in regard to imitation pistols, nor is there any conflict between the provisions of that article and the code, each of which seeks to regulate imitation pistols under its peculiar circumstances.

3. The enactment of the code provision was not only a proper exercise of the city's police powers, but the rampant use of imitation pistols in the commission of crimes in the city made it necessary.

People v. McNair


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


78 Misc. 2d 341; 356 N.Y.S.2d 205; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1399



June 4, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For all of the foregoing, defendant's application for an order staying the execution of the judgment pending the determination of her appeal and releasing her in her own recognizance is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an application for an order staying the execution of a judgment pending the determination of her appeal and releasing her in her own recognizance following her convictions for criminal possession of a controlled substance in the second degree, a class A-II felony, and criminal possession of hypodermic instruments and criminally using drug paraphernalia, both misdemeanors.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the crimes of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the second degree, a class A-II felony, criminal possession of hypodermic instruments, a misdemeanor, and two counts of criminally using drug paraphernalia, each misdemeanors. Defendant was sentenced to a term of from six years to life on the A-II felony conviction and one year on each of the misdemeanor counts, all sentences to run concurrently. Defendant filed an application for an order staying the execution of a judgment pending the determination of her appeal and releasing her in her own recognizance. The court held that it did not have the authority to grant the relief requested because N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 530.50 prohibited bail pending appeal where a class A felony sentence had been imposed. The court ruled that no bail could be set under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 460.50 because appellate reversal was highly improbable.


OUTCOME: Defendant's application for an order staying the execution of the judgment pending the determination of her appeal and releasing her in her own recognizance was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- stay of execution pending appeal -- defendant convicted of A-II felony is not entitled to stay, since bail is prohibited where class A felony sentence has been imposed and Legislature is assumed to have known of prohibition when A-I, A-II and A-III felonies were added -- discrepancy between search warrant and affidavit therefor, clarified by caption on warrant application, presents no reasonable possibility of reversal, and bail may therefore be denied.

1. Defendant who was convicted of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the second degree, a class A-II felony, is not entitled to a stay of judgment pending appeal. Such stays of judgment are treated primarily as a bail problem, and CPL 530.50 prohibits bail where a class A felony sentence has been imposed. The no-bail provisions apply to A-I, A-II and A-III felonies, since the Legislature is assumed to have known of the existing statute, when it added these felonies.

2. The affidavit on which the search [***2] warrant for the defendant's premises was issued requested a search of a downstairs apartment, while the warrant authorized a search of the upstairs premises. Since the heading of the application for the warrant specified an upstairs apartment, and the Court of Appeals has looked to warrant captions for clarification of discrepancies, there is no reasonable possibility of reversal on this ground. Since bail may be denied where appellate reversal is highly improbable, as is the case here, defendant is accordingly denied an order staying execution of judgment pending appeal.

 

1456 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Thompson v. Lomenzo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


78 Misc. 2d 298; 356 N.Y.S.2d 760; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1388



May 31, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant is awarded judgment dismissing the complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, licensed real estate brokers, brought an action to enjoin defendant, the Secretary of the State of New York, from enforcing an order prohibiting, until further notice, solicitation of listings of property for sale or purchase, within certain areas subject to the penalty provisions of N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 441-c.


OVERVIEW: The Secretary issued an order that prohibited licensed real estate brokers and salesmen from soliciting listings of properties for sale or purchase within a certain geographic area, subject to the penalty provisions of N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 441-c. The court held that the order: (1) was not violative of Fifth Amendment due process rights; (2) was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad; (3) was properly designed to cure an evil rampant in the community; (4) did not abrogate authority to the Secretary beyond that possessed by him or reasonably necessary to effectively deal with the problem; and (5) properly prohibited a pernicious practice and was reasonably limited in area and function.


OUTCOME: The court awarded judgment to the Secretary of State and dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Brokers -- real estate brokers -- Secretary of State acted within authority vested in him by statute when, after he found that homeowners in two areas of Brooklyn and Queens were being unduly harassed by excessive solicitation on part of licensed real estate brokers and salesmen, he issued blanket order which prohibited such brokers and salesmen from soliciting listings of properties for sale or purchase in those areas, and warned that violation of order would subject them to penalties prescribed by statute -- order was not violative of due process, was not unconstitutionally vague, and was not unconstitutionally overbroad -- brokers and salesmen have no absolute right of solicitation, and order does not prohibit them from pursuing their business activities, subject to reasonable limitation -- order imposes no discipline, but merely defines prohibited conduct, and violation of it could properly be regarded as evidence of untrustworthiness.

1. The Secretary of State acted within the authority vested in him by statute when, after he found that homeowners in two areas of Brooklyn and Queens were being unduly harassed [***2] by excessive solicitation on the part of licensed real estate brokers and salesmen, and in an attempt to resolve a serious challenge to the stability and tranquility of the community and to end abuses then rampant therein, he issued a blanket order which prohibited such brokers and salesmen from soliciting listings of properties for sale and purchase, in any manner whatsoever, in those areas, and warned that violation of the order would subject them to the penalties prescribed by statute, i.e., revocation or suspension of license for untrustworthiness.

2. The order was not violative of due process. It was made after public hearings had been held, following the receipt of some 8,000 statements by homeowners, who indicated that they did not wish to be solicited, and telephone and personal complaints from homeowners objecting to the practice, and notice of those hearings was sent to 30,000 homeowners and to every licensed broker in the area. The notice was sufficient, and a trial type hearing was not required.

3. The order was not unconstitutionally vague, since it gave fair notice of what conduct was forbidden, nor was it unconstitutionally overbroad, since the geographic boundaries [***3] encompassed by it were reasonable and its operative effect did not exceed the evil to be corrected.

4. Licensed real estate brokers and salesmen have no absolute right of solicitation. The order does not prohibit them from engaging in the real estate business, but only prohibits soliciting in the proscribed areas, and, subject to this reasonable limitation, they are free to pursue their business activities.

5. The order, per se, imposes no discipline, creates no new penalties, and merely defines prohibited conduct. Violation of it could properly be regarded as evidence of untrustworthiness, subjecting offenders to punishment.

 

1457 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Spencer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Tompkins County


78 Misc. 2d 643; 357 N.Y.S.2d 616; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1464



May 31, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the indictment filed by the State, which charged them with possession of marijuana, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.10, N.Y. Penal Law § 220.20(2), and N.Y. Penal Law § 220.15, and contended that the statutes were unconstitutional, null, and void.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were indicted for possession of marijuana and filed a motion to dismiss the indictment, contending that the statutes were unconstitutional, null, and void because marijuana was not a dangerous narcotic drug. The court held that the State had the broad power to regulate the narcotic drug traffic within its boarders and further found that the State had the power to regulate the administration, sale, prescription, and use of dangerous and habit-forming drugs. The court determined that it was not to be concerned with the policy, wisdom, or expediency of legislation and found that, where the means adopted by the legislature were not prohibited, the court was not permitted to inquire into the degree of other necessity. The court held that a strong presumption of constitutionality attached to all legislation and found that an appeal for judicial recognition of a drug dependent defense was to be addressed by the legislature. The court denied defendants' motion.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss the indictment, which charged them with possession of marijuana, and held that the statutes outlawing such activity were not unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- dangerous drugs -- court will not dismiss indictment on grounds that sections 220.20 and 220.15 of Penal Law treat marijuana as dangerous narcotic drug, alleged to be erroneous classification, since People cite references to harmful effects of drug, and trial court should not rule statute unconstitutional unless such conclusion is inescapable -- in view of State's broad power to regulate drugs, court, for policy reasons, will not interfere with role of Legislature on issue of cruel and unusual punishment.

1. The court will not grant a motion to dismiss an indictment for violation of sections 220.20 and 220.15 (subd. 2) of the Penal Law on the grounds that the statutes treat marijuana as a dangerous narcotic drug, which is alleged to be an erroneous classification, without rational basis and depriving defendants of equal protection of the laws. The People cite several references to the harmful effects of the drug, and courts of first instance should not declare a statute unconstitutional "unless such conclusion is inescapable". In view of the strong presumption of constitutionality of all legislation, the trial court will deny the defendants'  [***2] motion to dismiss.

2. The State has broad power to regulate dangerous drugs, and in consideration of the growing concern over drugs and crime, for policy reasons the court will not interfere with the role of the Legislature on the issue of cruel and unusual punishment.

 

1458 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York by Redlich v. Goldman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


78 Misc. 2d 693; 356 N.Y.S.2d 754; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1474



May 30, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the petition is granted and warrant will issue pursuant to section 715 of the Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law directing the removal of the tenant respondent Fulton Renting Corp. from the possession of premises 264-266 West 46th Street, New York, New York.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city brought a proceeding to evict respondent tenant under N.Y. Real Prop. Acts § 711(5) and N.Y. Real Prop. Acts § 715.


OVERVIEW: The city instituted the eviction proceeding against the tenant hotel operators in order to remove them from possession and control of the subject premises because of the open and notorious use thereof by persons of ill repute for purposes of prostitution. The tenant asserted that N.Y. Real Prop. Acts § 715 was unconstitutional because it adversely affected the rights of the hotel to continue in occupancy. The court held that if the city showed that the tenant's possession and use was illegal, then there was no constitutional right to continue in occupancy. The court found that the city had proved a condition of permanence sufficient to constitute a use of the premises for the purposes of prostitution, which justified the removal of the tenants.


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's petition and directed the removal of the tenant from the possession of the hotel premises.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- summary proceedings -- eviction for prostitution -- City of New York may, under section 715 of Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law, evict tenant-operator of hotel regularly used for prostitution, upon showing of 58 arrests and upon reputation testimony, since statute deems "ill repute" to be presumptive evidence of unlawfulness -- action for injunction under Public Health Law will not bar present action, since it is based on different arrests and is for different relief -- there is no constitutional right to continue occupancy shown to be illegal.

1. Petitioner, City of New York, is entitled to a judgment of eviction pursuant to section 715 of the Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law, directing the removal of the tenant respondent from possession as operator of a hotel regularly utilized for purposes of prostitution. The eviction may be based upon 58 prostitution [***2] arrests made in and about the premises and upon reputation testimony, despite lack of direct proof of commission of illegal acts on the premises themselves, since the statute sets forth "ill repute" as presumptive evidence of unlawfulness. An inference of acquiescence in an occupancy for the prohibited purposes is warranted, and the court will not permit continued deterioration of security, economic potential and appearance of this area of the city.

2. An action for injunctive relief under sections 2320-2333 of the Public Health Law, brought with respect to 31 prior arrests for prostitution, is not a prior action which will bar the present action, since this action is based upon different arrests and is for different relief.

3. There is no constitutional right to continue an occupancy shown to be illegal.

 

1459 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


O'Donnell v. Antin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


81 Misc. 2d 849; 369 N.Y.S.2d 895; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2027



May 16, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant school board adopted a resolution eliminating transportation of students to religious schools outside defendant school district. Plaintiff parents sued for declaratory and injunctive relief to prevent the implementation of the resolution. The court declared the resolution to be lawful. The appellate division affirmed the denial of a preliminary injunction, but remanded for a trial on the parents' constitutional claims.


OVERVIEW: The parents contended that N.Y. Educ. Law § 3635 required the district to bus their children. Section 3635 required a school district to provide transportation for all the children residing within the district to the school they legally attended where such students lived within a certain distance from such schools. A school district was not required to provide such transportation for children residing within a city school district, but if provided, such transportation was to be offered equally to all such children in like circumstances. The court found that students attending school outside the district were not in like circumstances to students going to school in the district and that since the district bused all children within the district and denied busing to children going out of the district, it was complying with the statute. The court also found the statute did not deprive city school districts of equal protection of the laws. There was no showing that the legislature's determination that rural areas did not have transportation facilities equal to those of urban areas was irrational. The court held the statute did not infringe on the parents' free exercise of religion.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the board's resolution and the actions of defendants in effectuating the resolution were lawful and proper and that the provision of the New York Education Law at issue was constitutional as written and as it applied to the parents.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- transportation -- city school district resolved to provide transportation for any child who lives not less than nor more than specified distances from school he attends, but not if such school is private or parochial school located outside of school district; such resolution is valid (Education Law, § 3635).

A city school district elected to provide transportation for all children residing in the district who live between 1.5 and 10 miles from any public, private, or parochial school they attend; and subsequently, for budgetary reasons, the school district resolved to eliminate such transportation to any private or parochial school which is located outside of the school district's boundaries. The latter resolution validly eliminates transportation for children who live within 1.5 to 10 miles from the private or parochial school which they attend if said school is located outside of the school district. The school district is still providing transportation "equally to all * * * children in like circumstances" (Education Law, § 3635).

 

1460 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Von Diezelski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


78 Misc. 2d 69; 355 N.Y.S.2d 556; 1974 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1332



May 13, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application for a writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant detainee sought a writ of habeas corpus challenging his treatment in the county jail, contending that his rights of equal protection and due process were violated because of his treatment as a prisoner, subject to the same rules and regulations as those who were imprisoned as punishment for convictions rather than simply as detainees awaiting trial. The detainee sought an order relieving him of his alleged unlawful confinement.


OVERVIEW: A detainee was held in the county jail awaiting trial on charges of second-degree kidnapping, first-degree rape, and first-degree robbery. On another indictment he was charged with other, more serious crimes. He sought a writ of habeas corpus challenging his detention subject to the same conditions as prisoners who had already been convicted. The court denied his petition, finding that the jail's rules and regulations could be applied equally to pretrial detainees and prisoners without violating constitutional limitations because such rules were designed to promote order, health, safety, and security, which were all legitimate government interests. The court found that none of the conditions the detainee complained of appeared to serve a punitive or rehabilitative purpose such as would prevent their equal application to pretrial detainees and prisoners. Overall conditions in the jail did not constitute such an unreasonable encroachment on protected freedoms as to render the detention unconstitutional because the practices in question furthered an important government interest and the limitation of freedoms was no greater than necessary to the furtherance of that interest.


OUTCOME: The court denied the detainee's application for a writ of habeas corpus in his action challenging his treatment as a prisoner.


HEADNOTES

Prisons and prisoners -- pretrial detainees -- application for writ of habeas corpus, made by defendant being held in county jail pending trial, who argues that rights of pretrial detainee are violated when he is treated in same fashion as convicted prisoner, is denied -- while restraints imposed upon pretrial detainee in excess of those reasonably related to purpose of his confinement, assuring attendance at trial, which is legitimate State interest, constitute deprivation of due process, conditions under which this defendant is confined and regulations to which he is subject do not constitute such unreasonable encroachment on his protected freedoms as to render his detention unconstitutional or otherwise illegal.

A defendant being held in a county jail pending trial seeks, by way of habeas corpus, to be relieved of "unlawful prison confinement" and detained "in a manner, which is not a prison situation", the major thrust of his argument being that the rights of a pretrial detainee are violated when he is treated in the same fashion as the convicted prisoner. His application is denied. A pretrial detainee may be [***2] subjected to such restraints as are reasonably related to the purpose of his confinement, i.e., assuring his attendance at trial, which is a legitimate State interest, and, while restraints imposed upon him in excess of those reasonably related to that purpose constitute a deprivation of due process, it does not follow that every restraint imposed equally upon him and the convicted prisoner is a violation of his rights. Institutional life necessarily entails rules and regulations designed to promote order, health, safety and security, which may be applied equally to pretrial detainees and convicted prisoners without transgressing constitutional limitations. The conditions under which this defendant is confined and the regulations to which he is subject do not constitute such an unreasonable encroachment on his protected freedoms as to render his detention unconstitutional or otherwise illegal.
