In re Estate of Johnson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Bronx County


75 Misc. 2d 502; 348 N.Y.S.2d 315; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1602



September 28, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner decedent's mother filed the claim asserting that she was the sole beneficiary of the estate in the proceedings to approve settlement of a wrongful death action, to settle the account of the administratrix, and for other relief with reference to administrative expenses and debts. Petitioner decedent's children also claimed entitlement to the decedent's estate.


OVERVIEW: The decedent died after an automobile accident, leaving eight illegitimate children. The decedent's mother and the children both petitioned to receive the damages awarded in subsequent wrongful death litigation. The court noted that N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2(a) para. 2 specifically denied to illegitimate children the right to be distributees of a father, unless a court of competent jurisdiction during the lifetime of the father entered an order establishing paternity within two years after the child's birth. No such order had been entered establishing the paternity of the eight children. The court further observed that illegitimate children were permitted to inherit from their mother and to be distributees of their mother's estate equally with legitimate children. The court concluded that, to the extent the statutes denied the children the right to participate in the distribution of the proceeds of an action for the wrongful death of their putative father, they were denied the equal protection of the law guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. The court granted the children's claim to inherit the wrongful death award.


OUTCOME: The court granted the claim filed by the decedent's children to the proceeds of the decedent's estate, including the wrongful death settlement. The court also approved the wrongful death settlement, and allowed other costs for the funeral, the hospital, and the attorney for the administratrix, from the decedent's estate.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Death -- wrongful death -- illegitimate children -- net amount of cause of action for wrongful death of father should be distributed to illegitimate children of decedent -- statutes disinheriting illegitimate children when there is no order of filiation are unconstitutional -- children have burden of establishing filiation and pecuniary loss.

To the extent that EPTL 5-4.4, 4-1.1 and 4-1.2 deny children the right to participate in the distribution of the proceeds of an action for the wrongful death of their putative father when there is no order of filiation, they deny to the infants the equal protection of the law guaranteed by the United States and New York State Constitutions. Without an order of filiation, the children must establish that the decedent was in fact their father and that as a result of their father's death they sustained a pecuniary loss. Having proven this, the balance of the settlement of the action after expenses should be distributed to the children of decedent.

 

1531 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Jerry v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Onondaga County


75 Misc. 2d 461; 347 N.Y.S.2d 917; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1609



September 24, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher filed an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 for relief after respondent school board confirmed the suspension of the teacher without pay until all the charges against him were disposed of. The teacher requested the rescission of the suspension, the striking of every reference to it from his personnel file, and to have been reinstated with all back pay and benefits.


OVERVIEW: The teacher contended that no statute authorized his suspension without pay and that if any statute did, it was unconstitutional for depriving him of due process. The court denied the teacher's petition. The court determined that the school board had a valid governmental interest in protecting the health and safety of the school children entrusted to its care. The school board must also have protected the governmental interest that there should have been no gifts of the public's money. The court determined that as long as the teacher was able to have been reimbursed when found innocent, the government's interest should have prevailed. Therefore, the court concluded that a prior hearing was not constitutionally required before a school board could have suspended a teacher without pay on charges that, if proven, would have warranted his dismissal. Finally, the court determined that article 78 was not available for interlocutory relief and that if the teacher was aggrieved by the hearing then he had the right to appeal when there had been a final determination.


OUTCOME: The court denied the teacher's petition in which he sought the rescission of his suspension without pay and reinstatement.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- teachers -- if formal charges have been made against teacher and would warrant his dismissal, he may be suspended without pay prior to hearing -- rulings on evidence and bill of particulars cannot be reviewed until charges have been heard and finally determined.

1. When a tenured teacher has been formally charged with misconduct -- physical punishment of schoolchildren and assaultive tendencies, in this case -- which, if proved, would warrant his dismissal, he may be suspended without pay, even before any hearing is had (Education Law, §§ 3020-a, 2554, 2566; N. Y. Const., art. VII, § 8; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.).

2. If the Hearing Officer is erroneously permitting evidence beyond the scope of the charges, or if the school board's bill of particulars of the charges is not sufficient, such interlocutory matters cannot be reviewed by the State Commissioner of Education, or by the court under CPLR article 78, until the school board has finally determined the proceeding against the teacher (Education Law, § 3020-a, subd. 4).

 

1532 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dolan v. Kelly


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


76 Misc. 2d 151; 348 N.Y.S.2d 478; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1454



September 21, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police officer sought an injunction prohibiting respondent Commissioner of the Suffolk County Police Department (commissioner) from requiring the officer to submit to a polygraph examination about the misconduct of a fellow officer.


OVERVIEW: The officer and his partner were on the scene of an apparent suicide. The partner was seen taking money from the deceased's pocket. During the investigation regarding the money, the officer was told to take a polygraph. Although he appeared for a polygraph test he was too agitated to proceed. The commissioner ordered that the officer take the test or be dismissed. The officer sought an injunction against the commissioner. The court recognized that police officers were protected by the Fifth Amendment privilege against self-incrimination. Therefore, the court found that the officer could not be required to waive his immunity. Although protected from self-incrimination by the Fifth Amendment, the court found that this did not extend to a protection of the officer's employment. The commissioner's investigation into the partner's misconduct was specifically, directly, and narrowly related to the officer's performance of his duties and was therefore within his discretion in managing the police department. The court acknowledged the controversy over the accuracy of the polygraph test, but found that did not preclude it from being useful within limits. The court dismissed the petition.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the officer's petition to enjoin the commissioner from requiring him to take a polygraph test.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- police -- submission to polygraph examination -- police officer during course of departmental investigation of alleged misconduct of fellow officer may be compelled to submit to polygraph examination or risk dismissal -- privilege against self incrimination is not bar to dismissal of police officer who refuses to answer questions relating to performance of official duties -- refusal to submit to polygraph examination constitutes justiciable controversy.

1. In an article 78 proceeding instituted to enjoin the Commissioner of the Suffolk County Police Department from requiring the petitioner, a police officer, to either submit to a polygraph examination during the course of a departmental investigation of alleged misconduct of a fellow officer or risk being suspended or dismissed, the petition is dismissed. The Commissioner having a justifiable interest in ascertaining whether the petitioner is telling the truth and the polygraph being a useful investigative tool whose results are frequently reliable within recognized limits, there is no constitutional prohibition which petitioner may raise against testifying and there is no statutory [***2] bar to the use of polygraph examinations by either public or private employees.

2. The privilege against self incrimination is not a bar to dismissal of a police officer who refuses to answer questions specifically, directly and narrowly relating to the performance of his official duties when he is not required to waive his immunity with respect to the use of his answers in a subsequent criminal proceeding.

3. Although the Commissioner has not ordered the petitioner to submit to a polygraph examination, an order will follow if his "request" is refused. After several postponements of the test, the institution of this proceeding constitutes a refusal to submit to the test so that there exists a justiciable controversy.

 

1533 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kuntz v. Newburgh Teachers Asso.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


75 Misc. 2d 389; 347 N.Y.S.2d 905; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1613; 84 L.R.R.M. 2799; 73 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P53,288



September 20, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs brought an action seeking a permanent injunction restraining defendants from engaging in a concerted work stoppage in violation of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs brought an action seeking a permanent injunction restraining defendants from engaging in a concerted work stoppage. The court stated that the language of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210 forbidding concerted work stoppages by teachers was clear. This law, which denied the right to strike to certain public employees, was an expression of the legislature's conclusion that in an orderly society, the benefits to be gained from the use of a strike in aid of collective bargaining were outweighed by the damage to the community as a whole when certain necessary public functions were brought to a halt by the strike. Nowhere was such damage more apparent than when the orderly operation of a city's school's was disrupted. The court stated that § 210 could not be intentionally violated, legally or morally, on the theory that it was being "tested." The court held that its duty to issue the injunction was clear, and that defendants' duty to obey the injunction was also clear.


OUTCOME: The court awarded the requested injunctive relief to plaintiffs and entered an order restraining defendants from engaging in a concerted work stoppage.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- Public Employees' Fair Employment Act -- strike by teachers -- teachers may not strike in aid of collective bargaining -- preliminary injunction granted -- objectives of Civil Service Law may not be tested by intentionally violating it.

1. A preliminary injunction restraining a strike by teachers, this right being denied by section 210 of the Civil Service Law, is granted. Other remedies are afforded if the Board of Education has failed to bargain collectively in good faith (Civil Service Law, §§ 209, 209-a, subd. 1).

2. The issue of whether the objectives of the statute are unconstitutional or invalid has been passed upon by the Court of Appeals and may not be tested by intentionally violating it.

 

1534 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Baylis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


75 Misc. 2d 397; 347 N.Y.S.2d 892; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1618



September 19, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, the motion is denied with leave to renew under the circumstances set forth above.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the indictment or, alternatively, to preclude the use by the State of any witnesses whose testimony rebutted defendant's alibi defense.


OVERVIEW: The State furnished a demand that defendant produce its alibi witnesses pursuant to statute. Defendant complied, but soon after, the United States Supreme Court held invalid an Oregon alibi statute. Defendant argued that the New York statute was also unconstitutional. The court denied the motion with leave to renew it at trial. The court reasoned that because defendant already produced the witness, holding the statute unconstitutional would not benefit defendant. The court further reasoned that it was doubtful if the new case law would be applied retroactively. Further, under the new case law, a defendant was only entitled to reciprocal discovery of those witnesses the State intended to offer in rebuttal to the alibi defense. The State had no witnesses in rebuttal. The court stated that if the State at trial attempted to offer alibi testimony, then defendant could file a motion to preclude.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the indictment but granted defendant leave to refile at trial if the State produced a rebuttal witness to the alibi.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- alibi -- after defendant named his alibi witnesses pursuant to notice under CPL 250.20, Wardius v. Oregon (412 U.S. 470) was decided; defendant is not entitled to dismissal of indictment or to declaration that CPL 250.20 is unconstitutional or that Wardius has retroactive effect -- under Wardius due process requires that defendant be given reciprocal discovery of alibi witnesses; such witnesses are not those who place defendant at scene of crime.

1. The People served on defendant, pursuant to CPL 250.20 a notice demanding that he furnish a list of alibi witnesses. Defendant complied with the demand. Shortly thereafter Wardius v. Oregon (412 U.S. 470), holding that the Oregon alibi statute was unconstitutional for failure to accord defendant reciprocal discovery rights was handed down. At this time defendant is not entitled to a declaration that CPL 250.20 is unconstitutional or that Wardius has retroactive effect, and is not entitled to dismissal of the indictment.

2. Under Wardius, due process requires that defendant be given reciprocal discovery [***2] of those witnesses the People intend to offer in rebuttal of his alibi defense. Rebuttal witnesses do not include those who place defendant at the scene of the crime but include only those who offer testimony that defendant was not where he claims to have been. Here the People do not have any rebuttal witnesses but should they produce any at the trial, defendant may move to preclude.

People v. Sumpter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Central Narcotics Part, New York County


75 Misc. 2d 55; 347 N.Y.S.2d 670; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1633



September 10, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant served a subpoena duces tecum on the Police Department of the City of New York requiring the production of the personnel records of two police officers who were expected to be witnesses against defendant in a narcotics prosecution so that defendant could ascertain whether the officers' credibility could be impeached by prior "bad acts." The police department's counsel moved to quash the subpoena.


OVERVIEW: In response to defendant's subpoena duces tecum, the police department produced laudatory citation forms for the two police officers who were expected to testify against defendant on narcotics charges. Defense counsel requested that the court direct that the police department produce all of the officers' personnel and disciplinary records. The court denied the police department's motion to quash the subpoena. The court held that, where a public officer such as a policeman was called as a witness in the performance of his public duty, the prosecution had a duty to make available to the court any information in its possession or in the police department's possession which might go to the issue of defendant's guilt, including evidence affecting the credibility of the police officer. The court could not, by quashing the subpoena, preclude the possibility of defense discovery of such evidence if it existed. The court ordered the police department to produce the sealed personnel records of the two officers for examination and determination as to whether any information should be made available to defense counsel to aid in cross-examination to impeach credibility or for other purposes.


OUTCOME: The court denied the police department's motion to quash the subpoena and ordered that the personnel records of the two officers be produced to the trial judge for examination and determination as to whether any information contained in such records should be made available to counsel for defendant in aid of cross-examination to impeach credibility or for other purposes.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- discovery -- credibility of witnesses -- Police Department is directed to produce, sealed, all personnel records of two police officers who are expected to be People's witnesses; Trial Judge will examine such records in camera and will determine whether any information contained therein shall be made available to defendant's counsel in aid of cross-examination to impeach credibility of such witnesses.

In response to a subpoena duces tecum issued to the Police Department by the defendant in a narcotics prosecution, the Police Department is directed by the court to produce to the Trial Judge all of the personnel records of two police officers who are expected to be the prosecution's witnesses. The records shall be sealed, and shall be examined by the Trial Judge in camera. The Trial Judge will then determine whether, in his discretion, any of the information contained in such records shall be made available to defendant's counsel in aid of cross-examination to impeach the credibility of such witnesses or for other valid purposes of the trial of defendant. (Cf. CPL 240.20, subds. 3, 4, 5; New York City Charter, §§ 1113, 1114.)

Lynbrook v. United Artists Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


75 Misc. 2d 124; 347 N.Y.S.2d 856; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1634



September 10, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant entertainment corporation brought before the court a motion to dismiss plaintiff village's action to enjoin the corporation from showing "Last Tango in Paris" in the village on the ground that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6330, under which the action was brought, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The issue in this case was whether N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6330 was constitutional. In this opinion, the court analyzed the language of the statute, including "obscene." The word was followed by other words: "lewd," "lascivious," "filthy," "indecent or disgusting". The court held that each of these words, especially because of the word "or" separating them, stood by itself and that the others that followed "obscene" did not and were not meant to define, describe, enlarge or circumscribe the word "obscene." The court held that the word "obscene" could be interpreted without the help or hindrance of the words that followed in the statute. The court held that the corporation failed to establish that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6330 was unconstitutional, and it failed to rebut the strong presumption of its constitutionality. The court held § 6330 to be constitutional. The court also held that the village's claim and the corporation's denial that the movie was obscene raised an issue that could not be decided on motion papers. A plenary trial was required.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion by the corporation to dismiss the action.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- obscenity -- CPLR 6330 which authorizes action to enjoin obscene motion picture is constitutional -- issues whether named motion picture is obscene and plaintiff's right to injunction require plenary trial.

1. CPLR 6330 which empowers the Supreme Court to enjoin the sale of obscene prints and authorizes certain named persons and entities to commence an action to enjoin the sale or display of a motion picture which is "obscene, lewd, lascivious, filthy, indecent or disgusting", is constitutional. Moving defendant has failed to rebut the strong presumption of constitutionality and has failed to establish that CPLR 6330 is unconstitutional.

2. The issues whether the motion picture "Last Tango in Paris" is obscene and whether plaintiff is entitled to an injunction require a plenary trial.

People v. Haynesworth


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


75 Misc. 2d 75; 347 N.Y.S.2d 540; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1643



September 4, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In all other respects the motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an indictment filed against him charging him with robbery in the first degree and assault in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was indicted by a grand jury, arraigned, and entered a plea of not guilty, the People served a notice of alibi pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.20. Defendant requested that the alibi notice be voided pursuant to a recent United States Supreme Court decision which defendant contended nullified § 250.20 and rendered it unconstitutional. The court denied defendant's motion holding that because the Supreme Court had indicated that reciprocity was the guideline for a constitutionally valid notice-of-alibi statute and the New York State alibi statute lacked reciprocal rights, it was not constitutionally valid. Furthermore, because defendant was entitled to mutual disclosure and had not received it the court invoked the penalty of preclusion and applied the standards of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.20(2) by excluding any testimony of the People's witnesses relating to defendant's alibi defense, but did not bar the people's right to cross-examine defendant's alibi witnesses.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss an indictment charging him with robbery in the first degree and assault in the second degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- alibi -- notice-of-alibi statute ( CPL 250.20) by failing to grant defendant reciprocal discovery fails to meet due process requirements -- indictment is not dismissed but defendant is entitled to exclude testimony of People's witnesses bearing on alibi defense.

1. The notice-of-alibi statute ( CPL 250.20) which requires defendant to state the names of his alibi witnesses but does not grant defendant reciprocal discovery rights fails to meet due process requirements.

2. While defendant is not entitled to dismissal of the indictment, he is entitled to the same penalty of preclusion granted the People in subdivision 2 of CPL 250.20 by excluding "any testimony of [the People's witnesses] relating to the alibi defense" at the time of trial. Such preclusion shall not bar the People's right to cross-examine defendant's alibi witnesses.

Redlich v. Capri Cinema, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


75 Misc. 2d 117; 347 N.Y.S.2d 811; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1674



August 14, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the Corporation Counsel of the City of New York and the District Attorney of New York County, filed suit pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6330, for judgments enjoining defendant motion picture exhibitors from displaying certain motion pictures. Plaintiffs therein sought injunctions during the pendency of the cases restraining defendants from displaying the films while defendants cross-moved for summary judgment of dismissal.


OVERVIEW: After prosecutions pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 235 were commenced against defendants in connection with their exhibition of four films claimed by the State to be obscene, plaintiffs filed suit for preliminary injunctions pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6330 to prevent the exhibition of the films in the interim. Defendants moved for summary judgment claiming that both the civil and criminal statutes were unconstitutionalbecause they were vague and overbroad. The court declared that both statutes were unconstitutional and granted defense summary judgments dismissing the complaints. It held that the definition of "obscene" found in N.Y. Penal Law § 235, which the court was statutorily prohibited from altering, was unconstitutional because it permitted the prohibition of material which, while "obscene, lewd, lascivious, filthy, indecent, or disgusting," nevertheless contained "serious literary, artistic, political, or scientific value." Because N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6330 incorporated the definition of "obscene" used in the penal statute, it, too, was unconstitutionally overbroad. Based on the finding of overbreadth, the court declared both statutes unconstitutional and dismissed the complaints.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the statute under which plaintiffs had filed suit was unconstitutionally overbroad and granted defendants' cross motions for summary judgment of dismissal.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- obscenity -- CPLR 6330 which empowers Supreme Court to enjoin distribution of obscene motion pictures is unconstitutional as overbroad regulation of expression.

 CPLR 6330 empowers the Supreme Court to enjoin the distribution of "any * * * motion picture * * * of an indecent character, which is obscene, lewd, lascivious, filthy, indecent or disgusting". Such statute is vague since it does not define the sexual conduct, the depiction of which it is aimed at. The quoted words do not relate only to sexual conduct, and even if they do, that conduct is not specifically defined. CPLR 6330 permits prohibition of material which, albeit "obscene, lewd, lascivious, filthy, indecent or disgusting" nevertheless contains "serious literary, artistic, political, or scientific value" which material may not be enjoined. CPLR 6330 therefore contains overbroad regulation of expression and is unconstitutional and violative of the First and Fourteenth Amendments to the Constitution of the United States. The complaint is dismissed.

District Three IUE Housing Development Fund Corp. v. Buckley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Rensselaer County


74 Misc. 2d 1078; 347 N.Y.S.2d 125; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1684



August 10, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, petitioner housing corporation sought an order of mandamus requiring respondent City of Troy (New York) to base the tax on the corporation's property on 60 percent of its assessed as it appears on the assessment rolls of the city. In its answer, respondent School District sought a declaration that it is not bound by the agreement between the city and the corporation because it was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The city and the corporation entered into an agreement wherein the city partially exempted the corporation from local and municipal taxes and from such taxes that were levied by or on behalf of any taxing jurisdiction described in N.Y. Priv. Hous. Fin. Law § 577(1)(b). The School District claimed the agreement did not limit the amount of taxes it could levy. The corporation contended that the School District was limited by the agreement. The court agreed with the corporation. Section 577(1)(b) provided that the maximum tax liability on the corporation, as a public housing development corporation, was 40 percent of the value of the property included in the project, or $ 45,500, whichever was lower. Under § 577(1)(b), because the city acted on behalf of the School District in levying the district's taxes, the tax exemption that bound the city also bound the School District. Thus, the tax was capped at $ 45,500. A constitutional issue based on a lack of adequate notice that was raised by the School District failed because the School District stipulated that it had actual knowledge of the resolution and agreement.


OUTCOME: The corporation was entitled to the relief sought.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- exemption -- city's resolution and agreement gave developer of low rent housing exemption from municipal taxes to extent of 40% of "value of property"; such value is assessed value not construction cost -- city's resolution and agreement bound School District -- School District may not question assessment nor method of arriving at it -- School District is entitled to proportionate share of minimum tax fixed by resolution and agreement.

1. The City Council of a city enacted a resolution, and the city entered into an agreement with petitioner, for the purpose of providing low income housing and granting petitioner exemption from local and municipal taxes to the extent of 40% of "the value of the property" included in the project but the tax liability in no event should be less than $ 45,500 per year. "Value of the property" upon which local and municipal taxes are based means the assessed valuation of the property as it appears on the city's assessment rolls, and not, as claimed by the city, the cost of construction of the completed project.

2. Section 577 (subd. 1, par. [b]) of the Private Housing Finance Law provides that where a municipality acts on behalf [***2] of another taxing jurisdiction in assessing real property for the purpose of taxation or in levying taxes therefor, the action of the local legislative body of such municipality in granting tax exemption shall have the effect of exempting the property from local and municipal taxes including school taxes levied by or on behalf of both such taxing jurisdictions. Under this section, the tax exemption granted by the city applies to the tax levied by the School District. The School District is bound by the agreement between the city and petitioner. The School District had actual knowledge of the resolution and agreement since the fall of 1972 and thus had ample opportunity to adjust its budget and finances. If any constitutional defect as to notice existed originally, it was rectified by the resolution adopted by the council on May 16, 1972 which ratified and confirmed the resolution of June 4, 1970 authorizing the tax abatement.

3. Since the School District must base the taxes levied by it on the city's assessment rolls, it may not question the assessment fixed by the city, nor the method of arriving at the assessed valuation, nor may it raise any valid objection to the granting [***3] of any exemption or abatement of taxes by the city.

4. The School District is entitled, against the city retroactively, to its fair proportionate share of the minimum tax.

 

1540 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Watts v. Watts


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


77 Misc. 2d 178; 350 N.Y.S.2d 285; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1213



August 8, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state, on behalf of the mother and children, brought an action to determine whether custody of the children should be awarded to the mother or respondent father.


OVERVIEW: The state, acting on behalf of the mother and children, brought an action to determine custody of three infant children. The court evaluated the law, within and without the state, in determining the best interests of the children. The court found that, in theory, the father had an equal right with the mother with respect to custody: however, the court noted that in the overwhelming majority of cases, the mother was awarded custody. The court also noted that the presumption favoring the mother violated state law. The court held that the tender years' presumption in favor of the mother was an unconstitutional discrimination against the father. After reviewing all of the testimony, reading all the medical reports and records, and observing the demeanor and responses of the parties on the witness stand, the court concluded that it was in the best interests of the children for them to be in their father's custody.


OUTCOME: The court awarded custody of the three infant children to the father.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and child -- custody -- as between mother and father, "there shall be no prima facie right to the custody of the child in either parent" (Domestic Relations Law, §§ 70, 240) -- father is constitutionally entitled to "equal protection of the laws" -- modern psychological studies contravene assumption that children of tender years belong in custody of mother -- Family Court awards custody to father.

1. In this custody dispute between the mother and the father of male and female children, the Family Court Judge (Family Ct. Act, § 651) has heard all the testimony and has observed the demeanor and responses of the parties testifying, and has studied all the medical records and reports, and the court now concludes that it is in the best interests of the children to be in their father's custody, and accordingly the court awards their custody to their father.

2. The Domestic Relations Law ( §§ 70, 240) provides that "In all cases there shall be no prima facie right to the custody of the child in either parent". These sections, when considered along with the constitutional requirement for "the equal protection of the laws", and recent liberal decisions of the United States [***2] Supreme Court, and with the published studies and conclusions of modern psychologists, psychoanalysts and anthropologists, have outlawed and repealed the sterotyped presumption that children of tender years belong in the custody of the mother and that only if the mother is proved to be unfit should their custody be awarded to their father.

In re Garrett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Monroe County


74 Misc. 2d 961; 346 N.Y.S.2d 651; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1718



July 26, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court adjudicated defendant, a juvenile, to be a juvenile delinquent. The court found that defendant had committed acts which, if committed by an adult, would constitute the crimes of murder, first-degree robbery and grand larceny. The court denied defendant's preliminary motion for a jury trial. Defendant's law guardian filed a motion asking the court to refrain from ordering any disposition under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 758(b).


OVERVIEW: In a bench trial the court found that the defendant had, in concert with two other persons, robbed and fatally bludgeoned a victim. Defendant's law guardian filed a motion requesting the court to refrain from sentencing defendant to an indefinite reformatory term as allowed by § 758(b); the guardian asserted that because defendant had been denied a jury at his disposition hearing, sentencing him to an indefinite reformatory term would violate defendant's constitutional rights under U.S. Const. amends. V, VIII, and XIV. The guardian also argued that the distinction that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 758(b) made, for dispositional purposes, between older and younger juveniles was unconstitutionally discriminatory. The court sentenced defendant pursuant to § 758(b). There was no jury trial right in juvenile adjudication hearings. Defendant's incarceration in a reception center instead of a state training school was not cruel or unusual punishment and did not violate his equal protection rights; the State had a legitimate interest in protecting younger, detained juveniles from more dangerous, older ones. The seriousness of defendant's crimes dictated the imposition of a serious disposition.


OUTCOME: The court committed defendant to a state reformatory for an indefinite term not to exceed three years for each of his criminal acts robbery and murder, with the terms to run concurrently.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- juvenile delinquents -- juvenile delinquent, who committed acts which if done by adult would be Class A felony (murder), is sentenced to Elmira Reception Center for three years.

Respondent was adjudicated a juvenile delinquent upon a finding by the Family Court that he committed acts which, if committed by an adult, would constitute the crimes of murder (a Class A felony), robbery in the first degree (a Class B felony), and grand larceny in the third degree. Subdivision (b) of section 758 of the Family Court Act provides for sentencing respondent to the Elmira Reception Center for an indefinite reformatory term not to exceed three years. On this dispositional hearing the court considers that the safety of the community, of respondent and of his peers requires that respondent be sentenced to said reception center for three years, without a jury trial, finding no evidence that incarceration therein would constitute cruel and unusual punishment. ( Matter of Reginald S., 64 Misc 2d 1002; Matter of Anthony F., 69 Misc 2d 932, and Matter of Rice v. Cory, 73 Misc 2d 813, not followed.)

Frost v. Mohawk Nat'l Bank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Rensselaer County


74 Misc. 2d 912; 347 N.Y.S.2d 246; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1722; 13 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 389



July 25, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion of the plaintiff is therefore denied, the order of this court to show cause, dated June 6, 1973, is vacated, and the plaintiff is directed to return the automobile to defendants forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff individual filed a motion for a preliminary injunction and temporary restraining order enjoining defendants, a bank and other parties, from continuing to deprive the individual of the use of an automobile in her action contesting the repossession of the automobile.


OVERVIEW: The individual purchased a vehicle, which was financed by the bank under a retail installment contract. The individual defaulted on two consecutive monthly payments and the bank repossessed the vehicle. In the individual's action challenging the repossession, the individual filed a motion for injunctive relief enjoining defendants from continuing to deprive the individual of the use of the automobile during the proceedings. The court denied the motion and vacated an order to show cause. The court stated that the contract contained a provision that allowed the seller, upon default, to repossess the vehicle without notice or process of law. The court held the contract was in compliance with U.C.C. § 9-503, which prohibited repossession without judicial process unless the debtor had expressly authorized "self-help" provisions of the contract.


OUTCOME: The court denied the individual's motion for a preliminary injunction and temporary restraining order enjoining defendants from continuing to deprive the individual of the use of a repossessed automobile in her action challenging the repossession of the vehicle.


HEADNOTES

Secured transactions -- repossession -- security agreement may validly authorize peaceful self-help repossession of automobile.

In a security agreement covering an automobile, a provision empowering the creditor, in the event of default, to take possession of the automobile peacefully and without process of law, is valid and constitutional (cf. Uniform Commercial Code, § 9-503).

People v. Mitchell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


74 Misc. 2d 1053; 346 N.Y.S.2d 495; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1729



July 20, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Consequently, defendants' motions to dismiss the complaints against them are hereby granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the owners of adult bookstores, were charged by the New York City ,New York Department of Consumer Affairs with maintaining places or premises of public amusement without a license, pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code §§ B32-1.0 and B32-4.0. The owners challenged those provisions as violating their First Amendment constitutional rights, and alleged that the ordinances constituted an impermissible prior restraint.


OVERVIEW: The department proceeded against the owners for maintaining places of public amusement without a license. The question for consideration was whether the licensing scheme constituted a valid exercise of the police power in an area, which clearly impinged on First Amendment freedoms. New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B32-1.0(a) provided for a license to operate a place of public amusement upon such terms and conditions as the commissioner deemed necessary for proper regulation and good order, and New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B32-4.0 stated that such licenses, in the discretion of the commissioner, could have been subject to conditions which, in his judgment, might have been essential for the welfare and benefit of the city. The court held that the sections were unconstitutional on their face in that they subjected the exercise of freedoms protected by the First Amendment to the prior restraint of a license without narrow, objective and definite standards to guide the licensing authority.


OUTCOME: The court granted the owners' motions to dismiss the complaints against them.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- peep shows -- statutes requiring license for coin-operated motion picture shows in bookstores which statutes give broad discretion to Commissioner of Consumer Affairs of City of New York are unconstitutional infringements on First Amendment rights; prosecutions for failure to obtain licenses are dismissed.

Prosecutions against defendants for failing to obtain a license for their places of public amusement as prescribed by sections B32-1.0 and B32-4.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York for operating peep shows through coin-operated motion picture machines in their bookstores are dismissed. The sections give unlimited discretion to the Commissioner of Consumer Affairs to establish conditions for the granting of a license, and since the showing of motion pictures involves the exercise of First Amendment freedoms, the statutes constitute an unconstitutional prior restraint of the right to free expression.

 

1544 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Amendola v. Jackson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


74 Misc. 2d 797; 346 N.Y.S.2d 353; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1738



July 16, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus against respondent penitentiary warden after the family court, in a hearing regarding his failure to pay child support, advised him of his right to be represented by his own attorney but not of his right to court-appointed counsel if he could not afford one.


OVERVIEW: The father was dealt with in accordance with N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 454, and the family court judge advised him that he had the right to be represented by his own lawyer and that he could waive that right and speak for himself. The court held that a person accused of a crime had a constitutionally protected right to the court appointment of counsel to represent him where he could not otherwise afford to pay for private counsel. The court then concluded that an indigent parent, faced with the loss of a child's society, as well as the possibility of criminal charges, N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act §§ 1014, 1055; N.Y. Penal Law § 260.10, was entitled to the assistance of counsel with assigned counsel if the parent lacked the means to retain a lawyer. The court found that the father faced criminal prosecution for the charges lodged against him by the family court's enforcement officer, N.Y. Penal Law § 260.05. Accordingly, the court granted the petition, and the writ was sustained.


OUTCOME: The court granted the father's application for a writ of habeas corpus for release.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and wife -- support -- husband who appears in Family Court for alleged violation of support orders must be informed that, if he cannot afford counsel, court will appoint one for him; immaterial that proceeding is civil in nature -- husband did not waive right to counsel.

1. Every respondent who appears before the Family Court to answer for the alleged violation of its support orders -- which could result in respondent's commitment to jail (Family Ct. Act, § 433) -- must be informed at the outset that, if he cannot afford a lawyer, the court will appoint an attorney to represent him. It does not matter that the proceeding is a civil proceeding. Section 433 provides that the court must inform respondent of the contents of the petition and advise him of his right to counsel. While the Family Court did tell respondent that he had a right to counsel, when respondent stated that he could not afford one, the Family Court should have advised him of his right to court-appointed counsel.

2. Since respondent was not advised of his right to a free lawyer, he cannot be deemed to have waived his right.

 

1545 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cornick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Narcotics Courts, New York County


75 Misc. 2d 169; 346 N.Y.S.2d 1004; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1746



July 12, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the foregoing reasons that branch of defendant's motion addressed to the dismissal of the indictment for lack of jurisdiction is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant field a motion to dismiss the indictment pending against him in the court for lack of jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Because of a critical backlog of drug cases pending in the courts of New York, the legislature passed a law to permit the creation of Special Narcotics Parts to handle only narcotics cases. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss his indictment, and argued that the legislative scheme adopted was unconstitutional. Defendant withdrew his motion to suppress a statement when the prosecution declared it would not use the statement. The court ruled that the motion to dismiss must be denied. The court held that the legislative authority for the adoption of the narcotics court system was a general law, not a local law, even though by virtue of population it only applied to New York City. The law uniformly applied to every citizen within the jurisdictions affected. The court rejected the argument that the scheme denied a right to trial by a jury of a defendant's peers, noting that any number of cases are routinely tried on motions to change venue, without damage to the defendant's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss his indictment for lack of jurisdiction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Special Narcotics Parts -- article 5-B of Judiciary Law establishing Special Narcotics Parts of Supreme Court in cities with population of one million or more is not unconstitutional as local law -- Grand Jury of Special Narcotics Parts, which in practice is New York County Grand Jury has power to indict for narcotics violations in any county without violating Constitution -- trial by petit jury in county other than that in which act was done does not deny defendant right to trial by jury of peers -- section 177-a of Judiciary Law does not preclude hearing cases commenced prior to September, 1972.

1. In April, 1972, the Legislature enacted article 5-B of the Judiciary Law establishing Special Narcotics Parts of the Supreme Court, in cities with a population of one million or more. Article 5-B does not violate section 17 of article III of the State Constitution which prohibits the Legislature from passing a private or local bill providing for changes of venue in civil or criminal cases. It is not a private bill changing [***2] venue. It applies uniformly to all narcotics defendants, rather than to a particular defendant or case. The reference to population in article 5-B does not designate and identify the place affected. It defines the problem in terms of population. Article 5-B is not a local law. The class, based on population, is reasonable because of the problems peculiar to the City of New York. The consolidation of narcotics prosecutions in the City of New York requires the utilization of manpower across county lines and it is the only such city. The Legislature's action to solve the problem does not constitute a local law.

2. The Grand Jury of the Special Narcotics Parts, which in practice is a New York County Grand Jury, is properly empowered to indict for narcotics offenses in any county. The procedure for Grand Jury presentment set forth in subdivision (iii) of section 177-d of the Judiciary Law is proper exercise of legislative power. Nothing in section 6 of article I of the State Constitution or the Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution mandates that a crime must be presented to a Grand Jury in the county where the crime was committed, or prohibits the Legislature from [***3] passing a law which directs that the offender be tried in a county other than that in which the act was done.

3. Trial by a petit jury of a county other than that in which the act was done does not deny defendant the right to a trial by a jury of his peers.

4. Section 177-a of the Judiciary Law does not preclude the court from hearing and determining cases commenced prior to September, 1972, when the Special Narcotics Courts were to be established.

Sundram v. Niagara Falls


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Niagara County


77 Misc. 2d 1002; 357 N.Y.S.2d 943; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1236



July 10, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant instituted an action seeking a judgment declaring Niagara Falls, N.Y., Ordinances ch. 365, § 16(e) unconstitutional because it required an applicant for a taxicab driver's license to be a United States citizen. The applicant also sought an order directing respondent City of Niagara Falls to issue the license.


OVERVIEW: The applicant, an Indian national and a permanent resident alien, applied for a taxicab driver's license, but the City denied the application because the applicant was not a United States citizen. In his lawsuit, the applicant alleged that the citizenship requirement in Niagara Falls, N.Y., Ordinances ch. 365, § 16(e) denied him equal protection of the laws. The court held that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The court found that the Fourteenth Amendment protected all persons and was not confined to citizens. Moreover, even though the City could establish reasonable classifications regarding the licensing of those individuals involved in occupations affecting the public interest, the court found that the City failed to justify the classification of applicants for taxicab driver's licenses into citizens and non-citizens. The court found that the City's fear that non-citizen applicants would be more inclined to be involved in criminal activities did not justify the classification.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the ordinance requiring the applicant for a taxicab driver's license to be a United States citizen was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- non-citizens -- licenses -- taxicab driver's license cannot be denied to alien resident, merely because he is not citizen.

An ordinance requiring that taxicab drivers be United States citizens is unconstitutional and void, when applied to an alien who is a permanent resident. The United States Constitution (14th Amdt.) gives "any person * * * the equal protection of the laws".

 

1547 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


North Syracuse v. County Legislature of County of Onondaga


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Onondaga County


74 Misc. 2d 842; 346 N.Y.S.2d 439; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1757



July 9, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county legislature submitted for court approval a redistricting plan. Three individual plaintiffs asked the court to adopt a weighted voting scheme. Plaintiff-intervenor village submitted its own plan for approval.


OVERVIEW: A county submitted for court approval a redistricting plan that contained 24 legislative districts. Three individuals argued that the plan exceeded the permissible population deviation and was unconstitutional; they proposed a weighted voting scheme as a temporary measure for the upcoming elections. A village submitted a similar plan, with the exception that the boundaries of two legislative districts were altered to include the entire village within one district rather than, as in the county plan, within two districts. The court rejected the weighted voting plan and the village plan, and adopted the county plan in its entirety. The court rejected the argument that the 9.02 percent variation between the largest and smallest districts was constitutionally impermissible. Larger deviations had been previously approved in decisions dealing with local legislative bodies. Courts allowed a broader scope of permissible deviations in state apportionment cases than in federal apportionment cases and had a more tolerant attitude toward the practical justification for such deviations. This tolerance was even greater when courts dealt with local, intrastate legislative bodies.


OUTCOME: The court adopted, approved, and imposed by judicial decree the county plan as the districting plan for the upcoming elections of county legislators and as the interim districting plan for the county beginning the following year, to be effective until it was superseded or adopted as the permanent plan.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of County Legislature -- plan of reapportionment of County Legislature providing for 24 districts and population deviation between largest and smallest districts of 9.02% is constitutionally valid -- village located in two towns seeks placement of village in one district which County Legislature rejected by adhering to town lines; no valid reason offered for changing Legislature's policy by judicial flat -- no support exists for weighted voting plan and it is rejected -- County Legislature's plan is adopted for 1973 elections and as final plan on stated conditions.

1. A plan of reapportionment for the Onondaga County Legislature proposed by such Legislature provides for 24 legislative districts. The largest district has a population 3.96% over the mean district population and the smallest district is 5.06% under the mean. The total deviation between the largest and smallest districts is 9.02%. Such deviation and the average deviation of 2.67% is within constitutionally permissible limits.

2. The Village of North Syracuse lies in two towns. Under the County Legislature's plan, town boundary lines are adhered to so that the portion of [***2] the village lying in one town is placed in one district and the portion lying in the other town is in a second district. The Village of North Syracuse objects to such division claiming that the whole of the village be in one district. The County Legislature considered the village's proposal and rejected it. No valid reason for not accepting the decision of the County Legislature has been offered. To override the policy of the Legislature would be an unwarranted act of judicial interference in a legislative function.

3. Individual plaintiffs ask for a weighted voting plan. The computer analysis that would be required has not been done. No reason for discarding the County Legislature's plan for a weighted voting scheme has been suggested. Weighted voting is rejected.

4. The County Legislature's plan is adopted as the interim districting plan for the 1973 elections and under stated conditions as the final districting plan.

Bevan v. New York State Teachers' Retirement System


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


74 Misc. 2d 443; 345 N.Y.S.2d 921; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1775; 7 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 74; 7 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9195



July 2, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion of the plaintiff for a preliminary injunction is denied as moot. The motions of the defendants are denied, all without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teacher brought an action against defendant state teachers retirement system seeking an injunction restoring him to his former position with back pay, and the system moved to dismiss the complaint arguing that involuntary retirement was proper due to the teacher's blindness.


OVERVIEW: A tenured elementary school teacher took extended sick leave due to vision problems and eventually became blind. After completing a rehabilitation program he sought to return to work; a medical examination determined that he was capable of working with limitations. The board of education, however, placed him on disability retirement. The teacher argued that involuntary retirement violated his constitutional rights because he had not been afforded a hearing. The retirement system claimed that a hearing was merely a formality because the teacher's blindness was not disputed. The court held that N.Y. Educ. Law § 511(1) violated due process to the extent that it permitted enforced retirement of a tenured teacher without a hearing. The teacher's blindness did not render the hearing a formality because the issue was physical capacity to teach, not blindness. Under N.Y. Educ. Law § 3004 blindness was not per se disabling. The teacher was entitled to reinstatement to his former position together with all back pay and benefits.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the teacher reinstated to his former position and denied his motion for an injunction as moot. The retirement system's motion to dismiss was denied.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- teachers -- blind, tenured teacher cannot be compelled to retire, without adversary hearing.

So much of subdivision 1 of section 511 of the Education Law as purports to authorize the involuntary retirement of a blind, tenured teacher, without an adversary hearing, is unconstitutional and void (cf. Education Law, § 3004). The court directs that the blind teacher be reinstated with back pay.
