C.D.M. Products, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


76 Misc. 2d 369; 350 N.Y.S.2d 500; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1491



June 29, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, plaintiffs' motion is in all respects denied, and the cross motion is granted to the extent of directing judgment in favor of defendants declaring that Local Law No. 9 and the rules and regulations promulgated pursuant thereto are in all respects constitutional and valid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, corporation and co-partnership, brought an action against defendants, city and police commissioner, questioning the constitutionality of New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 9 (1973). They sought a temporary injunction to restrain defendants from interfering with their operations. Defendants sought dismissal, and a declaration that the law, rules and regulations thereunder were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs manufactured hand guns and component parts for firearms. After the enactment of certain regulations, they were, inter alia, told to obtain licenses pursuant to the regulations. They brought an action against the city questioning the constitutionality and validity of New York City, N,.Y., City Local Laws No. 9 (1973). Plaintiffs sought a judgment declaring either that their activities were exempt from the subject law or, in the alternative, that the law, rules and regulations thereunder were unconstitutional. They also sought to enjoin the city from enforcing or attempting to enforce the ordinance and regulations against them. Defendants sought to have the action dismissed and to have the law declared constitutional. The court denied plaintiffs' request, noting that, inter alia, there was no showing of any direct restraint on interstate commerce, nor any showing that the legislation would in any way discriminate against those engaged in interstate commerce. The regulations applied equally and imposed the same requirements on every dealer and manufacturer of handguns in the city, whether his business was interstate or intrastate.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiffs' motion, and granted the city's cross-motion.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- city's gun control law -- New York City validly and constitutionally regulates, licenses and controls wholesale manufacturers of firearms, wholesale and retail dealers in firearms, assemblers of firearms, and gunsmiths.

The City of New York validly and constitutionally regulates, licenses and controls wholesale manufacturers of firearms, wholesale and retail dealers in firearms, assemblers of firearms, and gunsmiths (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 436-6.0 et seq., as amd. by Local Law No. 9 of 1973). The local law, and the rules and regulations published and promulgated thereunder by the city's Police Commissioner, are based on established guidelines and they comply with due process of law. They are not confiscatory. They do not burden interstate commerce unreasonably; moreover, Congress has expressly stated its intent not to pre-empt this field of [***2] regulation (U. S. Code, tit. 18, § 927). In this action against the city by Federally licensed manufacturers of firearms, judgment is directed declaring the local law and regulations valid and constitutional.

 

1550 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Will of McCarthy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Allegany County


75 Misc. 2d 193; 347 N.Y.S.2d 490; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1776



June 29, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The executor of the estate and beneficiaries sought construction of the decedent's will.


OVERVIEW: The decedent made bequests, with conditional reversionary interests to several charitable organizations, which were found to be unincorporated parts of other entities. The decedent also made substitute gifts in two codicils, which did not mention the restrictions. The court held that the bequests were gifts upon restriction consistent with the nature of the gifts as allowed by N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law §§ 6-4.5, 6-4.6, and 6-3.2(a)(1)(B), (C), and not charitable trusts. The court held that the remaindermen would be entitled to assert their interests under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 6-5.1, if the conditions that would have given rise to those interests were met. Because the organizations to which the bequests were made were parts of other entities, the court held that the testator intended the gifts to go to the actual legal entities. Since the codicils, which made substitute gifts, did not speak to the restrictions contained in the will, the court determined that the testator intended for the restrictions to apply to the substitute gifts.


OUTCOME: The court construed the testator's will.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Wills -- construction -- bequests "in trust" for masses are outright gifts, with such restriction enforceable by Attorney-General -- bequests to seminaries, convents, etc., shall be paid to incorporated institutions of which they are part -- legacy to "the Mother Superior of St. Francis Hospital * * * in trust" shall be paid to Mother Superior of incorporated institution of which said hospital is part, in trust for said hospital -- in codicil, testator stated that he had sold certain bequeathed stock and was substituting certain other stock; this was impliedly on same restriction as in will -- named legatees of shares of stock in corporations which subsequently merged or split their stock shall be given such new shares and such stock splits.

1. Testator bequeathed shares of stock of Argo Oil Corporation and of various other oil corporations to several different religious and charitable institutions. The following bequest is typical: "to Christ the King Seminary, St. Bonaventure, New York * * * in trust, nevertheless, the income from which is to be the sole property of Christ the King Seminary and some of the Priests at said Seminary shall say at least one mass a month for [***2] [testator] and [his wife], as long as said income from said stock is paid to the said Seminary". The will was made in 1956, and codicils in 1961 and 1965, and testator died in 1969. These bequests are outright gifts, with a restriction which is enforceable by the State Attorney-General (EPTL [eff. Setp. 1, 1967] 1-2.15; 6-3.2, subd. [a], par. [1], cls. [B], [C]; 6-4.5; 6-4.6; 6-5.1; 8-1.1; 8-1.4).

2. "Christ the King Seminary" is not a legal entity, but is the school of Theology of St. Bonaventure University. The bequest shall be paid to said university and shall be applied by said university to the benefit of its said school of theology.

3. Similarly, a bequest to "St. Elizabeth's Convent" shall be paid to the Franciscan Sisters of Allegany, New York, Inc., and shall be applied by said religious corporation to the benefit of its present St. Elizabeth's Residence.

4. Similarly, a bequest of $ 500 to "Missionary Poor Chares of St. Bonaventure, New York" shall be paid to a New Jersey religious corporation whose name is the Province of Immaculate Conception, and shall be expended by said corporation for the benefit of the Missionary Poor Clares, so called, at Christ [***3] the King Seminary at St. Bonaventure, New York.

5. A $ 1,000 legacy to "the Mother Superior of St. Francis Hospital * * * in trust * * * $ 100 of said bequest to be given to her each Christmas for 10 years" shall be paid to the Mother Superior of the Franciscan Sisters of Allegany, New York, Inc., as trustee, and shall be used by her for St. Francis Hospital.

6. In a codicil, testator stated that he had sold all of his Argo Oil stock and therefore he substituted, to the legatees of that stock, shares of Gulf Oil stock, Texaco Oil stock, Standard Oil of California stock, and Ohio Oil stock. Clearly, each of these substituted legacies was intended to be subject to the same restriction which testator had stated in the will (see headnote par. 1, above).

7. The named legatees of shares of stock in corporations which subsequently merged or changed their name or split their stock shall be given such new shares and such stock splits, but not any dividends which were declared before testator died.

 

1551 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Local 365 Cemetery Workers Green Attendants Bldg. Serv. Union-Emples. Int'l AFL-CIO


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Individual Calendar, New York County


76 Misc. 2d 148; 349 N.Y.S.2d 883; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1453; 85 L.R.R.M. 2029



June 21, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion for a temporary injunction is therefore granted. Submit order granting all the relief prayed for in the order to show cause, with leave to the defendants, however, to resettle the order with respect to any specific or particular provisions as may be deemed particularly inappropriate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff State sought a temporary injunction, pursuant to 1973 N.Y. Laws ch. 585, to prevent defendants, members of the cemetery workers union, from continuing a strike or lockout. The Governor had issued an executive order invoking the law. His order stated that a labor dispute existed that resulted in an actual threat to the health and welfare of state residents. The union contended that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The union contended that the law was, on its face and as applied, unconstitutional. It also contended that an emergency health condition did not factually exist. The court noted that the factual determination of an emergency was solely within the governor's power and that the court ought not to usurp his power. The grant of power to the governor was a valid emergency measure. In any event the evidence established that the governor had not abused the power granted to him, and that he had not acted arbitrarily or capriciously. The statute, as an emergency use of the state's police power did not facially offend the federal or state constitutions. The legislature could validly proclaim it to be the public policy of the state that, upon the occurrence of an emergency health hazard, cemetery workers could not continue a strike because their services were essential to the public health, welfare, and human dignity of the state's residents. The court noted that the union's conduct was an affront to the state's declared public policy that the body of a deceased person should be decently buried as soon as possible after death.


OUTCOME: The court granted the State's motion for a temporary injunction. The court ordered the State to submit an order granting all the relief prayed for in the order to show cause, with leave to the union, however, to resettle the order with respect to any specific or particular provisions as may be deemed particularly inappropriate.


HEADNOTES

Labor unions -- strike by cemetery workers -- statute (L. 1973, ch. 585) which provides for procedure to enjoin cemetery workers in New York City metropolitan area from striking is constitutional -- motion by Attorney-General for temporary injunction to enjoin continuing strike by cemetery workers is granted.

1. Chapter 585 of the Laws of 1973, which provides that the Attorney-General may apply for a temporary injunction against the commencement or continuation of any strike or lockout by cemetery workers upon the Governor's receipt of certifications of the Industrial Commissioner and the Commissioner of Health that there exists a labor dispute involving the cemeteries in the New York City metropolitan area which has not and cannot be resolved through authorized collective bargaining procedures and that such dispute has resulted in an actual threat to the health and welfare of [***2] the residents of the State, is constitutional.

2. The Governor, having been satisfied that there is a health hazard and having invoked the provisions of this act, and there being sufficient evidence to establish that the Governor has not abused this emergency use of the police power, the motion by the Attorney-General for a temporary injunction to enjoin the continuing strike by the cemetery workers is granted.

 

1552 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Turner v. Rochester Gas & Electric Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


74 Misc. 2d 745; 345 N.Y.S.2d 421; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1804



June 20, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff customers filed a motion for a preliminary injunction to enjoin defendant public utility from discontinuing gas and electric services to their premises during the pendency of their action, which sought a declaratory judgment and a permanent injunction on the grounds that N.Y. Transp. Corp. Law § 15 violated the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The customers contended that the statute was unconstitutional because it did not provide an opportunity for a hearing before an impartial hearing officer prior to the discontinuance of utility service. The court refused to grant the preliminary injunction because it found that the customers were not likely to ultimately succeed in their action as they failed to demonstrate that they were denied due process. The court did not determine whether public utility's discontinuance of service was state action, and found that the threat of termination of utility services for nonpayment involved none of the special irreversible economic hardships requiring prior judicial proceedings, as would wage garnishment or termination of public assistance payments. Even assuming a property right, the customer did not show that they or anyone else had been denied due process because there was no indication that the company's procedures were not fundamentally fair. The court found the public utility's shutoff notice went beyond the notice required by N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 16, §143.2 as it included an invitation to the customer to advise the public utility why the bill had not been paid.


OUTCOME: The court denied the customer's motion for a temporary injunction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Public utilities -- discontinuance of service -- customers of utility who do not pay bills and have gas and electric service discontinued have no property right in continued service within Due Process Clause of Constitution so as to require hearing before discontinuance -- assuming that there is property right, due process is satisfied by notice that service will be discontinued if bill is not paid which utility did here -- motion for temporary injunction is denied.

1. Plaintiffs, customers of a gas and electric utility, who, because they did not pay their bills after notice was given them, had their gas and electric service discontinued, do not have a property right, within the meaning of the Due Process Clause of the Federal Constitution, in the continuation of utility services, and thus requiring prior judicial proceedings before termination. The threat of termination of utility services for nonpayment involves no special irreversible economic hardships. Plaintiffs can avoid any grievous loss by paying the amount, if disputed.

2. Assuming that the continuation of utility services rises to the status of a property right within the Due Process Clause of the Constitution,  [***2] the only process due is not a hearing before an impartial hearing officer, but notice before termination and an opportunity to present to the utility any evidence the customer may have, if he claims that his bill has been paid. Here the notice sent by the utility complied with these requirements. Moreover, neither plaintiff disputes the unpaid bill the utility submitted.

3. Since plaintiffs have failed to demonstrate that they have been denied due process, there is little likelihood that they will succeed in the action and their motion for a temporary injunction is denied.

 

1553 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lazarus v. Yorkview Theater Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


74 Misc. 2d 729; 345 N.Y.S.2d 413; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1810



June 19, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a theatre and others, filed a motion to dismiss the action filed against them by plaintiff district attorney that sought to enjoin them from the sale, distribution, further distribution or acquisition, or possession with the state of a motion picture. The action specifically sought to enjoin them from exhibiting the picture within the county.


OVERVIEW: The district attorney charged defendants with criminal violations and then filed an action seeking to enjoin them from the sale, distribution, further distribution or acquisition, or possession with the state of a certain motion picture entitled "The Devil in Miss Jones." The action specifically sought to enjoin them from exhibiting the picture within the county. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the action, arguing that there were violations of the First, Fifth, and Fourteenth Amendment against them. Defendants also asked for a stay until the criminal matters were determined because a conviction could cause the picture to be outlawed as contraband. The court denied the motion, holding that there were no constitutional violations and that a preliminary injunction could be granted where defendants were doing or threatening to do an act which would violate a law. The court also denied a stay, holding that it would be futile. A trial conviction would only result in penal sanctions against the individual defendants.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion for a stay and its motion to dismiss an action against it.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- injunction -- obscene motion picture -- in action to enjoin distribution of obscene motion picture, CPLR 6330 is constitutional -- "affidavit-brief" disapproved as basis for setting forth facts on motion -- claim of another action pending is rejected -- argument that constitutional violations exist is specious -- argument that complaint does not allege "immediate and irreparable" injury is untenable -- stay until criminal matters are determined would be futile.

1. In an action under CPLR 6330 to enjoin defendants from selling and distributing a motion picture alleged to be obscene, CPLR 6330 is constitutional.

2. On a motion to dismiss the complaint, an "affidavit-brief" is unsatisfactory as a basis for setting forth facts to support the motion and is disapproved.

3. There are criminal prosecutions pending arising out of the same motion picture. However, only one defendant is the same. The claim that there is another action pending is rejected.

4. An argument that there are First, Fifth and Fourteenth Amendment violations is specious. Plaintiff in this action is not required to call individual defendants, including officers of corporate defendant [***2] in order to proceed, nor do they have to testify. The defense -- that redeeming social values outweigh the obscenity -- requires expert testimony.

5. The argument that the complaint does not allege "immediate and irreparable injury" is untenable ( CPLR 6301). The question of irreparable or immediate injury relates to a temporary restraining order which has not been granted.

6. A stay of the action until the criminal matters be determined would be futile.

 

1554 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Larocca v. Bronstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


74 Misc. 2d 488; 346 N.Y.S.2d 51; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1816



June 15, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is therefore granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee applied for a judgment pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to vacate and annul a determination by respondent Civil Service Commission of the City of New York, which denied his claimed veterans' preference credits on a competitive civil service promotion examination.


OVERVIEW: The employee worked for the city and took a competitive examination for promotion. He claimed veteran's preference credit based on his active military service. The eligibility list for examination at first ranked him upon recognition of his veteran's preference credit. On a revised list, he was denied the veteran's credit. He sought to overturn the decision on the basis of his service in the navy on active duty during time of war, pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6 and N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 85. On appeal, the Commission claimed that N.Y. Gen. Const. Law § 13-a barred the granting of the preferential status. The court reversed the denial of the preference and held that status as a veteran was established by honorable active federal service in some branch of the armed services during time of war and that the Commission acted in an arbitrary, capricious, and illegal manner in denying the preference. There was no difference in the nomenclature used by the armed services classifying the employee's six months' continuous duty, in time of war, as active duty or active duty for training. In either use, his normal civilian life and employment were disrupted for this extended period.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petitioner's request for judgment.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- veteran's preference -- Naval Air Reserve veteran who served on active duty in Tennessee for six months during Vietnam war is entitled to 2 1/2 points additional credit on civil service promotion examination.

Petitioner entered the United States Naval Air Reserve in Brooklyn, N. Y., in March, 1963 and was ordered to active duty and basic training in Tennessee on May 8, 1963, and there he continued on active duty and in training in aviation electronics until November, 1963, when he was ordered to return to the United States Naval Air Station in Brookyln, N. Y. where, on November 7, 1963 he was released to inactive duty. He was honorably discharged on March 26, 1971. He is employed in the civil service of the City of New York. On a competitive promotion examination, he is entitled to an additional credit of 2 1/2 points (N. Y. Const., art. V, § 6; art. I, § 11; Civil Service Law, § 85, subd. 1, par. [c]; § 85, subd. 2; cf. General Construction Law, § 13-a).

 

1555 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York State Soc. of Medical Masseurs, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


74 Misc. 2d 573; 345 N.Y.S.2d 866; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1829



June 14, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion for a preliminary injunction is granted and the cross motion for dismissal is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff masseurs filed an action against defendant city seeking injunctive and declaratory relief to prevent enforcement of N.Y. Local Law No. 11 (1973).


OVERVIEW: The masseurs claimed that the local law which required licenses for massage parlors and which broadly defined "massage" and "massage institute" was unconstitutional. The local law was so broad as to essentially prohibit any touching more intimate than a brief handshake at any location other than a medical facility. The masseurs sought an injunction to prevent enforcement of the local law and to have it declared unconstitutional. The court granted the masseurs' request for injunctive relief, finding the law unreasonably restrictive of the privileges and immunities of the citizenry. The court held it void on its face because no standards were set out for enforcement of the law, which left the law open to discriminatory enforcement.


OUTCOME: The masseurs' motion for a preliminary injunction was granted, and the local law was declared void and unconstitutional on its face.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- licenses -- massage parlors -- Local Law No. 11 of 1973 of City of New York is apparently unconstitutional; city is preliminarily enjoined from enforcing it.

Local Law No. 11 of 1973 of the City of New York is intended to render illegal, under penalty of fines and imprisonment, unlicensed "massage parlors", on the ground that they foster prostitution and pornography; but the local law's excessively broad wording and its lack of defined standards for judging violations make it readily misusable for arbitrary and unreasonable abridgments of the constitutional privileges and immunities of ordinary law-abiding citizens. The city is preliminarily enjoined from enforcing this apparently unconstitutional local law.

 

1556 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Cromwell v. Warden


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


74 Misc. 2d 642; 345 N.Y.S.2d 381; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1833



June 13, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator inmates filed separate applications for writs of habeas corpus that were heard together regarding their confinement in a reformatory, the denial of the benefits of rehabilitative programs, and the deprivation of the benefit of good behavior time as a consequence of being confined under a reformatory sentence.


OVERVIEW: The inmates contended that (1) no part of the complex they were confined to met the requirements of a reformatory; thus, their confinement was not in accordance with the sentences imposed upon them; (2) the reformatory sentence imposed entitled them to the benefit of certain rehabilitative programs, which they were denied; (3) confinement under a reformatory sentence deprived them of the benefit of good behavior time which those serving indeterminate or definite sentences could earn under the provisions of N.Y. Penal Law § 70.30(4). The court held that (1) the issue of whether the complex met the requirements of a reformatory was not in a position to permit disposition on the merits because the inmates failed to comply with the primary requirements of due process, i.e., to name some state official as a party and to give notice to the office of the Attorney-General; (2) the programs available to the inmates that were transferred to the correctional institution were substantially the same programs as those available in the reformatory; and (3) to the extent that the inmates had earned the benefit of good behavior time, they were entitled to credit therefor.


OUTCOME: The court held that the inmates were entitled to credit for good behavior time to the extent that they had earned such benefit and that the inmates' argument that the complex they were confined to did not meet the requirements of a reformatory was not in a position to permit disposition on the merits.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Prisons and prisoners -- good time -- imposition of extended reformatory sentence on relators carrying finding that reform is possible does not violate due process -- since State Commission of Correction provisionally certified reformatory, and neither it nor any individual State officer is party, certification cannot be reviewed -- evidence of rehabilitation programs at reformatory meets constitutional standards -- transfer of relators after they attained age 21 to correctional institution can be justified only on basis of waiver signed by four and waivers may be revoked requiring transfer back to reformatory -- denial of good behavior time to defendants sentenced to reformatory is unconstitutional; Commissioner of Corrections must compute such time if earned.

1. Relators were sentenced to a reformatory and after attaining age 21 and being found to have violated their parole, chose to stay in a correctional institution rather than in a reformatory. A reformatory sentence is extended because there is no opportunity to reduce the time by credit for good time. Two relators were convicted of misdemeanors but nevertheless given the extended reformatory sentence. The imposition [***2] of an extended sentence carries with it a finding that reformation is possible and therefore violated no concept of due process or equal protection of said two relators.

2. The Rikers Island reformatory was provisionally certified by the State Commission of Correction. Since relators attack such certification, their failure to name a State officer as a party and to give notice to the office of the Attorney-General deprived the Commission of Correction of the opportunity to submit evidence to justify its grant of conditional certification. The ruling of the State commission cannot be reviewed in this proceeding.

3. Relators were sentenced to the city reformatory for the purpose of rehabilitation. The evidence shows numerous programs at the city reformatory including educational, vocational, work release, and VISTA programs and others. Even though the level is not equal to that of the State, it meets the constitutional standards. Relators who are housed in the correctional institution also have these programs available.

4. The State law empowers the State Commissioner of Correctional Services to transfer inmates from one institution to another but similar power to the City Commissioner [***3] does not exist. The sole authority for the transfers to the correctional institution are waivers signed by four relators. While this is sufficient to sustain the transfers, the waiver is revocable and should any of these four indicate a desire to revoke, the City Commissioner is directed to transfer him to the reformatory.

5. Good behavior time is granted against the maximum term of an indeterminate sentence (not exceeding one third of the maximum sentence ) and up to one sixth of a definite sentence. A reformatory sentence does not fit into either of these categories and those so sentenced are the only persons under the statute who are not entitled to good behavior time. There is no rational basis for this distinction and the denial of good time is unconstitutional. Relators are entitled to good behavior time if they have earned it but if their records show that they have not, it will be denied. Accordingly, the New York City Commissioner of Correction is directed to forthwith examine into the institutional records of relators for the purpose of determining whether those records disclose warrant for administrative proceedings looking to a forfeiture of good behavior time.

Schulman v. New York City Health & Hospitals Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


75 Misc. 2d 150; 346 N.Y.S.2d 920; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1835



June 12, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion on remand is granted and judgment is awarded to the petitioners.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought review of a judgment from an Appellate Division (New York), which reversed and remanded a judgment, which had been in their favor, in their action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 against respondents seeking a judgment annulling the requirement that a fetal death certificate include the name and address of the aborting patient.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners brought an action seeking a judgment annulling the requirement that a fetal death certificate shall include the name and address of the aborting patient. The court entered judgment in their favor and the appellate court reversed and remanded. Upon remand, the court reaffirmed its prior decision and entered judgment in favor of petitioners. The court held that a woman's right to privacy was violated by the requirement that her name be disclosed as an abortion patient on a fetal death certificate. The court held that the disclosure of her name furthered no compelling interest on the part of the state. The court held that the post factor regulation respecting disclosure of an abortion patient's name as part of a fetal death certificate could not add to the state's knowledge or ability to implement and to protect its vital interests.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment in favor of petitioners.


HEADNOTES

Health -- identification of aborted patient -- unless patient permits disclosure, physician who performs abortion must not, and cannot be compelled by law to, fill in patient's name on fetal death certificate.

Unless the patient waives the confidential privilege, a physician who has performed an abortion must refuse to fill in the patient's name on the certificate of the fetal death. Any statutes or regulations requiring this item of information are an unnecessary and unconstitutional invasion of the patient's right of privacy.

 

1558 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Einhorn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Narcotics Courts, New York County


75 Misc. 2d 183; 346 N.Y.S.2d 986; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1843



June 11, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a pharmacist who was charged with the sale of methaqualone, filed an omnibus motion alleging that the procedure for making the sale of methaqualone a felony was an unconstitutional delegation of the legislative power, that the search warrant was overbroad, and that he was entitled to a bill of particulars revealing the purchasers' names.


OVERVIEW: The sale of methaqualone had been increased to a felony offense by the action of the State Commissioner of Health pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3372. The court held that this was not an unconstitutional delegation of the legislative function to the commissioner, because in listing methaqualone as a dangerous drug the commissioner was performing a ministerial function based on the results of tests proving that methaqualone was a dangerous drug. The court next held that it was no defense that the pharmacist was unaware that the degree of the offense had been increased. The court agreed, however, that a search warrant authorizing the seizure of everything except aspirin and toothpaste, as the court put it, was tantamount to a general warrant that was proscribed by the Fourth Amendment. The court also held that while a bill of particulars could not be sought simply to fish for evidence, it was proper to disclose the names of all purchasers of the drug because as a pharmacist defendant had a lawful right to dispense the drug to persons with prescriptions.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to inspect grand jury minutes relating to the delegation of legislative power, granted the motion to suppress evidence seized in the search of his pharmacy, and granted the motion for a bill of particulars.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- drugs and druggists -- immaterial that pharmacist did not know that offense of selling certain dangerous drugs without prescriptions had been raised from misdemeanor to felony by State Commissioner of Health's duly filed designation of such drugs -- no evidence that defendant has been prejudiced by delay between his sales and his indictment -- no warrant is needed to overhear telephone conversations with consent of sender -- warrant to search pharmacy and seize all dangerous drugs and records is too broad; all items thus seized shall be suppressed -- indicted pharmacist is entitled to names of persons to whom he allegedly sold dangerous drugs without prescriptions.

1. An indictment of a pharmacist properly alleged that, on or about March 1, 1973, in 10 instances he sold drugs without the requisite prescriptions and thus committed 10 violations of the Education Law as misdemeanors, and that, in the 11th instance, he sold methaqualone without the requisite prescription and thus committed one Class D felony (Penal Law, § 220.30; § 220.00, subd. 4; Public Health Law, former § 3371, subd. 1; former § 3372). The State Commissioner of Health properly filed a designation [***2] or order with the Secretary of State on February 9, 1973 listing methaqualone as a controlled dangerous depressant drug (10 NYCRR former 81.47 [c] [1]). This filing lawfully increased the offense of selling methaqualone from a misdemeanor to a felony (Penal Law, § 10.00, subd. 1; § 15.20, subd. 2; § 220.30). It is immaterial that the pharmacist did not know that his criminal offense had been increased to a felony.

2. Defendant has not presented any evidence that he has been prejudiced by the delay which occurred between the dates when he committed the crimes and the date when he was indicted and arrested therefor.

3. A telephone conversation which was overheard or recorded with the consent of the sender is not "intercepted" and hence no eavesdropping warrant is needed ( CPL 700.05, subd. 3). The admissibility in evidence of the conversation will be for the Trial Judge to determine.

4. A warrant to search and seize, from a pharmacist's drugstore, "narcotic, depressant and stimulant drugs, dangerous drugs, controlled drugs, invoices * * * records * * * books * * * correspondence * * * cancelled checks, journals, ledgers and other business records * * * relating to the purchase,  [***3] sale and distribution of [such] drug", is much too broad (U. S. Const., 4th Amdt.; CPL 690.10), and therefore all items and papers thus seized shall be suppressed from evidence.

5. Since the indicted pharmacist may assert as a defense that he lawfully sold the drugs pursuant to prescriptions (Public Health Law, former § 3381, subd. 1, par. [a]), he is entitled to a bill of particulars of the names of the persons to whom he allegedly sold the drugs without any prescription.

 

1559 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Malek v. Cruz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


74 Misc. 2d 448; 345 N.Y.S.2d 367; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2351



June 10, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord brought a summary proceeding for nonpayment of rent against respondent tenant.


OVERVIEW: After the tenant failed to pay his rent, the landlord brought a summary proceeding against him for the nonpayment of rent. The tenant asserted defenses and counterclaims. The court dismissed the counterclaims of intentional failure to maintain the premises in a habitable condition and willful breach of the warranty of habitability because there was no evidence to support the alleged acts and damages. The court awarded a judgment to the landlord for unpaid rent because (1) the nonpayment proceeding was a proper use of summary proceeding, (2) the technical defenses concerning the petition were without foundation, (3) the evidence did not support the defenses of constructive and actual eviction, (4) the court denied the tenant's motion to assert an additional defense under N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 302-a, and (5) the parties did not dispute the amount of unpaid rent.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment awarding back rent to the landlord.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and tenant -- summary proceedings -- rent administratorship and mortgage foreclosure receivership of multiple dwelling having terminated, landlord may institute nonpayment summary dispossess proceeding against tenant whose arrears were never collected -- landlord is granted judgment for such arrears and for dispossess -- execution of warrant of dispossess is stayed while tenant pays arrears at monthly rate along with regular rent; on 10 days' default, warrant shall be executed -- landlord has waived Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law ( § 732, subd. 2) which limits stay of warrant to five days -- landlord is making good effort to repair and maintain building; tenant's defenses and counterclaims, alleging nonhabitability and constructive eviction, are dismissed -- tenant has not deposited his arrears in court, hence his rent cannot be abated (cf. Multiple Dwelling Law, § 302-a) -- technical objections to landlord's petition are overruled.

1. An administratorship of this multiple dwelling (Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law, art. 7-a), and a mortgage foreclosure receivership have both been terminated, and the landlord is now in possession again. A tenant [***2] whose low income makes him practically judgment proof still owes uncollected rent for several months in 1970 and for 10 months in 1972 and for January through April of 1973. In this nonpayment summary dispossess proceeding, the landlord is granted judgment for the arrears and for a warrant of dispossess.

2. However, execution of the warrant is stayed so as to permit the tenant to pay the arrears at the rate of $ 13.50 a month, concurrently with his regular monthly rent, until the arrears have been paid in full. The warrant of eviction shall issue and shall be executed if a default by the tenant remains uncured after 10 days' notice and if an affidavit by the landlord or her attorney so attests.

3. The landlord has waived the benefit of the statute (Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law, § 732, subd. 2) which limits a stay of a warrant of dispossess to five days.

4. Although this building still needs many repairs and essential services, a recent report by the city's Department of Rent and Housing Maintenance states: "Good effort being made for 100% completion. Work in progress throughout bldg." After trial, the court dismisses this tenant's defenses and counterclaims which [***3] allege that the premises are not habitable or enjoyable and that he has been constructively or actually evicted.

5. Even if rent-impairing building violations have persisted for over six months, this tenant's rent cannot be abated, since he has not deposited his rent arrears in court (Multiple Dwelling Law, § 302-a; see, also, Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law, § 755).

6. The tenant's technical objections to the form of the landlord's petition are rejected by the court as being without foundation.
