State by Lefkowitz v. Colorado State Christian College of Church of Inner Power, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


76 Misc. 2d 50; 346 N.Y.S.2d 482; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1430



June 5, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the application by the Attorney-General for an injunction pursuant to subdivision (b) of section 349 of the General Business Law is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorney general sought an injunction pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 349 against defendant alleged college to enjoin defendant from conferring or offering to confer any academic or honorary degree to state citizens, from representing itself as a "college" in any degree, and from engaging in deceptive acts.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 349(a), plaintiff attorney general sought injunctive relief to prohibit defendant alleged college from conferring, advertising, and selling academic degrees to state residents, from representing itself as a "college," and from engaging in deceptive practices. The court granted plaintiff an injunction pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 349(b). The court held that defendant did not qualify as a "college" because it had no formal entrance requirements, no faculty members who were trained and competent to teach accredited and recognized academic courses, and no educational facilities. The court held that defendant transacted business as defined by N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 302(a)(1) because the totality of defendant's purposeful activity in the state included sales solicitation by mail addressed to the public and shipment in the course and conduct of business of at least one honorary doctorate degree to a purchaser in the state ordered pursuant to the solicitation. The court held that actual deception of defendant's advertising proved by deceived consumers was not necessary, as the capacity to deceive was the criterion by which the advertising was judged.


OUTCOME: Plaintiff attorney general's application for an injunction to enjoin defendant alleged college from selling and advertising academic degrees, representing itself as a "college", and engaging in deceptive business practices was granted on the grounds that defendant did not qualify as a college, actively solicited business and sold at least one honorary degree, and that defendant's advertising had the capacity to deceive state citizens.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions -- fraudulent or illegal business -- so-called "college" whose only business is selling Ph.D. degrees for $ 100 is enjoined from soliciting or selling in New York State (General Business Law, § 349) -- such defendant in Colorado "transacts any business" in New York State ( CPLR 302, subd. [a]).

1. A so-called college, located at a post office address in Colorado, which "college" has no students, no accredited faculty, no recognized curriculum, and no requirements other than $ 100 for an alleged Ph.D. degree, is enjoined (General Business Law, § 349) from representing itself as a college in any advertisement or communication addressed to any person in New York State, and from advertising or conferring or offering or selling any academic or honorary degree to any person in New York State (Education Law, § 224).

2. By the above acts, this alleged college "transacts any business within the state" of New York ( CPLR 302, subd. [a]) and hence our courts have jurisdiction over it.

 

1561 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Herrick v. Ingraham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Delaware County


74 Misc. 2d 234; 345 N.Y.S.2d 831; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1878



June 4, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner sought a declaration that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116 did not apply to the sales of real property made by the landowner. Alternatively, plaintiff sought a declaration that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116 was unconstitutional in a declaratory judgment action brought against defendant state commissioner of health.


OVERVIEW: The landowner purchased 13 farms and then sold off various portions of the farms for claimed recreational purposes. Most of the land was vacant. N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116 required approval by the department of health of subdivision plans and maps. The purpose of the statute was to assure adequate and satisfactory water supply and sewage facilities to residential lots. The state commissioner of health served a notice of hearing and complaint on the landowner, claiming violations of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116. The court held that the sales here did not require consideration of water or sewage. The sales were for recreational purposes only and there was no evidence of urbanization. The court would not blindly apply the words of a statute to arrive at an unreasonable or absurd result. The statute was too broadly drawn to include the instant case. The court further held that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116 was unconstitutional as so indefinite and vague. A reading of the statute in question did not enlighten the reader as to the terms "tract," "parcel," "subdivision," "residential lots," or "residential building plots."


OUTCOME: The court held that the related sections of the Public Health Law were unreasonable and unconstitutional as they were applied by the department of health to the sales of the landowners' property.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Health -- realty subdivision's water supply -- Public Health Law ( § 1116) does not apply to sales of 263 parcels of marginal farmlands to hunters, fishermen, skiers and vacationers -- in any event, statute is vague, unreasonable and unconstitutional as applied to such lands.

1. Plaintiff purchased 11 farms in Delaware County and 1 farm in Greene County, and 1 farm in Ulster County. These were marginal or unprofitable farms. Over the years, he sold 263 portions thereof, all of which were vacant land except those portions containing the original farmhouses or barns; that is, he sold 119 parcels each containing 10 or more acres, 90 parcels containing 5 to 10 acres each, 41 parcels of 2 to 5 acres each, and 13 parcels of less than 2 acres each. He sold these parcels as recreational land to hunters, fishermen, skiers, and vacationers. These lands did not constitute a "subdivision * * * tract of land * * * divided into five or more parcels * * * for sale * * * as residential lots" (Public Health Law, § 1115); and hence plaintiff was not required to file a plan or map and obtain the Health Department's approval of the "water supply * * * to said subdivision" (Public Health Law,  [***2] § 1116), under penalty of fines and/or imprisonment (Public Health Law, §§ 12, 12-b).

2. In any event, section 1116 of the Public Health Law, as applied to these lands by the Health Department, is so vague and unreasonable as to be unconstitutional.

 

1562 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Leland House, Inc. v. Antar


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


74 Misc. 2d 188; 344 N.Y.S.2d 661; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1899



May 25, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff housing company filed an action against defendant tenants for breach of lease when the tenants vacated the leased premises prior to the expiration of the lease term.


OVERVIEW: In four companion cases, the housing company sought retroactive rent and refused to credit rent security held on deposit. The court held that the legislative plan contained in the statutory provisions, authorizing a rent surcharge of up to a maximum of 50 percent of rent, appeared to be an appropriate equitable means of preventing the acute hardship of sudden eviction, especially under existing economic conditions in which tenants' incomes could vary greatly from year to year. The court also held that authority had to be delegated to a governmental agency to create rules for effectuating and implementing this public policy, and the rules were required to provide some means of procuring essential information as a reasonable guideline for imposition of any surcharge. The existing rules providing for income confirmation and surcharge procedure were a valid delegation and exercise of legislative authority. The legislative rationale and the expressed statutory purpose of the surcharge required tenant occupancy, since the surcharge was a statutory device to permit continued occupancy by a tenant after the tenant's income exceeded that statutory limit.


OUTCOME: The court awarded judgment to the housing company in the amount of the apartment rental loss, garage rental loss, and prorated damages for painting. The demand for the surcharge was not sustained. The tenants were credited with a setoff for rent security.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and tenant -- limited profit housing -- although tenant failed to fill in required annual income questionnaire, landlord accepted monthly rents until tenant moved; landlord cannot retroactively demand 50% monthly rent surcharge after tenant has moved out (Private Housing Finance Law, § 31, subd. 3) -- although lease prohibits any setoff of tenant's security deposit, judgment for landlord for monthly rents, until landlord re-rented apartment, must be reduced by amount of tenant's security deposit.

1. Against an apartment lessee who moved out, the landlord is entitled to judgment for the monthly rents for the three months which elapsed before the landlord re-rented the apartment, and for the prorated cost of repainting the apartment.

2. Although this lease prohibits any setoff of the tenant's security deposit against the landlord's claims, this provision cannot apply when all claims have matured, as in this case. Accordingly, the judgment for the landlord is reduced by the amount of the tenant's security deposit.

3. This apartment is in a limited profit housing development, but the tenant failed to fill in the required annual income questionnaire (Private Housing [***2] Finance Law, § 31, subd. 3). However, the landlord accepted the regular monthly rents until the tenant moved out, and only then did the landlord demand the 50% maximum monthly rent surcharge mentioned in the Rules and Regulations (art. III, § 3, subds. [a], [b]) of the New York City Housing and Development Administration (formerly the Housing and Redevelopment Board). The landlord cannot retroactively demand any such surcharge after a tenant has moved out. The landlord's claim for such surcharge is dismissed.

 

1563 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Weisenberg v. Dodd


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


74 Misc. 2d 311; 344 N.Y.S.2d 610; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1923



May 22, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate sought to compel respondent county board of elections to place all of the candidates that were running for city council designation on the same line and to have their respective positions on the ballot determined by drawing lots.


OVERVIEW: The candidate along with six others sought the nomination for one of five public offices on the city council. He properly filed a designating petition, which contained his name alone, with no other candidates for any public office or party position. The other candidates filed one petition collectively and their names were placed on a separate line from the candidate's. The court ordered the board of elections to conduct a drawing by lot to determine the relative positions on the primary ballot and to place the candidates' names on the same line in the order determined by lot. The court found that N.Y. Elec. Law § 104(2) directed the board of elections to determine the positions of the six candidates by lot and that there was no section that authorized the five other candidates to have been treated as a single entitle for the determination of their position on the ballot. The court found that if the other candidates were concerned that the placement might have confused the voters, then they could have taken their argument to the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court directed the board of elections to conduct a drawing by lot to determine the relative positions on the primary ballot of the six candidates and to place all of the names on the same line in the order determined by lot.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- position on voting machine -- in primary election for five City Councilmen, in which five candidates filed one designating petition and one candidate filed one designating petition, Board of Elections must draw by lot to determine relative position in which each of such six candidates is to appear on voting machines (Election Law, § 104, subd. 2).

In a Democratic Party primary election for five public offices of City Councilman to be filled at the general election, there was no designation by the regular Democratic Party organization, but five candidates filed one combined petition, and one candidate filed his separate petition. The Board of Elections must conduct a drawing by lot to determine the relative position in which each of the six candidates is to appear on line B of the voting machines (Election Law, § 104, subd. 2). The board cannot simply relegate the single candidate to line C.

 

1564 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ballentine v. Sugarman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


74 Misc. 2d 267; 344 N.Y.S.2d 39; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1969



May 1, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The Gotbaum petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a group of civil service employees and a group of home relief recipients, each brought an N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, which attacked N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 164, the "work relief" statute, and the actions of respondents, state and city officers and agencies, pursuant to § 164. The proceedings were dismissed as against the state, and the court addressed the claims of both groups against the city agencies and officials.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners contended that home relief recipients were being employed to perform work ordinarily performed by regular employees, and that they did so at a lower salary and without benefits enjoyed by regular employees. The court held that the work relief statute did not violate the constitutional requirement that appointments and promotions in the civil service be made according to merit and fitness because the assignment of the home relief recipients to work did not constitute such an appointment or promotion. The court determined that the statute did not prevent relief recipients from being used to do the same kind of civil service work as regular employees. The court found that the home relief recipients were not being unconstitutionally held in a state of peonage where the only sanction for their refusing to work was that they would not receive relief payments. The court noted that an issue of fact was presented as to whether the relief recipients were being paid at the requisite state minimum wage, but found that it would be impossible to conduct a hearing as to all 21,000 recipients. The court ordered a hearing as to the three recipients named in the petition.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition of the civil service employees. The court granted a hearing as to whether the home relief recipients named in the petition were being credited at rates that were in accordance with the quoted regulation, and dismissed the petition in all other respects.


HEADNOTES

Social services -- assignment of relief recipients to work -- statute providing for assigning by city of home relief recipients to work does not violate civil service provision of Constitution -- dismissal of civil servants to make way for relief recipients does not occur -- home relief recipients do not become peons by working -- compliance with State minimum wage or higher comparable pay is triable issue.

1. The assigning of employable home relief recipients to perform work on public work projects for the City of New York as required by section 164 of the Social Services Law, similar to work done by civil service employees but without salary or fringe benefits, does not constitute "appointments and promotions" in the civil service within section 6 of article V of the Constitution.

2. The work these relief recipients do is not work "ordinarily and actually performed by regular employees" (§ 164, subd. 2, par. [b]) and at a minimum, means dismissal of civil servants to make room for relief recipients which has not happened. The budgetary restrictions and the current job freeze in civil service did not result from these work relief [***2] statutes.

3. The status of home relief recipients is not peonage in violation of the Thirteenth Amendment; for choosing not to work, their only sanction is loss of current home relief payments.

4. Home relief recipients, not being civil service employees, are not entitled to all the benefits of civil service employees.

5. An issue of fact requiring trial is whether the city is complying with Social Services regulations by paying the State minimum wage or, if higher, at the wage paid to civil servants for comparable work.

 

1565 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Cooper


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Onondaga County


73 Misc. 2d 904; 342 N.Y.S.2d 995; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1975



April 30, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, as executors and trustees, brought an action for a determination relating to the proper payment of a renounced payment of $ 1,000 of the residuary a decedent's estate. Notice was given to respondents, the legatees, the grandchildren, and other interested parties.


OVERVIEW: The decedent left certain shares of his residuary estate outright to his daughters, in trust for his grandchildren. One daughter renounced $ 1,000 of her residuary bequest in accordance with the provisions of N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 3-3.10. N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 3-3.10, which became effective prior to his death, was applicable because no property right vested until the decedent died. Section 3-3.10 provided that because the decedent had not otherwise provided, the filing of the renunciation had the same effect with respect to the renounced interest as though the daughter had predeceased the testator. Because N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 3-3.3 subd. (a), par. (1) provided that the renouncing daughter was treated as predeceasing the decedent, the renounced bequest passed to the grandchildren. Therefore, the grandchildren were each entitled to one-half of the renounced amount. The amount was paid outright to the grandchildren and not in trust to the trustees, because the fact that the legacy passed in accordance with § 3-3.3 took the bequest out of the provisions of the will.


OUTCOME: The renounced amount was paid in equal amounts to the two grandchildren.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Wills -- renunciation of bequest -- children of daughter who renounced residuary bequest receive per stirpes, as if daughter had predeceased testator; statute which became law prior to testator's death but after execution of will governs since rights envisioned in will vest at time of death -- renounced bequest passes outright to children.

1. That part of a residuary bequest, and income therefrom, which was renounced by decedent's daughter should be paid outright to her two children. Under EPTL 3-3.3 (subd. [a], par. [1]), a bequest to a predeceased beneficiary vests per stirpes in the surviving issue. EPTL 3-3.10, which became law after the will was executed but before decedent's death and treats a renounced interest as though the renouncing person had predeceased the testator, applies to decedent's will, an ambulatory instrument vesting property rights at the date of death, not the date of execution.

2. Since the daughter's bequest was to pass outright, the children receive in the same fashion and not through the shares in trust created for them from part of the residuary estate.

 

1566 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kerr v. Urstadt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


73 Misc. 2d 953; 343 N.Y.S.2d 739; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1983



April 27, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Chapter 1012 of the Laws of 1971 is declared constitutional and the arbitrary determination of the respondent State Commissioners is vacated and annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, local public officials, applied pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for a judgment declaring that 1971 N.Y. Laws ch. 1012, amending the Local Emergency Housing Rent Control Act, 1962 N.Y. Laws ch. 21, § 1, subd. 5, unconstitutional, and annulling an order by respondents, the Commissioner of the New York State Division of Housing and Community Renewal and others, denying approval to Amendment No. 33 of local rent control regulations.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners amended the rent control regulations to provide for interim increases in rents in certain properties because implementation of a local law providing for the implementation of a maximum base rent (MBR) system which would provide relief to landlords had been continually delayed. Petitioners intended the amendment to provide interim relief to certain landlords pending the implementation of the new MBR system. However, respondents denied approval to the amendment, precipitating petitioners' art. 78 application seeking a declaration that 1971 N.Y. Laws ch. 1012 was unconstitutional and that respondents' action in denying approval was invalid. While agreeing that respondents' action was properly vacated, the court upheld the constitutionality of 1971 N.Y. Laws ch. 1012. It found that respondents' denial of approval was based on "suppositious effects of unknown numerical magnitude" resulting from respondents' analysis of the impact of the amendment. Because there was no factual support for respondents' determination, and therefore could not be founded on "good and valid reasons," it was vacated.


OUTCOME: The court vacated and annulled respondents' determination disapproving an amendment to the local rent control regulations implemented by petitioners. It also held that the challenged state law was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- Special Term annuls, as arbitrary, disapproval by State Commissioner of Housing and Community Renewal of maximum base rent Amendment No. 33 of New York City Rent, Eviction and Rehabilitation Regulations.

The maximum base rent (MBR) system for calculating and allowing or disallowing rent increases (N. Y. City Rent, Eviction and Rehabilitation Regulations [Amdt. No. 33, eff. Jan. 1, 1972], §§ 24, 25, 26, 33 [subd. e], 33.5, 33.6, 33.7, 33.8; Local Laws, 1970, No. 30 of City of New York; Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-5.0, subd. a, pars. [5], [6]; subd. g, par. [1]) is not "more stringent or restrictive" (L. 1971, ch. 1012) than prior rent regulations. The last-cited statute is constitutional; but the State Commissioner of Housing and Community Renewal's disapproval of the new system was not based on good and valid reasons but rather was arbitrary. Special Term of the Supreme Court ( CPLR art. 78) now vacates and annuls such arbitrary disapproval of said [***2] Amendment No. 33 of the New York City Rent, Eviction and Rehabilitation Regulations.

 

1567 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Mores v. Feel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


73 Misc. 2d 942; 343 N.Y.S.2d 220; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1990



April 25, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the respondent's contention that the dual Statute of Limitations as set forth in section 517 of the Family Court Act is unconstitutional is rejected and the motion to join the Commissioner of Social Services as copetitioner is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother commenced a paternity proceeding to procure an order declaring that respondent putative father was the natural father of her three children. Ten months after filing the action, the mother filed a motion to join the Commissioner of Social Service as a co-petitioner.


OVERVIEW: The father objected to joining the Commissioner as a co-petitioner on the basis that the 10-year statute of limitations provided in N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517(b) was unconstitutional under the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. Under § 517, a mother was limited to two years to bring a filiation action, while the Commissioner was limited to ten. Therefore, whether a putative father was subjected to either a two- or ten-year period of liability based solely upon the mother's income level at the time the action was brought. The court concluded that the dual statute of limitations provided in § 517 was reasonable when viewed in terms of the presumed legislative objective in enacting it, and that it was not arbitrary or invidious. As the 10-year limitation period incorporated some apparent degree of "reason" when viewed in terms of the presumed legislative purpose in enacting it, the statute also met minimal due process standards. The court noted, however, that the court was justified in dismissing a paternity petition on due process grounds in an exceptional case where a putative father was able to show "actual prejudice in preparing a defense."


OUTCOME: The court rejected the father's contention that the Act's dual statute of limitations was unconstitutional and granted the mother's motion to join the Commissioner as a co-petitioner.


HEADNOTES

Children born out of wedlock -- Statute of Limitations -- Family Court Act ( § 517) validly and constitutionally enables public welfare official to institute paternity proceeding within 10 years after birth of child, even though such petition by mother of child is barred by 2-year Statute of Limitations -- mother, represented by Corporation Counsel, is granted leave to join city's Commissioner of Social Services as copetitioner, even though 2 years have elapsed since birth.

1. The Family Court Act ( § 517) validly and constitutionally provides two different Statutes of Limitation, as follows: "(a) Proceedings to establish the paternity of the child may be instituted during the pregnancy of the mother or after the birth of the child, but shall not be brought after the lapse of more than two years from the birth of the child, unless paternity has been acknowledged by the father in writing or by furnishing support. (b) If the [***2] petitioner is a public welfare official, the proceeding may be originated not more than ten years after the birth of the child." This difference is not without some rational basis, and hence it does not violate constitutional due process of law or the equal protection of the laws.

2. In this paternity proceeding instituted by the mother of three children after the two-year limitation had run out as to two of them, she is represented by the city's Corporation Counsel and she alleges that the respondent has acknowledged his paternity. Respondent denies this. Though some 10 months have elapsed since she commenced this proceeding, she is granted leave to join the city's Commissioner of Social Services as copetitioner.

 

1568 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wilkerson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


73 Misc. 2d 895; 342 N.Y.S.2d 936; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1993



April 24, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed from an order of the Rochester City Court (New York) that dismissed an information against defendant charging him with violating City of Rochester Code § 44-12(A), which dealt with gambling activity.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with having been a keeper of a gambling establishment in violation of § 44-12(A). Defendant and others charged filed a motion to dismiss the informations on the ground that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The trial court ruled that the ordinance was unconstitutional in its entirety and granted the motion to dismiss. The court affirmed. It held that because New York had pre-empted the field of gambling, local laws on the same subject were prohibited regardless of whether or not they duplicated or otherwise conflicted with the New York law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing the information against defendant regarding illegal gambling activity.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- gambling -- Code of City of Rochester dealing with gambling which makes it crime to be casual player conflicts with State Penal Law -- local law, to extent that it includes location where casual player participates, also conflicts with State law -- State law pre -empts field of gambling and whole of local law is prohibited -- order of City Court dismissing information charging defendant with violation of subdivision A of section 44-12 of Code of City of Rochester is affirmed.

1. Chapter 44 of the Code of the City of Rochester deals with gambling and, since it creates criminal responsibility against a casual participant in a gambling game, is in conflict with the State Penal Law.

2. Section 44-12 of the Code forbids possession of the same kind of gambling equipment prohibited by section 225.30 of the Penal Law but the local law includes a location where the casual player as well as the professional gambler is playing. To that extent the local law further conflicts with State law.

3. Even though severance of the illegal portions of the local law would be permissible, the State Legislature by enacting antigambling laws has pre-empted the field and therefore all of [***2] the local law is thereby prohibited regardless of whether or not they duplicate or otherwise conflict with State law.

4. An order of the City Court of Rochester, insofar as it dismissed an information charging defendant with violating subdivision A of section 44-12 of the Code is affirmed. Since defendant was not charged with violating subdivisions C, D or E, the City Court had no authority to declare these subdivisions unconstitutional.

 

1569 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Badillo v. Katz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Bronx County


73 Misc. 2d 836; 343 N.Y.S.2d 451; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2023



April 16, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] That portion of the complaint which seeks an injunction against the Board of Elections from using the districts described in title ZB to prepare for the primary and general election of 1973 is denied and dismissed and the complaints in all other respects dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff politicians sought a declaration that Local Laws, 1973, No. 4 of City of New York City, N.Y. Title ZB, entitled "Councilmanic Districts," which established a plan for 33 new councilmanic districts to be voted upon in the primary and the general election was invalid. The politicians further requested that the court enjoin defendant Board of Elections from using the districts in the election and to promulgate a new plan.


OVERVIEW: The New York State Legislature enacted a law requiring the New York City Council to establish a plan for 33 new councilmanic districts. The New York City Council implemented Local Laws, 1973, No. 4 of City of New York City, N.Y. Title ZB, which divided the city into 33 councilmanic districts. One politician sought a declaration that the plan was invalid because some of the districts created were not contiguous. The court agreed that a couple of the districts were not contiguous. However, the court determined that the use of the newly drawn districts would be proper for that particular election because there was insufficient time to adequately prepare a new plan of reapportionment for that year's municipal election. The court held that the other politicians challenge to the newly drawn districts, namely that the new districts invidiously gerrymandered the Republican Party and deleted Republican voting strength, was not a proper challenge to the newly drawn districts.


OUTCOME: The court directed judgment in favor of the politicians in that the court declared Local Laws, 1973, No. 4 of City of New York City, N.Y. Title ZB was invalid. However, the court held that Title ZB, which redrew councilmanic districts, would be used in the upcoming municipal election, and dismissed that portion of the complaint that sought an injunction against the Board of Elections from using the newly drawn districts.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- apportionment of city council -- local law of City of New York which established plan for 33 councilmanic districts is unconstitutional but election of council for 1973 thereunder will be allowed as temporary expedient -- by State law, City Council was directed to establish plan for 33 districts, to be convenient and contiguous; such districts complied with requirement of equal number of voters -- Second Councilmanic District consisting of portions of Manhattan and Richmond is divided by another district and is not contiguous -- Eighth Councilmanic District consisting of portions of Manhattan, Queens and Bronx is also not contiguous -- Thirty-third Councilmanic District consisting of portions of Brooklyn and Richmond is contiguous and does not violate State statute -- lack of neatness in shape of some districts does not make them noncompact -- challenge to districts as to lack of racial and  [***2] ethnic representation is without merit -- saving clause of local law does not prevent holding of unconstitutionality of whole.

1. A local law of the City of New York which established a plan for 33 new councilmanic districts in said city to be voted at the primary election of June 4, 1973 and the general election in November is invalid in that the Second and Eighth Councilmanic Districts fail to conform to the statutory requirements of contiguity, convenience and compactness. Because of time limitations which would disrupt the electoral process and disenfranchise the voters, the plan is adopted as a temporary measure for the primary and general election of 1973 and is to remain in effect until the next councilmanic election by which time a new reapportionment plan shall be adopted.

2. By chapter 1206 of the Laws of 1971 the New York City Council was directed to establish a plan for 33 new councilmanic districts by a local law which was to describe the boundaries so as to contain as nearly equal a number of inhabitants as practicable and be "of convenient and contiguous territory and in as compact a form as practicable". The apportionment resulting, insofar as population and [***3] equal representation was involved, was perfect for the maximum variance between any of the districts is less than 1/10th of 1%.

3. The Second Councilmanic District consists of portions of Manhattan and Richmond. The southern tip of Manhattan, an island, which is closest to Richmond, another island, is not located in the Second District. It is impossible to travel from the Manhattan portion of the district to the Richmond portion without crossing either the first and third districts or New Jersey and the first district. The Second Councilmanic District does not comply with the requirement of "convenient and contiguous territory in as compact form as practicable".

4. The Eighth Councilmanic District is located in three boroughs, Manhattan, Queens and the Bronx. Those portions located in Manhattan and the Bronx are contiguous. That portion which is in Queens does not adjoin, touch or connect with the other portions. A reading of the word descriptions of the district shows that the Eighth Councilmanic District is bisected by the Seventh Councilmanic District and is not contiguous.

5. The Thirty-third Councilmanic District is composed of sections of the eastern portion of Richmond [***4] and a part of southwest Brooklyn. Since the eastern boundary of the Borough of Richmond is defined as the Borough of Brooklyn, these two areas are contiguous and as compact as possible and the district does not violate the statute.

6. While some challenged districts are irregular in form, lack of neatness does not render them noncompact, inconvenient or noncontiguous.

7. The challenge to the districts as to lack of racial and ethnic representation is without proof or merit.

8. Even though the local law contains a saving clause that if one section is held unconstitutional, it shall not affect the others, since each of the districts is dependent upon the other, if one is invalid, the whole is invalid. The local law is unconstitutional.

 

1570 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Newton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


73 Misc. 2d 854; 342 N.Y.S.2d 670; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2024



April 16, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons stated, I hold section 165.25 of the Penal Law is, on its face, constitutional, and accordingly the motion to dismiss the information is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the information filed against them for "jostling" in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 165.25. Defendants challenged the constitutionality of the legislation.


OVERVIEW: The information filed against defendants charged that they were observed inside a bus terminal, placing their hands in the proximity of the pockets of two intended victims and did attempt to remove property therefrom. Defendants were arrested for violation of § 165.25. Dismissing defendants' motion to dismiss the information, the court held that it was clearly the legislative intent in enacting this statute to cover the type of pick pocketing conduct which allegedly occurred in this case. However, defendants contended the term "unnecessarily" as used in the statute was susceptible of so many meanings that the statute was unconstitutionally vague. The court held that the doctrine of "vagueness" did not mean that the fact that the Penal Law had not specifically defined a key word in the statute, it became constitutionally defective. A lack of precision in statutory language did not automatically mean that the requirements of due process had been offended. The court noted that citizens who desired to obey this statute would not have difficulty in understanding it. There was no evidence presented that the statute encouraged arbitrary and erratic arrests and convictions.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed defendants' motion to dismiss the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- jostling -- in bus terminal, patrolman saw defendants place their hands in pockets of sleeping persons; defendants' motion to dismiss information accusing them of "intentionally and unnecessarily" jostling (Penal Law, § 165.25, subd. 1) denied -- statute is not unconstitutionally vague.

The Penal Law ( § 165.25) provides that "A person is guilty of jostling when, in a public place, he intentionally and unnecessarily: 1. Places his hand in the proximity of a person's pocket or handbag". This wording of a class A misdemeanor is not unconstitutionally vague. A patrolman in a bus terminal observed the defendants place their hands in the pockets of two named persons who were sleeping on benches. Defendants' motion to dismiss the information is denied.

 

1571 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Welfare v. Jones


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


73 Misc. 2d 1014; 343 N.Y.S.2d 661; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2046



April 11, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Commissioner of Welfare of the City of New York (commissioner), sought to declare respondent, the putative father, to be the father of a male child who, along with his mother, was receiving public assistance. The mother's previous paternity suit against the putative father had been dismissed as untimely under the two-year statute of limitations of Fam. Ct. Act § 517(a), which applied to private litigants.


OVERVIEW: The putative father contended that he was denied equal protection under the Fourteenth Amendment of the Constitution of the United States because, under Fam. Ct. Act § 517(b), the commissioner could bring a paternity suit until 10 years after the birth of the child if the child was receiving public assistance, although the natural mother was limited to two years under Fam. Ct. Act § 517(a). The court disagreed and found that the commission's claim was not time-barred. The legislature could have excluded the commission from any limitations period, but instead it imposed the 10-year period to balance the protection of public funds with the protection of defendants from stale claims. A welfare mother was not compelled to name the father of her child, and she often was an adverse party because of her desire to protect the father and receive public assistance. In relieving the public from the support of those liable to become public charges, the legislature could give the commission a greater period of time to bring suit. The commissioner's status as a public official in charge of public money placed him in a different position from a private litigant suing in an individual capacity.


OUTCOME: The court denied the putative father's assertion that the commission's paternity action against him was time-barred.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children born out of wedlock -- Statutes of Limitation -- after mother's paternity petition was dismissed because begun more than 2 years after birth, Commissioner of Social Services properly presented paternity petition alleging that mother and child are public charges; 10-year Statute of Limitations for Commissioner is constitutional -- no defense that, after latter petition was served, mother and child ceased to be public charges.

1. A paternity proceeding instituted by the mother of the child was dismissed because begun more than 2 years after the birth (Family Ct. Act, § 517, subd. [a]. Nevertheless since 10 years have not elapsed, the Commissioner of Social Services may institute a paternity proceeding, alleging that the mother and child are public charges (Family Ct. Act, § 517, subd. [b]. The difference between these Statutes of Limitation is not unconstitutional.

2. The fact that, after the Commissioner instituted this proceeding, the mother and child ceased to be public charges, is no defense.

 

1572 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kiernan v. Bronstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


73 Misc. 2d 629; 342 N.Y.S.2d 977; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2061; 83 L.R.R.M. 3095



April 5, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, about 17,000 city police officers, filed a special proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for a determination that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210 was unconstitutional on its face or as applied to them. They sought to annul the determination of respondent, a city director of personnel, that they had violated § 210 and that fines and forfeited tenure be imposed. Intervenor, a state attorney general appeared in opposition to the petition.


OVERVIEW: The officers were fined and placed on probation for having engaged in an illegal strike, described as a "work stoppage," during a five-day period. The officers filed their petition against the director of personnel, and other respondents, a city and its mayor, comptroller, and police commissioner. A state attorney general appeared in opposition to the petition. In response to the director's determinations, over 18,000 affidavits of objection were filed. The director designated independent attorneys who examined them, reviewed the original determinations and proofs submitted in opposition, and made recommendations as to a necessity for a hearing. The court held that: (1) the mayor's delegation of administrative functions, powers and duties to the director was proper because § 210 determinations were administrative determinations; (2) there was implicit power in the director to do whatever was administratively necessary because the presumption was that the director properly discharged his duties; and (3) the officers were afforded a right to a meaningful opportunity to be heard within the limits of practicality and approved procedures for objection and review by special proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court entered a declaration that the statute was constitutional on its face and as applied to the officers, and that the statute was validly enforced.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil service -- strikes -- police section 210 of Civil Service Law is valid and constitutional and was validly enforced against New York City policemen who engaged in work stoppage -- Mayor properly delegated his duties to Special Assistant, and latter did not improperly subdelegate his responsibilities -- since there were thousands of cases, five-month period between strike and administrative determinations was not unreasonable.

1. The Civil Service Law ( § 210, subd. 1) validly and constitutionally prohibits strikes by public employees; and it provides that "an employee who is absent from work without permission, or who abstains wholly or in part from the full performance of his duties in his normal manner without permission, on the date or dates when a strike occurs, shall be presumed to have engaged in such strike on such date or dates" (§ 210, subd. 2, par. [b]); and that "the chief executive officer of the government involved shall, on the basis of such investigation and affidavits as he may deem appropriate, determine whether or not such violation has occurred and * * * the names of employees who committed such violation and the date or dates thereof" (§ 210, subd.  [***2] 2, par. [d]); and that "The chief executive officer shall forthwith notify each employee [by personal service or by certified mail] that he has been found to have committed such violation, the date or dates thereof and of his right to object to such determination" (§ 210, subd. 2, par. [e]); and that such employee "shall be on probation for a term of one year following such determination during which period he shall serve without tenure" (§ 210, subd. 2, par. [f]); and that "Not earlier than thirty nor later than ninety days following the date of such determination, the chief fiscal officer of the government involved shall deduct from the compensation of each such public employee an amount equal to twice his daily rate of pay for each day * * * that he had violated this subdivision" (§ 210, subd. 2, par. [g]); and that an employee may object to such determination, whereupon the chief executive officer may appoint a hearing officer to determine the facts as to such employee; and that these determinations shall be reviewable pursuant to article 78 of the CPLR (Civil Service Law, § 210, subd. 2, par. [h]).

2. The foregoing provisions are valid and constitutional, and [***3] they were validly and constitutionally enforced, in conformity with due process, against policemen of the City of New York who went on a strike or work stoppage for one or more days during a six-day period, from January 14 to January 19, 1971.

3. The presumption (Civil Service Law, § 210, subd. 2, par. [b]) is easily rebuttable by showing a death in the family, or that the employee was donating blood, or some other acceptable excuse.

4. The Mayor of the City of New York (its "chief executive officer") properly delegated his duties under section 210 of the Civil Service Law to the Personnel Director of the City of New York, whom he designated as his Special Assistant (Mayor's Executive Order No. 32 dated January 21, 1971; New York City Charter, § 3).

5. Said Special Assistant properly designated independent attorneys to examine over 18,000 affidavits of objection and to recommend to him, as to each employee, whether a hearing should be held. It has not been shown that he subdelegated his responsibilities.

6. Said Special Assistant acted reasonably promptly on some 25,000 reports of duties performed by policemen during the period January 14 to 19, 1971. He gave probation and [***4] fine notices on or about June 18, 1971, and payroll deductions began 30 days thereafter, and he commenced issuing determinations within two months after objections were filed (Civil Service Law, § 210, subd. 2, pars. [d], [f], [g], [h]). Under the circumstances, there was no unreasonable delay in the due process of the law.

 

1573 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Caggiano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


78 Misc. 2d 187; 342 N.Y.S.2d 203; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1243



April 3, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, upon renewal, the order herein dated August 7, 1972 is vacated, the petition is granted, with costs and disbursements of this proceeding, and respondents are directed to return to petitioner his sum of $ 660, without interest.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner accused wrongdoer moved to renew his prior application pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, to annul a decision of respondents, county police department and police commissioner, that refused to return to him certain funds that were seized from him during his arrest. Another justice of the court had previously dismissed the petition as untimely.


OVERVIEW: During the arrest of the accused wrongdoer, county police department officers seized certain funds from his person. The criminal proceedings against the accused wrongdoer were later dismissed, and he petitioned respondents for the return of those funds. Respondents refused to return the funds, so the accused wrongdoer petitioned the court to obtain the return of the funds. A justice of the court denied the petition, and the accused wrongdoer moved to renew his application before the whole court. The court determined that petition should not have been dismissed as untimely, because the applicable limitations period for a mandamus action had not expired. The court thus granted the motion to renew. Further, it determined that the affirmative defenses asserted by respondents lacked merit, and that justice and fundamental fairness required the return of the funds to the accused wrongdoer. The accused wrongdoer's petition to secure the return of the funds therefore was granted, and respondents were ordered to return the funds without interest.


OUTCOME: The accused wrongdoer's motion to renew his petition for the return of the funds seized during his arrest was granted. Further, the court's previous order that denied the petition was vacated, and the petition granted. Respondents were therefore directed to return to the subject funds without interest to him.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against body or officer -- limitations of time -- when remedy appropriate -- article 78 proceeding challenging validity of refusal to return, on demand, money seized from petitioner when he was arrested on numbers running charge, subsequently dismissed, was timely commenced when brought within four months of refusal to return money, although more than four months from date of dismissal of criminal proceeding -- petitioner not barred by laches from making motion to renew, where prior order which had dismissed petition as untimely was not served on him, and no prejudice has been shown -- article 78 remedy is appropriate -- service of notice of claim is not required before bringing article 78 proceeding -- money belongs to petitioner and there is no claim of public right to it -- moreover, it should be returned as matter of fundamental fairness and justice.

1. On July 6, 1971 the police arrested petitioner on a numbers running [***2] charge and seized $ 660 from him. The criminal proceeding was dismissed on November 23, 1971, his attorney demanded return of the money on January 10, 1972, and, on April 18, 1972, after the demand was refused, he commenced an article 78 proceeding. By court order dated August 7, 1972 his petition was dismissed as untimely. On a motion to renew, that order is vacated and his petition is granted.

2. The four-month period of limitation applicable to the proceeding did not begin to run on the date the criminal proceeding was dismissed but on the date of the municipal act which is challenged as invalid, i.e., the refusal to return the money on demand.

3. Petitioner is not barred by laches. He was not served with the prior order of dismissal and notice of entry thereof, and no prejudice to any party has been shown.

4. The fact that he may have alternate remedies of conversion and replevin does not preclude him from bringing an article 78 proceeding as a citizen aggrieved by the determination of a county agency. The article 78 remedy is particularly apt where, as here, there are no substantial factual issues which might render summary disposition inappropriate. And service of a [***3] notice of claim under the General Municipal Law is not required before bringing an article 78 proceeding.

5. The money belongs to petitioner, and there is no claim that the county has a continuing right to hold it. Moreover, it should be returned as a matter of fundamental fairness and justice. Cash taken incidental to a numbers running charge does not appear to be contraband, as opposed to the numbers stubs themselves. In any event, any arguable public right to it dissipated when the criminal charge was dismissed.

 

1574 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Yedvobnick v. Grupposo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


73 Misc. 2d 687; 343 N.Y.S.2d 293; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2073



April 2, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion for summary judgment is accordingly denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff claimant filed a complaint against defendant police property clerk to recover funds seized in connection with the claimant's arrest on a gambling charge, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 225.15. The clerk filed a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The claimant was arrested on possession of gambling records, policy slips, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 225.15. He pled guilty and paid a fine. He filed a complaint against the clerk to recover the funds seized in connection with his arrest. The clerk filed a motion for summary judgment. The court denied the motion. Under the provisions of N.Y. City Admin. Code § 435-4.0, it was a plaintiff's burden to prove that the property in question was not contraband subject to forfeiture. Although § 435-4.0 was declared unconstitutional precisely because it placed this untoward burden of proof upon the claimant from whom moneys had been seized, nothing precluded the city from seizing or confiscating contraband. The claimant could therefore be able to make out a prima facie case by merely showing the seizure of the money alleged to be his, the final disposition of the criminal charge, and the demand and refusal of the clerk to the return of the money. However, the city on its part could be in a position to prove the money in question was indeed contraband, did not belong to the claimant, and was subject to forfeiture. That issue could not be determined without a trial.


OUTCOME: The court denied the clerk's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Searches and seizures -- return of seized property -- motion for summary judgment for recovery of money from City of New York seized in connection with plaintiff's arrest is denied; although statute which placed burden upon claimant to establish lawful title to property seized was declared unconstitutional, city may nonetheless seize or confiscate contraband; because city may be in position to prove that money seized was contraband, issue cannot be determined without trial.

A motion for summary judgment for the recovery of $ 3,000 from the Police Property Clerk of the City of New York seized in connection with plaintiff's arrest on a gambling charge is denied. Although section 435-4.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which placed the burden upon a claimant to establish that he had a lawful title or property right in any property seized, was declared unconstitutional ( McClendon v. Rosetti, 460 F. 2d 111), nothing precludes the city from seizing or confiscating contraband. Because the city may be in a position to prove that the money in question is contraband, the issue cannot be determined [***2] without a trial.

 

1575 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lopez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


73 Misc. 2d 878; 342 N.Y.S.2d 984; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2083



March 30, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an indictment brought by the People of the State of New York and voted by the Grand Jury of Kings County, which charged him with two counts each of sale of a dangerous drug in the third degree, possession of a dangerous drug in the fourth degree, and possession of a dangerous drug in the sixth degree. Defendant's arrest warrant had not been executed until nearly 32 months after the indictment had been voted.


OVERVIEW: The People had not presented any evidence to explain the long delay in arresting defendant. All the People stated was that the delay had been through inadvertence or negligence due to overburdened and understaffed warrant squads. The People denied any attempt to delay the trial to hinder defendant's case. The court noted that defendant had a right to a speedy trial, which was guaranteed not only by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 30.20, but also by the Sixth Amendment to the United States Constitution. However, the court noted that it was unclear whether the Sixth Amendment speedy trial right extended to a case, such as this, where defendant not only was not arrested promptly, but had not even been informed that there had been an indictment voted against him until almost 32 months later. The court found that such a long period of delay could have prejudiced defendant's case due to loss of physical evidence and the fading of witnesses' memories of the incident. Therefore, the court ruled that a hearing was needed in order to determine in what way, if any, defendant's case had been prejudiced and to give the People the chance to show that the delay had been reasonable under the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that a determination on defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment brought against him by the People would be held in abeyance until a hearing could be held at which defendant could present evidence of any prejudice to him from the delay in arresting him and the People could present evidence that it had acted diligently to execute the arrest warrant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- right to speedy trial -- delay of 31 months in arresting indicted defendant, who asserts he was always available, requires hearing to determine whether his ability to prepare his defense has been unconstitutionally impaired by delay.

On December 23, 1969 in Kings County, defendant was indicted and an arrest warrant was issued for sale of a dangerous drug and possession of a dangerous drug; but somehow defendant was not arrested until almost 31 months later, on July 18, 1972, although defendant asserts that he lived and worked in Kings County at all times. The court will conduct a hearing to determine whether the People's delay is excusable and whether the defendant's ability to prepare his defense has been unconstitutionally impaired by the delay.

In re Apple


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


73 Misc. 2d 553; 342 N.Y.S.2d 352; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2098



March 26, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a motion for reimbursement by the State of New York for contribution to the education of a handicapped child under N.Y. Educ. Law § 4403 as part of an order imposing liability on a city for the education of a handicapped child.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner of Education argued that the motion to order the State to contribute to the education of a handicapped child under § 4403 was premature and that the family court could not impose liability for the cost of an order upon the State. The court disagreed. In making their orders for the education of a handicapped child, most family courts had been charging the State with reimbursement as a matter of course. The city had not been required to institute an independent proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to receive 50 percent of its payment from the State. Under N.Y. Educ. Law § 4403, subd. 1, when the family court issued an order for the education of any handicapped child, the Commissioner should issue a certificate to that effect. The counsel to the Commissioner stated that the only reason for not approving the child was that she was a public charge. The court held that failing to provide payment for the child for this reason was invidious discrimination. Many family courts did not require a parent to contribute to the cost of educating a handicapped child.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for the reimbursement by the State as part of an order imposing liability on the city for the education of a handicapped child.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- handicapped children -- Family Court orders city to pay $ 2,000 to school for physically handicapped children, and orders State to repay half to city (Family Ct. Act, § 232; Education Law, § 4403) -- immaterial that child is recipient of public assistance.

As to a handicapped child who is a recipient of public assistance, the Family Court directs the City of New York to pay $ 2,000 to a special school for the education of handicapped children and directs the State of New York to reimburse the city to the extent of half thereof, or $ 1,000 (Family Ct. Act, § 232; Education Law, § 4403; cf. Social Services Law, § 398, subd. 4, par. [a]). No proceeding under article 78 of the CPLR is necessary.

People v. McClafferty


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Queens County


73 Misc. 2d 666; 342 N.Y.S.2d 208; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2114



March 21, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied and the defendants will proceed to trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, who were charged with misdemeanors by a prosecutor's information after the case was presented to a grand jury, moved for a preliminary hearing pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 170.75 to determine if there was reasonable cause to believe that they had committed the misdemeanors.


OVERVIEW: A grand jury directed the district attorney to file a prosecutor's information charging defendants with the misdemeanors of criminal mischief and assault. Defendants moved for a preliminary hearing pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 170.75, which allowed a defendant who had been arraigned upon a prosecutor's information to request a hearing to determine if there was reasonable cause to believe that the defendant had committed the misdemeanor. The court denied the motion, holding that the charges against defendants had already been examined by a grand jury. The court found that a grand jury could not direct the filing of a prosecutor's information unless the evidence was both legally sufficient to establish the commission of a crime and gave rise to reasonable cause to believe that the defendant committed the crime. The court found that in the grand jury hearing, defendants had received a more rigorous examination of the case than they would receive in the hearing authorized by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 170.75.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion for a preliminary hearing to determine if there was reasonable cause to believe that defendants had committed the misdemeanors. The court found that a grand jury had already examined the charges against defendants and had considered both the legal sufficiency of the evidence and whether there was reasonable cause. The court ordered defendants to proceed to trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- preliminary hearing -- when Grand Jury directs District Attorney to file misdemeanor information in New York City Criminal Court, defendant is not entitled to preliminary hearing ( CPL 170.75, 190.70).

When a Grand Jury, instead of indicting a defendant for felony, directs the District Attorney to file a prosecutor's information in the New York City Criminal Court, charging a misdemeanor -- in this case, two Class A misdemeanors -- the defendant is not entitled to a preliminary hearing ( CPL 170.75; 100.10, subd. 3; 190.70; 190.75; 190.50; 70.10).

 

1578 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


McCoy v. Mayor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


73 Misc. 2d 508; 342 N.Y.S.2d 83; 1973 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2117



March 19, 1973 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, state administrator, filed an action, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, with the court seeking a judgment directing respondents, the mayor and related parties, to take whatever action was necessary to implement additional appropriations for the Housing Part of the Civil Court of the City of New York. The mayor moved to dismiss the action for failure to state a cause of action, but the court denied the motion.


OVERVIEW: The administrator sought to compel the mayor and related parties to provide additional appropriations for the operation of the Housing Part of the Civil Court of the City of New York. Essentially, the communications between the executive and the judicial branches of the city government deteriorated to such an extent that the lawsuit became necessary in order to determine how and when the Housing Part was to be funded. Upon review, the court held that the administrator had standing to bring the proceeding. The court then ruled that the duty to fund could not be avoided or subverted because budgetary modifications or future appropriations entailed some degree of discretion. The court stated that discretion could not be abused or converted into a subterfuge to exercise a de fact veto over legislative enactment. The court found that, despite the existence of certain established budgetary procedures, budget appropriations for the court for all practical purposes were lump sum allocations. Thus, the court ordered that the mayor and the related parties were required to provide the funding, as the right of the public to a swift adjudication of housing complaints prevailed over obstinacy.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the mayor and related parties to proceed to take necessary action to make available the funds that were required to properly staff and operate the Housing Part of the Civil Court of the City of New York.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- funds for operation -- State Administrator of Judicial Conference and Administrative Judge of Civil Court of City of New York have standing to bring proceeding for judgment directing Mayor of City of New York and others to provide funds for newly created Housing Part of Civil Court -- while courts, by 1962 amendment of Constitution, lost their budgetary powers and were required to submit their monetary needs to review by city, city has no discretion to cut off funds -- it is no defense that budget estimates were not properly submitted -- petitioners are granted stated relief.

1. The State Administrator of the Judicial Conference and the Administrative Judge of the Civil Court of the City of New York have standing to bring a proceeding for judgment directing the Mayor of the City of New York and others to take action to fund the newly created Housing Part of the Civil Court of the City of New York. Petitioners are aggrieved by the failure to do so.

2. In 1962, the Constitution was amended so that the courts lost their mandatory budgetary powers and thereafter were required to submit their monetary needs to a review by the city prior to appropriation. However the [***2] city was not relieved of its obligation to furnish the funds required by the courts. The city has no power to cut off all funds for the courts. Its discretion may not be abused, and the limits of its discretion are constitutionally proscribed.

3. The Civil Court has power, within the limits of the law creating the Housing Part, to designate hearing officers and fix their number, function and salary. It is no bar to relief that normal budgetary procedures were not complied with. If petitioners did not follow the ordinary procedures to obtain a modification of the city's 1972-1973 budget to procure the funds for the period from April 1, 1973 through June 30, 1973, respondents nevertheless do not deny that they were on written notice beginning in November, 1972, that city funds were needed for the new Housing Part of the Civil Court. The defense that budget estimates were not properly submitted is dismissed.

4. Petitioners are granted stated relief.
